The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Exhaustiveness of the First Figure's Four Moods
Author calculates moods and refutes a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute an attempted fifth mood in the first figure by identifying the violation of the condition that the minor must be affirmative.

Core Idea
The four moods of the first figure are exhaustive; any apparent exception violates a condition or reduces to another figure.

The first figure is the most perfect syllogistic form, with only four valid moods. However, a student might think that a universal negative minor with a universal affirmative major yields a valid conclusion. In this lesson, you will learn how the author calculates the possible moods and why such an attempt fails. You will then be able to distinguish genuine first-figure syllogisms from those that only seem to work.

Guided Path
1
Understand the calculation of faces per mode
The author multiplies the possible faces (quantity and quality combinations) for each premise, considering the conditions of the first figure. For the first mode, he gets 128 faces by multiplying the four possibilities of the major by the eight remaining (after negation/affirmation) for the minor.
This calculation is a theoretical exercise to show the huge number of possible premise combinations, but only four yield valid syllogisms.
2
Identify the four recognized moods of the first figure
The four moods are: Barbara (AAA), Celarent (EAE), Darii (AII), and Ferio (EIO). All have an affirmative minor premise.
Memorize these four moods; they are the only valid ones in the first figure.
3
Analyze the attempted counterexample
Consider 'No J is B; every B is A; therefore not every J is A.' This seems to produce a novel conclusion. The terms are: minor term J, middle term B, major term A. The minor premise is negative (universal negative), violating the condition that the minor must be affirmative.
The conclusion 'not every J is A' is a particular negative, which is already covered by Ferio if the premises were appropriate.
4
Apply the author's refutation
The author gives two refutations: (1) The minor is negative, violating a condition. (2) If you try to salvage it by conversion, it reduces to the second figure, not a direct first-figure syllogism. Therefore, it is not a valid first-figure mood.
A valid syllogism must produce the conclusion 'from itself' (لذاته) without extra steps or figure changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكبرى أو بالعكس، فتكون الوجوه الواقعة في كل ضرب من الضروب أربعة بهذا الاعتبار. فلو ضربت الأربعة الواقعة في الضرب الأول بحسب العموم والخصوص في الثمانية الباقية التي هي بحسب العدول والتحصيل فيه؛ والمبلغ في هذه الأربعة كذلك بلغت الوجوه الواقعة في الضرب الأول مبلغ مائة وثمانية وعشرين؛ وفي الضرب الثاني بلغت مبلغ النصف من هذا؛ إذ فيه اثنان بحسب العموم والخصوص؛ وفي الثالث مبلغ الضعف؛ إذ فيه ثمانية بحسب العموم والخصوص؛ وذلك مائتان وستة وخمسون؛ وفي الرابع مبلغ النصف من هذا وهو مثل ما في الأول. ثم إذا ضربت هذه الوجوه بأجمعها في جميع أقسام الأقيسة البسيطة والمختلطة بلغت مبلغاً كثيراً أضعاف هذه المبالغ لا محالة. ولا يبعد أن يخطر ببال أحد أن المنتج من هذا الشكل لا يكون منحصرا في هذه الضروب الأربعة؛ إذ السالبة الكلية إذا كانت صغرى الموجبة الكلية أو الجزئية كما يقال لا شيء من (ج) ب)، وكل ب أ ينتج ليس كل «أج»، فإن الكبرى إذا عكست ينتج من الشكل الثاني ليس كل «أج» . وذلك لأنها تنعكس جزئية، والجزئية لا تصلح للكبروية في ذلك الشكل فيكون صغراه هكذا بعض «أب»، ولا شيء من «ج» «ب» فينتج ليس بعض أج» . وكذلك إذا كانت السالبة الكلية صغرى الموجبة الجزئية. ثم إنه وما هو بمثله أيضاً فذلك من الوهميات؛ ورفعها بالنظر في قولنا إن من الشرائط أن تكون الصغرى موجبة في الشكل الأول؛ إذ الصغرى فيه ما فيه الأصغر الذي يجب أن يكون موضوع النتيجة وهو «ج»، فإذا جعلناه موضوع النتيجة وحملنا عليه «أ» لم يلزم منه البتة ليس بعض «ج أ». فإذن إن كان ينتج ما ذكر تم فليس ذلك من الصغرى والكبرى على ما وضع وكيف وإنه راجع إلى الشكل الثاني بعكس الكبرى وجعلها صغرى والشكل الثاني إنما تظهر قياسيته بالانعكاس، ورده إلى الشكل الأول فيتضاعف العمل إلى أن يلتحق بالشكل الرابع الذي هو بعيد عن الطبع؛ وفيه ما فيه من التكلف في بيان قياسيته. وبالجملة فذلك يندفع بقولنا لزم عنه لذاته في تعريف القياس فإنه لا يلزم لذاته وإن لزم عنه شيء، وإذا لم يلزم لذاته فلا يعتبر.
The major (premise) or vice versa, so the faces occurring in each mode are four by this consideration. If you multiply the four occurring in the first mode according to universality and particularity by the remaining eight which are according to negation and affirmation, and the sum in these four is likewise, the faces occurring in the first mode amount to one hundred and twenty-eight; in the second mode it amounts to half of this, since there are two according to universality and particularity; in the third mode it amounts to double, since there are eight according to universality and particularity, and that is two hundred and fifty-six; in the fourth mode it amounts to half of this, and it is like the first. Then if you multiply all these faces by all the divisions of simple and compound syllogisms, they will amount to a great number, many times these sums, without doubt. It is not unlikely that someone might think that what is produced in this figure is not restricted to these four moods; for if a universal negative is the minor with a universal affirmative or particular affirmative, as when one says 'Nothing of J is B, and every B is A', it produces 'Not every J is A'; for if the major is converted, it produces from the second figure 'Not every J is A'. That is because it converts as particular, and a particular is not suitable as major in that figure, so its minor would be 'Some A is B' and 'Nothing of J is B', so it produces 'Some J is not A'. Similarly if the universal negative is the minor with a particular affirmative. Then this and what is like it is among the illusions; its refutation is by considering our statement that among the conditions is that the minor be affirmative in the first figure; for the minor in it is that which contains the smaller term which must be the subject of the conclusion, which is J, so if we make it the subject of the conclusion and predicate A upon it, it does not necessarily follow at all that 'Not some J is A'. Therefore, if it does produce what is mentioned, then it is not from the minor and major as set forth, and how? And it reduces to the second figure by converting the major and making it the minor, and the second figure only shows its syllogistic form through conversion, and its reduction to the first figure doubles the work until it attaches to the fourth figure, which is far from nature, and there is in it what there is of effort in demonstrating its syllogistic form. In summary, that is repelled by our statement that it follows from itself in the definition of syllogism, for it does not follow from itself, even if something follows from it, and if it does not follow from itself, it is not considered.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the moods of the first figure and a refutation of a potential counterexample that seems to yield a valid mood beyond the four recognized ones. The author begins by calculating the number of 'faces' (وجوه) in each 'mode' (ضرب) of the first figure. He multiplies the four possibilities (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) for the major premise by the four for the minor, but adjusts for the conditions of the first figure. For the first ضرب (where the major is universal and the minor is affirmative), he gets 128 by multiplying the four possibilities of the major by the eight remaining after considering negation and affirmation. Similarly for the other ضروب, he gives numbers. Then he says that if you multiply all these by the types of simple and compound syllogisms, the number becomes huge. Next, he raises an objection: someone might think that a syllogism with a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major (e.g., 'No J is B; every B is A') is valid and produces 'Not every J is A', which is not one of the four moods. He refutes this by first noting that converting the major premise ('Every B is A' becomes 'Some A is B') and then using it as a minor in the second figure leads to the conclusion, but this is not a direct first-figure syllogism. He then gives the real refutation: one of the conditions of the first figure is that the minor premise must be affirmative. In the attempted example, the minor is negative, so it fails the condition. If you try to force it, it does not produce the desired conclusion from the given terms as set. He also mentions that such a syllogism reduces to the second figure and then to the first, but with much effort, and ultimately it does not produce the conclusion 'from itself' (لذاته) according to the definition of syllogism, so it is not considered a valid mood of the first figure. The key point is that the four moods of the first figure are exhaustive because any valid syllogism with the middle term as subject of the major and predicate of the minor must have an affirmative minor; otherwise it either fails or reduces to another figure.

Key Terms
ضرب
الوجوه
الأصغر

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the syllogism 'No J is B; every B is A; therefore not every J is A' not a valid mood of the first figure?
A) Because the minor premise is negative, violating the condition that the minor must be affirmative in the first figure.
B) Because the major premise is not universal.
C) Because the conclusion is particular, which is not allowed.
D) Because the middle term is not distributed.

Key Takeaway
The four moods of the first figure are exhaustive; any attempted addition that violates the condition of an affirmative minor is either invalid or reduces to another figure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid first-figure syllogism in Darii (AII) on a fiqh topic: e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram; some nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore some nabidh is haram.' Check that the minor is affirmative.
?
Identify whether the following is a valid first-figure mood: 'No human is immortal; every prophet is human; therefore no prophet is immortal.' Explain why or why not.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Refuting Misinterpretations of Universal Affirmative Premises
Author refutes objections about the meaning of predication in the first figure's moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the identity and predication interpretations of 'كل ج ب' using the author's arguments.

Core Idea
'Every J is B' means logical inclusion, not identity or mere attribute-attribution.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange. An objector questions what is meant by the universal affirmative premise in the first figure. The author refutes two plausible interpretations: that J is identical to B, and that J merely possesses the attribute B. Understanding why these fail is essential for grasping the nature of syllogistic validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's four possibilities
The objector lists four meanings for 'كل ج ب': identity, predication, coexistence in one essence, or something else. Focus on the first two, which the author refutes in detail.
2
Analyze the refutation of identity
The author gives three arguments: (1) 'Every human is an animal' shows difference because human is animal with a differentia; (2) self-attribution is meaningless for inference; (3) terms must differ in quiddity for the syllogism to work.
3
Analyze the refutation of predication
If 'كل ج ب' means 'J is described by B', then from 'J is B and B is A' it does not follow that J is A, because the middle term's essence differs from the minor's. Having a property of the middle does not transfer that property to the minor.
4
Understand the consequence
The correct interpretation must allow transitivity. The refuted interpretations would make the syllogism invalid, so they must be rejected in favor of inclusion in essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول هب أن الضروب المعتبرة هي هذه الأربعة لكن في كل واحد من هذه الأربعة من الشبهات ما فيه منها أن يقال في الضرب الأول مثلا إذا قلنا كل (ج) ب)، فالمراد منه هو أن الجيم بعينه هو الباء، أو المراد منه هو أنه موصوف بصفة الباء، أو أن معنى الجيم ومعنى الباء يجتمعان في ذات واحدة، أو معنى هو رابع هذه الثلاثة. أما الأول فباطل بوجوه منها إنا إذا قلنا كل إنسان حيوان مثلا علمنا بالضرورة أن حقيقة الإنسان غير حقيقة الحيوان وكيف لا وقد كان الإنسان هو الحيوان مع الغير، ومن المحال أن يكون الحيوان مع الغير هو بعينه الحيوان لا مع الغير. ومنها أن الغرض من هذه الأقيسة هو الاستدلال على اتصاف الموضوع بالمحمول، واتصاف الشيء بنفسه غير معقول. ومنها أن الاختلاف في الماهية بين الحدود من اللوازم، ولو كان الموضوع عين المحمول فيما ذكرناه فلا اختلاف بين الحد الأصغر والأوسط البتة. وأما الثاني فباطل أيضاً فإن معنى القياس حينئذ هو أن الأصغر موصوف بالأوسط، والأوسط موصوف بالأكبر؛ ولا يلزم منه أن يكون الأصغر موصوفاً بالأكبر فإن حقيقة الأوسط مخالفة لحقيقة الأصغر. فلا يلزم من اتصاف الأوسط بشيء اتصاف الأصغر بذلك الشيء. نعم يلزم منه أن يكون الأصغر موصوفاً بالموصوف بالأكبر لكن لا يجب أن يكون الموصوف بالموصوف بالشيء موصوفاً بذلك الشيء. ولأن الاتحاد في الحد
Then an objector may say: Granted that the considered moods are these four, but in each one of these four there are doubts, among them that in the first mood, for example, when we say 'Every J is B', what is meant is that J itself is exactly B, or what is meant is that it is described by the attribute of B, or that the meaning of J and the meaning of B come together in one essence, or a meaning that is a fourth to these three. As for the first, it is false by several arguments: from them, when we say 'Every human is an animal', for example, we know necessarily that the reality of human is other than the reality of animal, and how not? For human is animal with an addition, and it is impossible for animal with an addition to be exactly the animal without the addition. And from them, the purpose of these syllogisms is to infer the attribution of the subject to the predicate, and the self-attribution of a thing is unintelligible. And from them, the difference in quiddity between the terms is necessary, and if the subject were identical to the predicate in what we mentioned, there would be no difference between the minor and middle term at all. As for the second, it is also false, for the meaning of the syllogism then is that the minor is described by the middle, and the middle is described by the major; but it does not follow that the minor is described by the major, because the reality of the middle is different from the reality of the minor. So it does not follow from the middle being described by something that the minor is described by that thing. Yes, it follows that the minor is described by what is described by the major, but it is not necessary that what is described by what is described by a thing is described by that thing. And because the union in the term...
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes objections regarding the interpretation of the universal affirmative premise 'كل ج ب' in the first mood of the first figure. The objector suggests four possible meanings: (1) identity: J is exactly B; (2) predication: J is described by the attribute of B; (3) coexistence: the meanings of J and B coexist in one essence; or (4) some other meaning. The author refutes the identity interpretation with three arguments: first, when we say 'Every human is an animal', we know that human's reality is not identical to animal's reality because human is animal with a differentia; second, the purpose of syllogisms is to show that a subject has a predicate, and self-attribution (identical subject and predicate) is meaningless; third, there must be a difference in quiddity between the terms, otherwise the minor and middle would be identical. Then he refutes the predication interpretation: if 'كل ج ب' means that J has the attribute of B, then the syllogism becomes: J has attribute B, and B has attribute A, but it does not follow that J has attribute A, because the middle term (B) has a different reality from the minor (J). For example, if J is 'human' and B is 'animal', having the attribute 'animal' does not automatically give 'human' all attributes of 'animal'. The author notes that it does follow that J is described by what is described by A (i.e., J is described by the thing that is described by A), but that does not entail that J itself is described by A. This is a subtle distinction: possession of a property by the subject of a property does not transfer the property. The passage breaks off while discussing the third meaning (coexistence) and the fourth.

Key Terms
الموصوف
المحمول
الاتحاد في الحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the interpretation of 'كل ج ب' as predication (J is described by B) insufficient for the syllogism to be valid?
A) Because the minor term may not actually have the attribute of the middle.
B) Because the middle term's reality differs from the minor's, so the minor's having the middle's attribute does not transfer the major's attribute.
C) Because the major premise must be universal.
D) Because the conclusion would be false.

Key Takeaway
The universal affirmative premise in a syllogism must be understood as inclusion in essence, not identity or accidental predication, for the syllogism to be valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine an opponent argues that 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' means intoxicant and forbidden are identical. Using the author's three arguments, write a refutation in the context of Islamic law.
?
Now the opponent argues it merely means intoxicant is described by forbidden (predication). Explain why this would make the qiyas syllogism 'Nabidh is intoxicant, therefore nabidh is forbidden' invalid, referencing the author's reasoning about the middle term's essence.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Essential Implication and the Rejection of the Fourth Figure
Critique of fourth figure's unnaturalness and its failure to meet qiyas definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the fourth figure fails the condition of essential implication (luzum dhati) and thus is not considered a valid syllogism.

Core Idea
A syllogism must entail its conclusion essentially; the fourth figure requires artificial operations, so it fails.

This passage examines the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The author argues that it is unnatural and requires multiple conversions to be reduced to the first figure. More importantly, it fails the defining condition of a valid syllogism: essential implication. We will see how the definition of qiyas (syllogism) itself excludes the fourth figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about the fourth figure
The author asserts that the fourth figure is far from natural disposition and involves artificiality (takalluf) in explaining its syllogistic form.
2
Understand the reduction process
The fourth figure is said to revert to the second figure by converting the major premise and making it minor, and then the second figure requires further conversion to the first figure. This multiplies the work.
Conversion here refers to 'aks (simple conversion).
3
Grasp the definition of qiyas as essential implication
A valid qiyas (syllogism) is defined as an argument that entails its conclusion essentially (lizatihi). That is, the conclusion must follow from the premises alone without any external operation.
The term 'essential implication' (luzum dhati) is key.
4
Apply the definition to the fourth figure
The fourth figure's conclusion does not follow essentially from the premises; it only follows after operations like conversion. Therefore, it does not meet the definition.
5
Conclude why it is rejected
Since the fourth figure does not entail its conclusion essentially, it is not considered a valid syllogistic form, regardless of any logical validity after conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنه راجع إلى الشكل الثاني بعكس الكبرى وجعلها صغرى والشكل الثاني إنما تظهر قياسيته بالانعكاس، ورده إلى الشكل الأول فيتضاعف العمل إلى أن يلتحق بالشكل الرابع الذي هو بعيد عن الطبع؛ وفيه ما فيه من التكلف في بيان قياسيته. وبالجملة فذلك يندفع بقولنا لزم عنه لذاته في تعريف القياس فإنه لا يلزم لذاته وإن لزم عنه شيء، وإذا لم يلزم لذاته فلا يعتبر.
And it (the fourth figure) reverts to the second figure by converting the major premise and making it minor, and the second figure only shows its syllogistic form by conversion, and its reduction to the first figure multiplies the work until it joins the fourth figure, which is far from natural disposition; and it has what it has of artificiality in explaining its syllogistic form. In summary, that is refuted by our saying: essential implication in the definition of qiyas, for it (the fourth figure) does not imply essentially even if something is implied by it, and if it does not imply essentially then it is not considered.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage critiques the fourth figure of the syllogism. The author argues that the fourth figure is unnatural and requires artificial reduction to the first figure through multiple conversions, making it cumbersome. The central criticism is based on the definition of qiyas (syllogism): a valid syllogism must entail its conclusion essentially (lizatihi). The fourth figure fails this condition because its conclusion does not follow essentially from the premises alone; rather, it only follows after additional operations like conversion. Therefore, the fourth figure should not be considered a valid syllogistic form. This argument uses the standard definition of qiyas from the logical tradition, which emphasizes essential entailment.

Key Terms
القياس
اللزوم الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the fourth figure as a valid syllogism?
A) Because it has too many moods.
B) Because it does not entail its conclusion essentially.
C) Because it uses conversion, which is invalid.
D) Because it is not found in any classical text.

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure is not a valid syllogism because its conclusion does not follow essentially from its premises; it depends on additional operations like conversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Necessity of Unity in the Middle Term
Author refutes third and fourth objections about the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objection against syllogistic validity fails due to violating the unity of the middle term.

Core Idea
The middle term must be univocal; objections that deny this are false.

In categorical syllogisms, the middle term must be identical in both premises. The author here dismisses objections that attack this condition. Understanding this helps you spot flawed objections in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being addressed
Locate the objection in the passage. The author refers to 'the third' and 'the fourth' objections against syllogistic validity. Determine what each objection claims.
The first two objections are not in this passage; focus on what is given.
2
Extract the author's criterion for falsity
The author says the third objection is 'among what contradicts unity necessarily'. This indicates that any objection that denies or violates the necessary unity of the middle term is automatically false.
Unity (ittiḥād) means the middle term must have the same meaning in both premises.
3
Apply the criterion to the third objection
Since the third objection contradicts unity, it is dismissed as false. Explain why this is a decisive refutation if the unity condition is accepted.
This shows the dialectical power of a necessary condition.
4
Recognize the burden of proof for the fourth objection
The fourth objection is labeled 'unknown' by the author. The objector must clarify it until it becomes known. This shifts the burden to the objector.
In jadal, an unclear objection is not accepted until explained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنطق الكبير - الأوسط من اللوازم، وإنه مما ينافي الاتحاد بالضرورة. وأما الثالث فباطل كذلك لأنه من جملة ما ينافي الاتحاد أيضًا؛ إذ المحمول هو الاجتماع بينهما حينئذ. وأما الرابع فذلك غير معلوم؛ فعلى المعترض بيانه إلى أن يعلم بأنه ما هو وكيف هو.
The middle term of the necessary concomitants, and it is among what contradicts unity necessarily. As for the third, it is false likewise because it is among what contradicts unity as well; for then the predicate is the connection between them. As for the fourth, that is unknown; so upon the objector is its clarification until it is known what it is and how it is.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is the tail end of a discussion of objections (shubahat) about syllogistic reasoning. The author has already discussed the first two objections and now addresses the third and fourth. The third objection is dismissed as false because it contradicts the necessary unity of the middle term; the fourth is rejected as unknown and the burden of proof is placed on the objector. The passage emphasizes that the middle term must be univocal and identical across premises for a valid syllogism.

Key Terms
الأوسط
اللوازم
الاتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the third objection false?
A) It denies the middle term's unity.
B) It uses a disjunctive premise.
C) It assumes the predicate is the connection.
D) It is unknown.

Key Takeaway
The unity of the middle term is a non-negotiable condition for categorical syllogisms; objections that violate it are self-refuting, and unclear objections must be clarified by the objector.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following objection: 'The world is temporally originated but not every temporally originated thing has a cause because some are uncaused.' Identify whether this objection violates the unity of the middle term and explain.
?
In a debate about divine attributes, someone argues: 'God is eternal; the Qur'an is eternal; therefore the Qur'an is God.' What condition of the middle term is violated?

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Second Shubha: Predicate of the Minor Premise
Author presents and refutes an objection about the predicate of the minor premise being problematic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objector's two alternatives for the predicate of the minor premise and explain why both are claimed to break the identity of the middle term.

Core Idea
The predicate of the minor must be identical to the subject of the major for a valid syllogism.

In this lesson, we examine a sophism (shubha) that attacks the very structure of a categorical syllogism. The objector argues that the predicate of the minor premise—here 'animal'—cannot be properly identical to the subject of the major premise, thus invalidating the conclusion. We will parse the objector's reasoning and the author's response, learning how logicians defend the necessity of the syllogistic consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the attacked syllogism
The objector targets the standard syllogism: 'Every human is an animal; every animal is sentient; therefore every human is sentient.' Note the middle term is 'animal'.
The middle term must appear in both premises.
2
Extract the two possibilities for the predicate of the minor
The predicate 'animal' in the minor premise is either taken absolutely (as the genus itself) or restricted (by some condition). The objector argues both are problematic.
3
Analyze the first possibility (absolute)
If 'animal' is absolute, then it is a genus common to all animals, not identical to any particular animal. Thus the predicate of the minor is not the same as the subject of the major (which is 'animal' as a species or individual). Identity fails.
The objector notes that 'animal is a genus' is correct but 'every animal is a genus' is false, showing the term functions differently.
4
Analyze the second possibility (restricted)
If 'animal' is restricted, its exact meaning is unknown because we cannot specify the restriction. Therefore we cannot know if it is identical to the subject of the major, and the syllogism's validity becomes uncertain.
The objector claims that without knowledge of the restriction, no necessary conclusion follows.
5
Evaluate the author's refutation
The author asserts that the logical necessity (luzum) of the conclusion from the premises is self-evident among logicians. The objector's sophisms do not undermine this necessity.
Consider whether this refutation is logically satisfactory or merely an appeal to authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] ومنها أي من الشبهات أن يقال إنا إذا قلنا كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان حساس؛ فالمحمول في الصغرى لا يخلو من أن يكون هو الحيوان مطلقا، أو لا يكون بل يكون مقيدا. والأول باطل فإن المحمول في الصغرى على خلاف الموضوع في الكبرى حينئذ لما أن الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان مغاير لكل واحد من الحيوان. وقد علمنا بأنه يصح أن يقال الحيوان جنس وعام ومشترك بين جميع الحيوانات، ولا يصح أن يقال كل حيوان جنس وعام أو مشترك أصلا. وأما الثاني فكذلك فإنه لا يدرى بأنه ما هو وكيف هو؛ واللفظ قاصر عنه فلا يدرى بأنه متحد مع الموضوع في الكبرى أم غير متحد. ولو كان كذلك فلا يلزم منه ومن أمثاله شيء؛ واللزوم من جملة الضروريات عند أهل المنطق.
Among them, i.e., the sophisms, is that it is said: When we say 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is sentient', then the predicate in the minor premise is either 'animal' absolutely, or it is not but rather restricted. The first is false, for then the predicate in the minor would be contrary to the subject in the major, because animal qua animal is different from each animal. And we know that it is correct to say 'animal is a genus, universal, and common to all animals', but it is not correct to say 'every animal is a genus, universal, or common' at all. As for the second, it is likewise, for it is not known what it is and how it is; the expression falls short of it, so it is not known whether it is identical to the subject in the major or not. And if it were so, nothing follows from it and its likes; and the following is among the necessary things according to the logicians.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents the second sophism (shubha) regarding the categorical syllogism. The objector argues that the predicate of the minor premise ('animal') either is the absolute genus or something restricted. If absolute, then animal qua animal is not identical to any particular animal, so the predicate of the minor is not the same as the subject of the major ('animal' in the major premise), breaking the unity of the middle term. Moreover, saying 'animal is a genus' is correct, but 'every animal is a genus' is false, showing the term functions differently. If restricted, the exact meaning is unknown and cannot be assessed for identity. The author refutes by asserting that the logical necessity of the conclusion is self-evident among logicians; such objections do not undermine it.

Key Terms
الصغرى
المحمول
الكبرى
الموضوع
اللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second shubha, what does the objector claim about the predicate of the minor premise?
A) It must be restricted to avoid contradiction.
B) It is either absolute or restricted, both problematic.
C) It is always the same as the subject of the major.
D) It is a disjunctive term.

Key Takeaway
The objector's attack on the identity of the middle term is refuted by the self-evidence of logical necessity among logicians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Wine is intoxicating; every intoxicant is forbidden; therefore wine is forbidden.' Identify the middle term and explain why the identity condition is satisfied.
?

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
The Third Shubha: Self-Reference Dilemma
Objection to universal major premise using a self-reference dilemma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical dilemma used in the third shubha and explain why it fails.

Core Idea
A universal cannot be an individual of itself.

The third shubha attacks the necessity of a universal major premise by posing a dilemma: the term 'animal' either falls under its own universal or not, both leading to absurdity. This objection, though seemingly clever, rests on a category mistake. In this lesson, we will dissect the objection's logical structure and pinpoint its flaw.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target
The objector targets the universal quantifier in 'Every animal is sentient'. What is being challenged? The necessity of the major premise being universal.
2
Extract the disjunction
The objector presents two alternatives: either 'animal' is included in the scope of 'every animal' or it is not. Write these as two conditions.
3
Analyze each horn
For inclusion: animal qua animal would be an individual of animal, which is absurd because a universal cannot be an individual. For exclusion: animal would be outside the concept of 'every animal', contradicting universality.
4
Reconstruct the dilemma
Combine the two conditionals: if included then absurd, if not included then contradiction. The conclusion is that the major premise fails to yield the conclusion.
5
Identify the mistake
The flaw is treating the universal as if it were an individual member of its own extension. A universal is not a particular instance.
This is a category mistake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] ومنها أن يقال إذا قلنا وكل حيوان حساس: فالحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان لا يخلو من أن يكون داخلا في قولنا كل حيوان؛ وحينئذ يكون الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان فردًا من أفراد الحيوان وذلك محال؛ إذ هو مشترك بين الكل والفرد أي فرد كان لا يمكن أن يكون مشتركًا. أو لا يكون داخلا؛ وحينئذ يكون الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان خارجا عن مفهوم قولنا كل حيوان. وإنه مما ينا في الكلية؛ وقد كانت الكلية في الكبرى من اللوازم فلا يلزم إذن من هذا القول قولنا كل إنسان حساس.
Among them is that it is said: If we say 'Every animal is sentient', then animal qua animal is either included in our saying 'every animal'; then animal qua animal would be an individual of animal, which is impossible, for it is common to the whole and the individual—any individual cannot be common. Or it is not included; then animal qua animal is outside the concept of our saying 'every animal', and that contradicts the universality; and the universality in the major was among the necessary concomitants, so from this statement our statement 'Every human is sentient' does not follow.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the third sophism targeting the universal quantifier in the major premise. The objector argues: when we say 'Every animal is sentient', the term 'animal' (the universal) either falls under its own extension or not. If it does, then the universal 'animal' would be an individual of itself, which is absurd because a universal cannot be a particular instance. If it does not, then the universal 'animal' is excluded from the scope of 'every animal', contradicting the intended universality. Since the universality of the major is essential for the middle term to be fully taken, the conclusion 'Every human is sentient' does not follow. The author implies this objection fails because it misunderstands the nature of universals.

Key Terms
الكلية
المشترك
الفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the third shubha consider about 'animal' in 'Every animal is sentient'?
A) It is an individual of itself.
B) It is either included or excluded from its own extension.
C) It is always restricted.
D) It is a predicate.

Key Takeaway
The third shubha confuses a universal term with an individual instance, creating a false dilemma that does not invalidate the necessity of a universal major premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the syllogism 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden'. Apply the same shubha to the term 'intoxicant' and explain why it fails using the distinction between universal and individual.
?
Identify a passage in a classical usul al-fiqh text where a universal term is used in a major premise. Check if this shubha could be raised and write a brief defense.

The Syllogism

10 min
The Fourth Shubha: Is Premise Order Necessary?
Objection questioning necessity of premise order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the dilemma about premise order in categorical syllogisms and explain why the order is essential for validity.

Core Idea
Premise order determines whether a syllogism can produce its conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine a classic sophism that questions whether the specific order of premises (minor then major) is a necessary condition for syllogistic production. The objection presents a dilemma: if order is a condition, then granting the premises individually does not grant the conclusion; if it is not a condition, then the conclusion could follow without order, which contradicts the fact that only the first mood of the first figure (with its specific order) yields a universal affirmative. By parsing this objection, you will see why order is not an arbitrary convention but a structural requirement for valid inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dilemma
The passage presents a dilemma: either the specific order of premises is a condition for the syllogism to produce the conclusion, or it is not. Each horn leads to a problematic consequence.
Note the two alternatives: condition vs. no condition.
2
Examine the first horn
If the order is a condition, then merely granting each premise individually does not entail the conclusion unless the premises are composed in that exact order. This contradicts the claim that a syllogism is self-sufficient once its premises are granted.
The objector argues this would make the syllogism dependent on an external ordering requirement.
3
Examine the second horn
If order is not a condition, then the conclusion could follow without that specific arrangement. But this is impossible because only the first mood of the first figure (which requires that specific order) produces a universal affirmative like 'Every human is sentient'.
The objection assumes that the first figure's mood is the only valid form for such a conclusion.
4
Synthesize the author's response
Although the passage only presents the objection, the implied response is that the order is not an external condition but an intrinsic part of the syllogistic form. The first figure's structure inherently requires the minor premise before the major for the conclusion to follow necessarily.
The author (likely the commentator) defends that order is not an extra condition but a constitutive element of the syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤] ومنها أن يقال النظر في الترتيب المخصوص بها بين المقدمتين لا يخلو من أن يكون شرطا للإنتاج؛ وحينئذ لا يلزم من تسليم كل واحدة منهما تسليم قولنا كل إنسان حساس بل يفتقر إلى أن يكون التركيب منهما بذلك الترتيب. وإنه على خلاف قولنا حتى سلمت لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر. أو لا يكون شرطا؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون اللزوم ممكناً بدون ذلك الترتيب، وذلك محال لما كان المنتج للموجبة الكلية هو الضرب الأول من الشكل الأول ليس إلا.
Among them is that it is said: Consideration of the specific order between the two premises is either a condition for production of the conclusion; then from granting each one of them, granting our statement 'Every human is sentient' does not follow, but rather it requires the composition of them in that order. And that contradicts our saying 'even if granted, it necessarily follows by itself another statement'. Or it is not a condition; then the following would be possible without that order, and that is impossible because the producer of the universal affirmative is only the first mood of the first figure.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth sophism about the necessity of the order of premises. The objector queries whether the specific order (minor then major) is a condition for the syllogism to produce the conclusion. If it is a condition, then simply granting each premise individually does not yield the conclusion unless they are combined in that order, contradicting the claim that the syllogism is self-sufficient. If it is not a condition, then the conclusion could follow without that order, which is false since only the first mood of the first figure (with that order) produces a universal affirmative like 'Every human is sentient'. The author thus defends the necessity of order.

Key Terms
الترتيب
الإنتاج
الضرب الأول من الشكل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth shubha, if the specific order of premises is a condition for production, what follows?
A) The conclusion follows from each premise individually.
B) The conclusion follows only when premises are composed in that order.
C) The conclusion follows even without that order.
D) The syllogism is invalid regardless of order.

Key Takeaway
The order of premises in a categorical syllogism is not arbitrary; it is intrinsic to the syllogistic form that produces the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Ensure the minor premise comes before the major. Then try reversing the premises and explain why the resulting syllogism is invalid.

The Syllogism

5 min
Self-Predication Fallacy in Syllogisms
Author refutes the fifth sophism about self-predication in syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify self-predication conclusions in categorical arguments and explain why they are invalid.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism cannot conclude a tautology like 'A is A'.

The fifth shubha attacks syllogisms by claiming that from premises like 'Every human is rational' we get the tautology 'Every human is human'. This lesson examines that objection and clarifies why such a conclusion is not actually entailed and is considered invalid by agreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed inference
The objection presents two examples: from 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal can be human or not', it claims the conclusion 'Every human can be human or not' follows; and from 'Every human is rational', it claims 'Every human is human' follows.
The second example is a direct case of self-predication (ittiṣāf al-shay’ bi-nafsih).
2
Check the logical necessity
In the second example, the conclusion 'Every human is human' is not logically entailed by the premise 'Every human is rational' alone. A valid inference requires a middle term; here there is none.
The objection misrepresents what a syllogism can produce.
3
Recognize self-predication
Self-predication means stating that a thing is itself (e.g., 'human is human'). This is considered bāṭil (invalid) because it adds no information and is universally rejected as a meaningful statement.
The author notes it is bāṭil bi-l-ittifāq (invalid by agreement).
4
Understand the refutation
The author argues that such conclusions do not follow from the premises. The objection fails because it confuses valid deduction with tautology construction.
A properly formed syllogism avoids self-predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[0] ومنها أن يقال إذا قلنا كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان يمكن أن يكون إنسانًا، ويمكن أن لا يكون فلا يلزم منه قولنا فكل إنسان يمكن أن يكون إنسانا ويمكن أن لا يكون. وكذلك إذا قلنا كل إنسان ناطق يلزم منه فكل إنسان إنسان؛ وإنه باطل وإلا لما بطل اتصاف الشيء بنفسه وقد بطل بالاتفاق.
Among them is that it is said: If we say 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal can be a human and can not be', then from it our saying 'Every human can be a human and can not be' does not follow. Similarly, if we say 'Every human is rational', from it follows 'Every human is human'; and that is false, otherwise predication of a thing of itself would not be invalid, but it is invalid by agreement.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth sophism concerning syllogisms that produce tautologies or self-predications. For example, from 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal can be human or not', one might think the conclusion should be 'Every human can be human or not' – which is not necessary. More directly, from 'Every human is rational', it might seem to imply 'Every human is human', which is a tautology. The author refutes by noting that self-predication (e.g., 'A is A') is universally considered invalid as a meaningful statement, so such a conclusion is absurd and does not follow correctly.

Key Terms
اتصاف الشيء بنفسه
باطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the fifth shubha claim about the syllogism 'Every human is rational'?
A) It produces 'Every human is human'.
B) It produces 'Every human is rational'.
C) It produces 'Every rational is human'.
D) It produces nothing.

Key Takeaway
Self-predication conclusions are invalid and should be rejected; a valid syllogism never yields 'A is A'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify if the following argument contains a self-predication: 'Every act of worship is an act of obedience; every act of obedience is an act of worship; therefore every act of worship is an act of worship.' Explain why it is invalid.
?

Definiteness and Validity in Second Figure Syllogisms
Author critiques second figure and third mood using indefinite/definite distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a categorical syllogism fails due to a mismatch between indefinite and definite terms in the middle term, and recognize when a conclusion is restricted.

Core Idea
The identity of the middle term requires careful attention to definiteness and indefiniteness across figures.

In the previous lesson, we saw objections to the first mood of the first figure. Now the author shows that these objections are not unique—they apply to the second figure and other moods as well. By examining the logical mismatch between indefinite and definite terms, we uncover a deeper condition for valid syllogisms: the middle term must be conceptually unified. This lesson will train you to spot such mismatches in Islamic syllogistic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and mood
Locate which figure and mood the author is critiquing. Here, the second mood of the second figure (e.g., Cesare or Camestres) and the third mood (probably of the first figure) are discussed.
Moods are labeled differently in various traditions; focus on the logical shape, not the name.
2
Check the definiteness of the middle term
In the example 'Every human is an animal' (minor) and 'No animal is lifeless' (major), note that 'animal' in the minor is the predicate—it is indefinite (nakira) because it lacks the definite article. In the major, 'animal' is the subject and is definite (ma'rifa) because of the definite article. These are not identical terms, so the middle term is not unified.
In Arabic logic, the definiteness of a term affects its logical behavior.
3
Analyze why the conclusion fails
Because the middle term is not truly identical across premises, the syllogism does not yield a universal negative conclusion as expected. The author provides a counterexample: 'Every human is a respirator' and 'No respirator is an inhaler' would imply 'No human is an inhaler', but that conclusion does not actually follow due to the indefinite-definite mismatch.
This shows that the second figure is not immune to the same criticisms as the first.
4
Examine the third mood restriction
For the third mood (e.g., Disamis or Datisi), the passage shows that from 'Some human is a writer' and 'Every writer is moving', the conclusion 'Some human is moving' does not follow unconditionally. Instead, only 'Some human who is a writer is moving' follows—a restricted particular affirmative. The unrestricted conclusion would require the term 'writer' to be distributed in the minor premise, which it is not.
The lesson: particular conclusions may rely on restrictions from the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا في الضرب الثاني وغيره من الضروب الأربعة؛ فإن هذه الوجوه بأجمعها غير مخصوصة بالضرب الأول. وقد يقال في الثاني منها أيضًا إنه لا ينتج أصلا فإن الأوسط فيه لا يكون متحدا في مثل قولنا كل إنسان حيوان، ولا شيء من الحيوان بجماد لما أن المحمول في الصغرى منكر، والموضوع في الكبرى مُعرَف؛ والمعرف غير المنكر ؛ إذ المفهوم من المُعرَف نفس الماهية لا غيره، ومن المنكر لا يكون كذلك. ولأنه لا ينتج سالبة كلية وإلا لكان قولنا كل إنسان متنفس، ولا شيء من المتنفس بمستنشق ينتج سالبة كلية؛ وهي قولنا لا شيء من الإنسان بمستنشق، ولا ينتج. وفي الثالث يقال إنه لا ينتج موجبة جزئية من غير قيد فإنا إذا قلنا بعض الإنسان كاتب وكل كاتب متحرك لا ينتج قولنا بعض الإنسان متحرك بل ينتج بعض الإنسان الذي هو كاتب متحرك. ولا يلزم من اتصاف الإنسان الذي هو كاتب بالحركة اتصاف بعض
And upon this in the second mood and other moods of the four; for these aspects altogether are not exclusive to the first mood. And it may be said of the second of them also that it does not produce at all, for the middle term in it is not united in like our saying 'Every human is an animal' and 'No animal is a lifeless thing', because the predicate in the minor is indefinite, and the subject in the major is definite; and the definite is not the indefinite, for the concept of the definite is the essence itself, not other, and of the indefinite it is not so. And because it does not produce a universal negative, otherwise our saying 'Every human is a respirator' and 'No respirator is an inhaler' would produce a universal negative, which is our saying 'No human is an inhaler', but it does not produce. And in the third it is said that it does not produce a particular affirmative without a restriction; for if we say 'Some human is a writer' and 'Every writer is moving', it does not produce our saying 'Some human is moving', but rather produces 'Some human who is a writer is moving'. And it does not follow from the description of the human who is a writer by movement the description of some
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage extends the discussion of objections to the second figure and other moods. The author argues that the same problematic aspects found in the first mood also apply to other moods. Specifically, in the second figure (where the middle term is predicate in both premises), the middle term may not be identical when combining an affirmative minor with a negative major, because the predicate of the minor is indefinite (nakira) while the subject of the major is definite (ma'rifa), leading to a mismatch. Moreover, some moods fail to produce a universal negative conclusion when expected, as with the example about respirators. Regarding the third mood (of the first figure?), it fails to produce a particular affirmative without a restriction: from 'Some human is a writer' and 'Every writer is moving', the conclusion 'Some human is moving' does not follow unconditionally; only 'Some human that is a writer is moving' follows, which is restricted. The author uses these examples to show that the objections are not limited to the first mood but apply broadly.

Key Terms
منكر
مُعرَف
الضرب الثاني
الضرب الثالث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the second mood of the second figure (e.g., 'Every human is animal, No animal is lifeless') not produce a conclusion?
A) The middle term is not united due to indefinite/definite mismatch.
B) The premises are both particular.
C) The conclusion would be tautological.
D) The order of premises is wrong.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a categorical syllogism depends on the precise identity of the middle term, which can be disrupted by differences in definiteness between its instances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every prayer requires purification' (minor) and 'No purification is valid without intention' (major). Does this produce the conclusion 'No prayer is valid without intention'? Identify if there is an indefinite-definite mismatch and evaluate validity.
?
Take the premises 'Some faqih is a scholar' and 'Every scholar is knowledgeable'. Does the conclusion 'Some faqih is knowledgeable' follow unconditionally? What restriction would be needed according to the author's critique?

10 min
The First Doubt: Arrangement as a Condition for Productivity
Refutation of the first doubt about arrangement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that premise arrangement is a condition for productivity and evaluate the author's refutation using a disjunctive syllogism.

Core Idea
Arrangement reveals an existing entailment; it is not an external condition.

The objector presents a dilemma: if the specific arrangement of premises is a condition for productivity, then admitting each premise separately does not give the conclusion; if it is not a condition, then entailment should be possible without it, which is false. The author refutes this by clarifying that the arrangement is not a condition but the expression of the middle term's function. Let's walk through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive dilemma
The objector gives two options: arrangement is either a condition or not. Summarize each horn.
First horn: arrangement as condition → premises alone insufficient. Second horn: not a condition → entailment possible without arrangement.
2
Analyze the first horn
If arrangement is a condition, then admitting each premise alone does not entail the conclusion; the composition is needed. This challenges the claim that first-figure form alone yields certainty.
3
Analyze the second horn
If arrangement is not a condition, then entailment should be possible without it, but only the first mood of first figure produces universal affirmative, contradicting that.
4
Examine the author's refutation
The author argues that 'Every J is B' means everything that is J is B in some way, and the middle term is considered in terms of individuals. The arrangement is not an external condition but the expression of the relationship.
The author distinguishes between expression of qualification and judgment about qualification.
5
Reconstruct the refutation as a disjunctive syllogism
The author shows that the disjunction is not exhaustive: arrangement is neither a condition nor irrelevant; it is the only way the middle term functions. Thus the objection fails.
The author's argument can be seen as a valid disjunctive syllogism denying both horns by showing the middle term's identity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤] ومنها أن يقال النظر في الترتيب المخصوص بها بين المقدمتين لا يخلو من أن يكون شرطا للإنتاج؛ وحينئذ لا يلزم من تسليم كل واحدة منهما تسليم قولنا كل إنسان حساس بل يفتقر إلى أن يكون التركيب منهما بذلك الترتيب. وإنه على خلاف قولنا حتى سلمت لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر. أو لا يكون شرطا؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون اللزوم ممكناً بدون ذلك الترتيب، وذلك محال لما كان المنتج للموجبة الكلية هو الضرب الأول من الشكل الأول ليس إلا. [0] ومنها أن يقال إذا قلنا كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان يمكن أن يكون إنسانًا، ويمكن أن لا يكون فلا يلزم منه قولنا فكل إنسان يمكن أن يكون إنسانا ويمكن أن لا يكون. وكذلك إذا قلنا كل إنسان ناطق يلزم منه فكل إنسان إنسان؛ وإنه باطل وإلا لما بطل اتصاف الشيء بنفسه وقد بطل بالاتفاق. وعلى هذا في الضرب الثاني وغيره من الضروب الأربعة؛ فإن هذه الوجوه بأجمعها غير مخصوصة بالضرب الأول. وقد يقال في الثاني منها أيضًا إنه لا ينتج أصلا فإن الأوسط فيه لا يكون متحدا في مثل قولنا كل إنسان حيوان، ولا شيء من الحيوان بجماد لما أن المحمول في الصغرى منكر، والموضوع في الكبرى مُعرَف؛ والمعرف غير المنكر؛ إذ المفهوم من المُعرَف نفس الماهية لا غيره، ومن المنكر لا يكون كذلك. ولأنه لا ينتج سالبة كلية وإلا لكان قولنا كل إنسان متنفس، ولا شيء من المتنفس بمستنشق ينتج سالبة كلية؛ وهي قولنا لا شيء من الإنسان بمستنشق، ولا ينتج. وفي الثالث يقال إنه لا ينتج موجبة جزئية من غير قيد فإنا إذا قلنا بعض الإنسان كاتب وكل كاتب متحرك لا ينتج قولنا بعض الإنسان متحرك بل ينتج بعض الإنسان الذي هو كاتب متحرك. ولا يلزم من اتصاف الإنسان الذي هو كاتب بالحركة اتصاف بعض الإنسان من حيث هو بعض الإنسان بالحركة. وكذلك في الرابع يقال إنه لا ينتج سالبة جزئية من غير قيد بل ينتج مع القيد؛ وذلك على خلاف ما ذكرتم. ولنقتصر على هذا القدر من الشبهات فإن مثل هذه الشبهات تذكر من بعد في الأقيسة السوفسطائية وغيرها. أما الجواب فنقول : [۱] في الوجه الأول إذا قلنا كل «ج» (ب) فالمراد منه أن كل ما صدق عليه «ج» صدق عليه «ب» في الجملة، وكل ما صدق عليه (ج) فهو (ج) لا محالة، إما ذاتا وإما عرضًا. وكذلك كل ما صدق عليه «ب» فإنه «ب»، إما ذاتا أو عرضًا. ثمَّ إذا قلنا وكل «ب أ» فكأنا قلنا وكل ما صدق عليه (ب) فهو «أ» لكن ما يدل عليه بطريق الاتحاد هو قولنا كل «ج» «ب»، وهذا من جملة ما لا فساد فيه أصلا. وقد يقال في الجواب عنه إن المراد من قولنا كل «ج» «ب» بیان اتصاف الجيم بالباء بهذا اللفظ. وبيان اتصاف الشيء بالشيء غير الحكم عليه بأنه متصف بذلك الشيء فلا يلزم منه إذن ما يلزم من الحكم عليه . ثمَّ من المعلوم أن اتصاف الشيء بالشيء لا يمكن إلا وأن يكون هذا الشيء غير ذلك، وأن بيان الاتصاف بقولنا هذا ذلك لا يكون مشتملا على ما ذكرتم من الفساد وكيف وقد كان الأصغر متصفًا بالأوسط، والأوسط بالأكبر على وجه يلزم منه أن يكون الأصغر متصفًا بالأكبر، والأوسط متحد لا محالة فيه أن يكون في الصغرى غير ما في الكبرى؛ وبالعكس حينئذ. [٢] وأما في الثاني فنقول المحمول في الصغرى هو الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان؛ وإنه لا يغاير الموضوع في الكبرى؛ إذ الموضوع في الكبرى هو الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان أيضًا. وكلمة الكل لا تكون داخلة فيه بل إنما تذكر لبيان الكمية بحسب ما تحته من الأفراد فقولنا كل حيوان حساس يفهم
[4] Among them is to say: Consideration of the specific arrangement between the two premises cannot be free from either being a condition for productivity; then the admission of each one of them does not necessitate the admission of our statement 'Every human is sentient', but rather it requires the composition of them in that arrangement. And this is contrary to our statement 'if admitted, it necessarily entails by itself another statement'. Or it is not a condition; then it would necessitate that the entailment be possible without that arrangement, and that is impossible because the producer of the universal affirmative is only the first mood of the first figure. [0] Among them is to say: If we say 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal can be a human and can not be', then our statement 'Every human can be a human and can not be' does not follow. Likewise, if we say 'Every human is rational', it follows that 'Every human is human'; and that is false, otherwise a thing's self-predication would not be invalid, and it is invalid by agreement. And so on for the second mood and other of the four moods; for these aspects collectively are not specific to the first mood. It is also said about the second mood that it does not produce at all, because the middle term in it is not identical in such as our statement 'Every human is an animal' and 'No animal is inanimate' — since the predicate in the minor is indefinite, and the subject in the major is definite; and the definite is not the indefinite; for the concept of the definite is the essence itself not other, and from the indefinite it is not so. And because it does not produce a universal negative, otherwise our statement 'Every human is breathing' and 'No breathing thing is a non-respirer' would produce a universal negative, which is our statement 'No human is a non-respirer', and it does not produce. In the third it is said that it does not produce a particular affirmative without a qualification; for if we say 'Some human is a writer' and 'Every writer is moving', it does not produce our statement 'Some human is moving' but rather produces 'Some human who is a writer is moving'. And the qualification of the human who is a writer by movement does not necessitate the qualification of some human insofar as he is some human by movement. Likewise in the fourth it is said that it does not produce a particular negative without a qualification, but produces with the qualification; and that is contrary to what you mentioned. We will limit ourselves to this amount of doubts, for such doubts are mentioned later in sophistical syllogisms and others. As for the answer, we say: [1] Regarding the first aspect: When we say 'Every J is B', what is meant is that everything of which 'J' is true, 'B' is true of it in some way, and everything of which 'J' is true is 'J' necessarily, either essentially or accidentally. Likewise, everything of which 'B' is true is 'B', either essentially or accidentally. Then when we say 'And every B is A', it is as if we said 'And everything of which B is true is A', but what is indicated by way of identity is our statement 'Every J is B', and this is among that which has no corruption at all. It may be said in answer to it that what is meant by our statement 'Every J is B' is the expression of the qualification of J by B with this wording. And the expression of a thing's qualification by something is other than the judgment that it is qualified by that thing, so what follows from the judgment does not follow from it. Then it is known that a thing's qualification by something is not possible except that this thing is other than that, and that the expression of qualification by our saying 'this is that' does not contain the corruption you mentioned. How could it when the minor term was qualified by the middle term, and the middle term by the major term in a way that necessitates the minor term being qualified by the major term, and the middle term is necessarily identical [in both premises]? It must be that what is in the minor [premise] is other than what is in the major, and vice versa then. [2] As for the second [aspect], we say: The predicate in the minor is animal insofar as it is animal; and it does not differ from the subject in the major; for the subject in the major is also animal insofar as it is animal. And the word 'every' is not included in it, but rather it is mentioned to indicate the quantity according to the individuals under it, so our statement 'Every animal is sentient' is understood
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections (shubuhat) against the claim that only the first figure's first mood produces universal affirmative conclusions, followed by the author's refutation (jawab). The author first lists four doubts, then answers the first two in detail (the third and fourth are mentioned but the answer is cut off due to page break). The doubts challenge the necessity of the specific arrangement of premises, the identity of the middle term, and the productivity of moods beyond the first. The answer for the first doubt clarifies that the statement 'Every J is B' means that everything that is J is B, and that the middle term is considered in terms of the individuals it applies to, not as a mere word. For the second doubt, the author insists that the predicate in the minor is 'animal' as a species, identical to the subject in the major, and that 'every' indicates quantity, not inclusion in the nature. The author uses examples from the Islamic sciences: 'human', 'animal', 'rational', 'sentient', 'inanimate', 'writer', etc., which are standard in logic texts. The commentary should follow the order: first the presentation of doubts, then the answer. The passage is a typical jadal (dialectical) exchange aiming to defend the standard Aristotelian syllogistic against skeptical challenges.

Checkpoint
In the refutation of the first doubt, what does the author say about the statement 'Every J is B'?
A) It is a judgment about the arrangement.
B) It expresses the qualification of J by B, not a separate judgment.
C) It is only valid if J and B are definite.
D) It requires the arrangement to be a condition.

Key Takeaway
The arrangement of premises is not an external condition but the natural expression of the middle term's identity, so the objection fails.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Second Doubt: Middle Term Identity with Indefinite and Definite Terms
Refutation of the second doubt about middle term identity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection that the middle term is not identical due to definiteness and evaluate the author's refutation using the concept of essence (mahiyya).

Core Idea
The middle term is identical in essence, not in word form.

The objector argues that in 'Every human is an animal' and 'No animal is inanimate', the middle term 'animal' is indefinite in the first premise and definite in the second, so they are not identical. The author refutes by showing that the predicate in the minor is 'animal' considered as a species (essence), which is identical to the subject in the major. Let's examine this subtle but crucial point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims the middle term is not identical because the predicate in the minor is indefinite (nakira) while the subject in the major is definite (mu'arraf). In 'Every human is an animal', 'animal' is indefinite; in 'No animal is inanimate', 'animal' is definite. These are different, so the middle term is not unified.
2
Understand the definiteness distinction
In Arabic logic, an indefinite noun (nakira) refers to any individual of the species, while a definite noun (mu'arraf) refers to the essence itself. The objector argues that these are not the same, so the middle term fails.
The definite term indicates the essence (mahiyya) itself, while the indefinite indicates some instantiation.
3
Analyze the author's refutation
The author states that the predicate in the minor is 'animal' insofar as it is animal – i.e., the species itself, not an indefinite individual. The definite article does not change the essence; it only indicates quantity or reference. The middle term is the same species concept in both premises.
The author says: 'المحمول في الصغرى هو الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان' – the predicate in the minor is animal as such.
4
Clarify the role of 'every' (kull)
The word 'every' in the major premise ('Every animal is sentient') does not change the identity of the term; it only specifies the quantity of individuals. The essence remains the same.
5
Apply the refutation to the objection
Since the middle term is identical in essence, the syllogism is valid. The objection based on grammatical definiteness is irrelevant to logical identity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the difference between the indefinite 'animal' in the minor and definite 'animal' in the major not affect the syllogism?
A) The indefinite and definite are synonyms.
B) The predicate in the minor is animal as a species, identical to the subject in the major.

Key Takeaway
The middle term is identical in logical essence despite grammatical differences in definiteness; the essence (mahiyya) is what matters.

Answering the First Objection: Predication in Syllogisms
Author resolves the first objection regarding predication terms in the categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the expression of attribution and the judgment of predication in a categorical syllogism, and explain why the former does not lead to a logical paradox.

Core Idea
Attribution is not identity in syllogistic predication.

In categorical syllogisms, the statements 'every J is B' and 'every B is A' may seem to imply that J and A become identical, but the author clarifies that these are only attributions. This lesson will help you understand the distinction between expressing an attribution and making a judgment of predication, which is crucial for avoiding fallacies in syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection likely claims that if every J is B and every B is A, then J and A become identical in a problematic way. Understand this potential paradox.
2
Examine the first response
The author states that 'every J is B' means that everything that is J is also B, not that J and B are identical. This is mere attribution.
The text says: 'كل ما صدق عليه «ج» صدق عليه «ب» في الجملة'.
3
Note the distinction
The author distinguishes between 'expression of attribution' (بيان الاتصاف) and 'judgment of predication' (الحكم). Attributive statements do not carry the same implications as judgments of identity.
The expression of attribution is different from judging a thing to be described by that attribute.
4
Apply to the syllogism
The middle term B is attributed to the minor J, and A is attributed to B, so by continuity A is attributed to J. No paradox arises because it is not a judgment of identity.
The author emphasizes that the middle term is 'necessarily identical in that what is in the minor is not what is in the major'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الوجه الأول إذا قلنا كل «ج» (ب) فالمراد منه أن كل ما صدق عليه «ج» صدق عليه «ب» في الجملة، وكل ما صدق عليه (ج) فهو (ج) لا محالة، إما ذاتا وإما عرضًا. وكذلك كل ما صدق عليه «ب» فإنه «ب»، إما ذاتا أو عرضًا. ثمَّ إذا قلنا وكل «ب أ» فكأنا قلنا وكل ما صدق عليه (ب) فهو «أ» لكن ما يدل عليه بطريق الاتحاد هو قولنا كل «ج» «ب»، وهذا من جملة ما لا فساد فيه أصلا. وقد يقال في الجواب عنه إن المراد من قولنا كل «ج» «ب» بیان اتصاف الجيم بالباء بهذا اللفظ. وبيان اتصاف الشيء بالشيء غير الحكم عليه بأنه متصف بذلك الشيء فلا يلزم منه إذن ما يلزم من الحكم عليه . ثمَّ من المعلوم أن اتصاف الشيء بالشيء لا يمكن إلا وأن يكون هذا الشيء غير ذلك، وأن بيان الاتصاف بقولنا هذا ذلك لا يكون مشتملا على ما ذكرتم من الفساد وكيف وقد كان الأصغر متصفًا بالأوسط، والأوسط بالأكبر على وجه يلزم منه أن يكون الأصغر متصفًا بالأكبر، والأوسط متحد لا محالة فيه أن يكون في الصغرى غير ما في الكبرى؛ وبالعكس حينئذ.
Regarding the first aspect: when we say 'every J is B', what is meant is that everything to which J applies, B applies to it in general, and everything to which J applies is necessarily J, either essentially or accidentally. Similarly, everything to which B applies is B, either essentially or accidentally. Then when we say 'every B is A', it is as if we said 'everything to which B applies is A', but what is indicated by way of identity is our statement 'every J is B', and this is among things that have no corruption at all. And it is said in answer to it that what is meant by our statement 'every J is B' is the expression of the attribution of J to B by this wording. And the expression of the attribution of a thing to a thing is different from judging it to be attributed to that thing, so there does not follow from it what follows from the judgment. Then it is known that the attribution of a thing to a thing can only be that this thing is other than that, and the expression of attribution by our statement 'this is that' does not contain the corruption you mentioned. How could it, when the minor term was attributed to the middle term, and the middle to the major, in a way that necessitates that the minor be attributed to the major, while the middle term is necessarily identical in that what is in the minor is not what is in the major, and vice versa then.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the first objection raised against syllogistic inference. The author clarifies that when we state a universal affirmative proposition like 'every J is B', we mean that every individual that falls under J also falls under B, without implying that J and B are identical in essence. The objection presumably claimed a paradox of self-predication. The author gives two responses: first, the meaning is straightforward and leads to no corruption; second, even if one interprets the expression as a mere attribution (not a judgment of identity), the conclusion follows validly because attribution is distinct from judgment. The example uses the standard categorical syllogism with terms ج (minor), ب (middle), أ (major).

Key Terms
اتصاف
الحكم
الاتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The attribution of ج to ب is impossible.
D) The syllogism is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In a categorical syllogism, the premises express attribution, not identity, so no paradoxical identity between subject and predicate follows.

Resolving Predicate-Subject Identity in Syllogisms
Author answers the objection about the predicate in the minor premise being different from the subject in the major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and resolve equivocation between the predicate of the minor premise and the subject of the major premise in a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The middle term must be taken in its absolute essence to ensure syllogistic validity.

In this lesson, we examine a classic objection in syllogistic logic: the claim that the predicate in the minor premise differs from the subject in the major. The author demonstrates that when both are considered in their absolute quiddity (e.g., 'animal as such'), they are identical. This understanding prevents equivocation and upholds the integrity of the syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Locate the claim that the predicate of the minor premise (e.g., 'sentient' in 'every animal is sentient') is not the same as the subject of the major premise (e.g., 'animal').
This objection assumes the predicate is the linguistic predicate, not the quiddity.
2
Extract the quiddity of the predicate
According to the author, the logical predicate in the minor is not the surface predicate but the quiddity of the subject term, i.e., 'animal as such'.
In 'every animal is sentient', the predicate term 'sentient' is accidental; the essential subject is 'animal as such'.
3
Compare with the subject of the major
The subject of the major premise is also 'animal as such'. Thus, both are identical in quiddity, resolving the equivocation.
The word 'every' only indicates quantity, not a change in essence.
4
Apply to a syllogism
Test a syllogism where the middle term is taken in its absolute essence. For example, in a legal analogy, the middle term 'intoxicating' must be considered in itself, not restricted.
This ensures the middle term is genuinely shared between premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الثاني فنقول المحمول في الصغرى هو الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان؛ وإنه لا يغاير الموضوع في الكبرى؛ إذ الموضوع في الكبرى هو الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان أيضًا. وكلمة الكل لا تكون داخلة فيه بل إنما تذكر لبيان الكمية بحسب ما تحته من الأفراد فقولنا كل حيوان حساس يفهم منه أن الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان موضوع لهذا المحمول في كل مادة وصورة. فلو كان المراد بالحيوان مطلقا هو الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان فلا نزاع فيه . وإن كان المراد به غيره فلا بد من البيان؛ إذ هو مقيد حينئذ؛ والقيد غير معلوم.
As for the second aspect, we say: the predicate in the minor premise is 'animal insofar as it is animal', and it does not differ from the subject in the major premise, for the subject in the major premise is also 'animal insofar as it is animal'. The word 'every' does not enter into it; rather it is only mentioned to indicate the quantity with respect to the individuals under it. So our statement 'every animal is sentient' is understood from it that animal insofar as it is animal is the subject for this predicate in every matter and form. So if what is meant by 'animal' absolutely is 'animal insofar as it is animal', then there is no dispute. But if what is meant is other than that, then clarification is necessary, for it is then restricted, and the restriction is unknown.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage answers the second objection, which likely claimed that the predicate in the minor premise (e.g., 'sentient' in 'every animal is sentient') is not the same as the subject in the major premise. The author argues that the predicate in the minor is actually 'animal qua animal', which is identical to the subject in the major. The word 'every' only indicates quantity (all individuals of animal), not a different entity. Thus, there is no discrepancy. The example uses 'حساس' (sentient) as the predicate of animal.

Key Terms

الكمية

Key Takeaway
To avoid equivocation, the predicate of the minor premise must be understood as the absolute quiddity of the subject, identical to the subject of the major premise.
