Animal qua Animal as an Individual Among Universals
Author denies that 'animal qua animal' is an individual of the species animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a universal being an individual of its own species versus being an individual among universals.

Core Idea
A universal is not an individual of its own species but an individual among universals.

This passage answers a third objection which argued that because 'animal qua animal' is included in 'every animal', it must be an individual member of the species animal. The author refutes this by clarifying the concept of 'individual' (fard). He explains that while 'animal qua animal' is indeed an individual—but not as a particular member of the species; rather, it is an individual among the class of universals. Understanding this distinction is key to navigating the hierarchy of universals in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being answered
The objection claims: if 'animal qua animal' is included in 'every animal', then it would be an individual among the individuals of the species 'animal'. Find and summarize this reasoning.
The objection mistakenly treats the inclusion of a universal in a statement about its instances as making that universal an instance of itself.
2
Locate the author's denial and its reason
The author says: 'it does not follow that it is an individual among the individuals of animal; rather it follows that it is an individual among the individuals of animals.' Note the shift from 'animal' (the species) to 'animals' (the categories of living things).
The author reinterprets 'individual' to mean a member of a broader set—here the set of all universal concepts related to animals.
3
Apply the principle of relative generality
The author states that 'animal qua animal' is a universal that encompasses everything, so it cannot be under another more general thing. This means it cannot be an individual of itself or of its own species; it is at the top of its genus.
A universal cannot be a member of the class it defines; it is a member of the class of universals.
4
Distinguish the two senses of 'individual'
One sense: a concrete particular (e.g., this horse). Another sense: a universal considered as one of the universals (e.g., the concept 'animal' as a member of the set of genera). The author accepts the second sense only.
This distinction prevents a category mistake: confusing levels of abstraction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الثالث فنقول الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان داخل في قولنا كل حيوان؛ ولا يلزم منه أن يكون الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان فردا من أفراد الحيوان بل يلزم منه إنه فرد من أفراد الحيوانات لما أنه بالنسبة إلى ما عداه عام يعم الكل فلا يمكن أن يكون تحت غيره الذي هو أعم. وكونه فردًا من أفراد الحيوانات لا يكون من جملة المتخيلات أصلا وقد مرَّ الكلام فيه من قبل.
As for the third aspect, we say: 'animal insofar as it is animal' is included in our statement 'every animal'; but it does not follow from that that 'animal insofar as it is animal' is an individual among the individuals of animal; rather it follows that it is an individual among the individuals of animals, because in relation to what is other than it, it is a universal that encompasses everything, so it cannot be under another thing that is more general. And its being an individual among the individuals of animals is not among the imaginary things at all, and the discussion about it has passed before.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage answers the third objection which likely argued that if 'animal qua animal' is included in 'every animal', then it would be an individual of the species 'animal'. The author refutes this: 'animal qua animal' is not an individual of the species 'animal' but rather an individual among the categories of animals (i.e., it is a universal that is a member of the class of universals). It cannot be under a more general term because it is itself the most general in its domain. The author references earlier discussion.

Key Terms
فرد
الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A universal term is not an individual of the species it names; it is an individual among the universals of that category.

Why Specific Arrangement is Necessary for a Syllogism
Author affirms that specific arrangement is a necessary condition for syllogistic productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the specific arrangement of premises is a necessary condition for syllogistic entailment, using the author's reasoning.

Core Idea
The specific arrangement of premises is not optional; it is built into the definition of a syllogism.

You may wonder whether a syllogism requires its premises to be in a particular order. The fourth objection raised this point, and the author responds by clarifying that the definition of syllogism itself implies a specific composition. Without that arrangement, no conclusion follows necessarily.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being answered
The fourth objection likely claimed that specific arrangement is not necessary for a syllogism to be productive. Recognize what claim the author is rejecting.
2
Extract the author's key claim
The author states that consideration of specific arrangement (al-tartīb al-makhṣūṣ) is a condition (sharṭ). Without it, the syllogism is not productive (lā yakūnu muntijan).
3
Analyze the definition of syllogism
The definition of syllogism is 'a statement composed of statements which, when accepted, necessitates by itself another statement.' Focus on what 'accepted' (taslīm) means here: not acceptance of the premises individually, but acceptance of them composed in a specific way.
The phrase 'composed in a specific composition' (mu'allafatan ta'līfan makhṣūṣan) is key.
4
Apply the concept to a counterexample
If you take the same true premises but rearrange them incorrectly (e.g., major before minor in a first-figure syllogism), the conclusion may not follow. Thus, arrangement is constitutive of the syllogistic form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الرابع فنقول النظر في الترتيب المخصوص شرط؛ إذ القياس بدونه لا يكون منتجا، وكونه شرطًا من جملة ما يفهم من قولنا قول مؤلف من أقوال متى سلمت لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر؛ إذ المراد من التسليم تسليم تلك الأقوال لا مطلقا بل تسليمها مؤلفة تأليفًا مخصوصًا لا يمكن أن يكون قياسا بدون ذلك التأليف والترتيب البتة.
As for the fourth aspect, we say: consideration of the specific arrangement is a condition, for the syllogism is not productive without it, and its being a condition is part of what is understood from our statement 'a statement composed of statements which, when accepted, necessitates by itself another statement', for what is meant by acceptance is acceptance of those statements not absolutely, but acceptance of them composed in a specific composition, and it cannot be a syllogism without that composition and arrangement at all.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage answers the fourth objection, which likely claimed that the specific arrangement of premises is not necessary for a syllogism to be productive. The author states that arrangement is a condition, as understood from the definition of syllogism: a discourse that, when accepted (with the correct composition), necessitates another statement. The acceptance is not of the premises in isolation, but of them composed in a specific manner. Without that specific arrangement, there is no syllogistic entailment.

Key Terms
الترتيب المخصوص
التسليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The specific arrangement of premises is not a stylistic choice but a necessary condition for syllogistic productivity, embedded in the very definition of a syllogism.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Conditions for a Valid Syllogism: The Middle Term Must Not Be a Concomitant
Author refutes objection using man/animal example, showing second premise false and dismissing convertible terms as non-syllogistic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a pair of universal affirmatives can form a valid categorical syllogism by checking if the middle term is a genuine intermediate or a mere concomitant.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism requires a middle term that mediates, not one that is a convertible concomitant.

The author of our text refutes a fifth objection that presented problematic examples involving 'man' and 'animal'. He shows that the second premise is false, and also that the example 'every man is rational, every rational is a man' does not constitute a proper syllogistic composition because the terms are concomitants (al-lawāzim). This teaches us a crucial condition for syllogistic validity: the middle term must genuinely mediate between the subject and predicate, not merely be a logical equivalent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises in the first example
The first example: 'every man is an animal' and 'every animal can be a man and can not be a man'. Locate these in the Arabic: 'كل إنسان حيوان' and 'وكل حيوان يمكن أن يكون إنسانًا ويمكن أن لا يكون'.
2
Check the truth of the second premise
The author argues that the second premise is false because man is a specific animal that cannot be both possibly man and possibly not man. In reality, man is necessarily man, so the premise 'every animal can be a man or not' does not hold.
This shows that a syllogism requires both premises to be true.
3
Examine the second example and why it fails as a syllogism
The second example: 'every man is rational' and 'every rational is a man'. The author says this is not a syllogistic composition because the terms are 'different definitions' that are concomitants (al-lawāzim). For a valid syllogism, the middle term must be a genuine mediator, not a term that is mutually convertible with the subject or predicate.
In this case, 'rational' and 'man' are interchangeable definitions; they do not function as a distinct middle term.
4
Generalize the condition for a valid middle term
From these examples, we learn that for a categorical syllogism, the middle term must be a genuine intermediate that connects the subject and predicate without being a concomitant. If the middle term is a logical equivalent of one of the extremes, the composition is either circular or trivial.
This condition is essential for syllogisms used in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الخامس فنقول إذا صدق قولنا كل إنسان حيوان فلا يمكن أن يصدق قولنا وكل حيوان يمكن أن يكون إنسانًا ويمكن أن لا يكون. وكيف يمكن والإنسان من الحيوانات ما لا يمكن أن يقال إنه يمكن أن يكون إنسانا ويمكن أن لا يكون. وأما قولكم كل إنسان ناطق، وكل ناطق إنسان فقد مرَّ من قبل أن مثل هذا التركيب لا يكون من التركيبات القياسية لما أن الحدود المختلفة من اللوازم؛ ولا وجود لها فيه وفي أمثاله، وهذا ظاهر.
As for the fifth aspect, we say: if our statement 'every man is an animal' is true, then our statement 'every animal can be a man and can not be a man' cannot be true. How can it be, when man is among the animals that cannot be said that it can be a man and can not be a man? As for your statement 'every man is rational, and every rational is a man', it has already passed before that such a composition is not among syllogistic compositions, because the different definitions are among the concomitants, and there is no existence of them in it and its likes, and this is clear.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage answers the fifth objection, which likely presented a problematic example with man and animal. The author argues that if 'every man is an animal' is true, then the premise 'every animal can be a man and can not be a man' is false because man is a specific animal (a human) and cannot be both possibly man and possibly not man — it is necessarily man. The author also dismisses the example of 'every man is rational, and every rational is a man' as not a proper syllogistic composition because the terms are convertible definitions (concomitants), not suited for syllogistic inference.

Key Terms
اللوازم
التركيبات القياسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Because the conclusion would be 'every man is a man', which is trivial.

Key Takeaway
Not every pair of true universal propositions forms a valid syllogism; the middle term must be a genuine intermediate, not a concomitant.

The Second Figure of the Syllogism and Its Conditions
Author defines the second figure and explains its conditions for productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second figure of a categorical syllogism and explain why its premises must differ in quality.

Core Idea
The second figure yields only negative conclusions because it infers contradiction from contradicting consequents.

This passage defines the second figure of the syllogism and explains its logical basis. The author shows that the second figure reduces to an inference from the contradiction of consequents to the contradiction of antecedents. Consequently, the conclusion must be negative. He then argues that for the inference to be valid, the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). Understanding this condition is crucial for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Define the second figure structure
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both the minor and major premises. Write an example from Islamic sources, e.g., 'All intoxicants are forbidden; this drink is not forbidden; therefore this drink is not an intoxicant.'
The middle term 'forbidden' appears as predicate in both premises.
2
Understand the logical basis
The second figure works by inferring contradiction of the antecedents (the subjects) from contradiction of the consequents (the predicates). This is why it only produces negative conclusions: if the consequents contradict, the antecedents must also contradict.
The author uses the phrase 'تعاند اللوازم على تعاند الملزومات' (contradiction of consequents implies contradiction of antecedents).
3
Identify the condition for validity
The premises must differ in quality: one affirmative and one negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, the syllogism is invalid because mere sharing of a consequent does not guarantee contradiction.
Check any second-figure syllogism: if both premises are affirmative, the conclusion cannot be negative; if both are negative, no valid deduction follows.
4
Distinguish types of difference
The author distinguishes between difference in accidents (العوارض) and difference in necessary consequences (اللوازم). Only difference in necessary consequences is useful; difference in accidents is common to both compatible and contradictory things and thus insufficient.
For example, two things may differ in accidental attributes but still be compatible; only if the difference is in a necessary consequent (e.g., existence vs. non-existence) does it indicate contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في الشكل الثاني ]
أما الشكل الثاني وهو الذي يكون الأوسط فيه محمولاً في الصغرى وفي الكبرى كذلك. فذلك بالحقيقة راجع إلى الاستدلال بتعاند اللوازم على تعاند الملزومات؛ ولذلك لا ينتج إلا سالبة جزئية كانت أو كلية. والضروب الحاصلة بالنسبة إلى المحصورات وغيرها هي ما يحصل من ضرب الثمانية في الثمانية. وبالنسبة إلى المحصورات لا غيرها هي ما يحصل من ضرب الأربعة في أربعة كما مرَّ في الشكل الأول لكن البعض منها ساقط لما أن الشرط في إنتاج هذا الشكل هو أن تكون إحدى مقدمتيه مخالفة للأخرى في الكيف؛ إذ الأمور المتشاركة في لازم واحد سلبًا كان أو إيجابًا. فإنها قد تكون متعاندة، وقد تكون متوافقة: إما في السلب فلأن كل شيئين متعاندين كانا أو متوافقين فلا بد من اشتراكهما في سلب ما عداهما عنهما، وإما في الإيجاب فلأن المتعاندين يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما معاندا للآخر بل يشتركان فيه وفي الغير من الأشياء نحو الجنس وفصل الجنس وجنس الفصل وغير ذلك. وكذلك إذا كانا متوافقين فإنهما يشتركان في كثير من الأشياء؛ وهذا ظاهر. وبالجملة فلا يمكن تحقق شيئين إلا وأن يكونا متشاركين في شيء ومتباينين في شيء سواء كان وجوديين أو لا وجوديين؛ وما به المشاركة غير ما به المباينة لا محالة. ولو كان كذلك فلا يصح الاستدلال به لا على التعاند البتة، ولا على التوافق كذلك فلا بد إذن في هذا الشكل من الاختلاف بين المقدمتين كيفا.
والاختلاف قد يكون في العوارض، وقد لا يكون بل يكون في اللوازم.
والأول لا يفيد فإنه يشترك فيه الأشياء المتوافقة والمتعاندة. وكيف لا وإنه لا يستحيل بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد على الخصوص بالنسبة إلى الأشياء
Section: On the Second Figure
As for the second figure, it is that in which the middle term is predicate in the minor premise and also predicate in the major premise. That in reality reduces to inference from the contradiction of consequents to the contradiction of the antecedents; therefore it yields only a negative conclusion, whether particular or universal. The moods that obtain with respect to quantified propositions and others are what result from multiplying eight by eight. With respect to quantified propositions only, they are what result from multiplying four by four, as mentioned in the first figure, but some of them are invalid because the condition for productivity in this figure is that one of its premises be opposite to the other in quality; for things that share a single consequent, whether negative or affirmative, may be contradictory or may be compatible. As for the negative: every two things, whether contradictory or compatible, must share in the negation of what is other than them. As for the affirmative: contradictory things share in that each one of them is contrary to the other, indeed they share in that and in other things such as the genus, the differentia of the genus, the genus of the differentia, and so on. Likewise, if they are compatible, they share in many things; this is obvious. In summary, it is impossible for two things to exist without sharing in something and differing in something, whether they are existential or non-existential; what constitutes the sharing is necessarily different from what constitutes the distinction. If that is the case, inference from that shared aspect to contradiction is not valid at all, nor to compatibility either. Therefore, in this figure, there must be difference between the two premises in quality. And the difference may be in accidents, or it may not be but rather in necessary consequences. The first [difference in accidents] is not useful, because both compatible and contradictory things share in it. How could it be otherwise? For it is not impossible with respect to a single thing specifically with respect to things...
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory discussion of the second figure of the categorical syllogism (الشكل الثاني). The author begins by defining the second figure: the middle term is the predicate in both the minor and major premises. He then explains its logical basis: it works by inferring the contradiction of the antecedents (الملزومات) from the contradiction of the consequents (اللوازم). Consequently, the second figure only produces negative conclusions (سالبة), whether universal or particular. The author mentions that the possible moods are derived from multiplying the number of premise types: eight by eight for all quantified propositions, or four by four for categorical propositions specifically, as in the first figure. However, not all combinations are valid; the key condition for productivity is that the two premises must differ in quality—one affirmative and one negative. The reasoning is that if two things share a single consequent (lāzim), they could be either contradictory or non-contradictory (compatible). For example, contradictory things share in having the opposite of something else, and compatible things also share in many attributes. In general, any two things necessarily share in something and differ in something. Therefore, mere sharing of a consequent does not indicate contradiction or compatibility. Hence, to infer contradiction, the premises must differ in quality. The author then distinguishes two types of difference: difference in accidents (العوارض) and difference in necessary consequences (اللوازم). Difference in accidents is insufficient because it is common to both compatible and contradictory things. The passage is cut off but continues the argument that difference in necessary consequences is what matters.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني
الأوسط
تعاند اللوازم
الكيف

B) They must both be universal.
C) They must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative).
D) They must have the same subject.

Key Takeaway
The second figure requires premises of different quality to validly infer a negative conclusion from the contradiction of consequents.

Distinguishing Accidental and Essential Difference for Second Figure Productivity
Author distinguishes accidental from essential difference and its effect on second-figure syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish accidental difference from essential difference in second-figure syllogisms and explain why only essential difference yields valid conclusions.

Core Idea
Accidental difference fails because both compatible and contradictory subjects share it; essential difference alone ensures contradiction.

In the second figure, the condition for productivity is not just any difference in quality between premises, but a specific kind: difference in essential consequences (lawāzim). The author warns that accidental difference (ʿawāriḍ) is insufficient, as it does not guarantee that the middle term truly distinguishes the subjects. This distinction is crucial for constructing sound syllogisms in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of difference
Locate where the author distinguishes accidental (ʿawāriḍ) from essential (lawāzim) difference. The key sentence is 'والاختلاف قد يكون في العوارض، وقد لا يكون بل يكون في اللوازم.'
2
Understand why accidental difference fails
The author says accidental difference is useless because both compatible and contradictory things (الأشياء المتوافقة والمتعاندة) share in it. This means the middle term does not specifically indicate contradiction.
3
Grasp the requirement for essential difference
Essential consequences (lawāzim) are necessary attributes that belong to one subject but not the other inevitably ('بالتعاند بينهما لا محالة'). This ensures a genuine contradiction.
4
Apply to second-figure syllogisms
When constructing a second-figure syllogism, check that the negative premise is based on an essential consequence, not an accidental attribute. Otherwise, the syllogism is invalid.
This condition eliminates eight moods from the second figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاختلاف قد يكون في العوارض، وقد لا يكون بل يكون في اللوازم. والأول لا يفيد فإنه يشترك فيه الأشياء المتوافقة والمتعاندة. وكيف لا وإنه لا يستحيل بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد على الخصوص بالنسبة إلى الأشياء المختلفة. ولو كان كذلك فلا يصح الاستدلال به البتة؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن ينعقد هذا الشكل من الممكنة الخاصة والعامة والوجودية اللا ضرورية واللا دائمة والوقتية والمنتشرة والمطلقة العامة. وبالجملة من جملة ما لا ينعكس في السوالب لا بسيطا ولا مختلطا والمشهور أنه إذا كان من المطلقتين ينعقد منتجا. والحق أنه لا ينتج بل إنما ينتج إذا كانت السالبة منعكسة على نفسها وهي المشروطة بشرط دوام الموضوع موصوفا بما وصف به. وأما الثاني فإنه يفيد لما أنه من جملة ما يصح الاستدلال به، وقد صح على أحد الطرفين دائما ما لا يصح على الآخر البتة؛ وذلك بالتعاند بينهما لا محالة. وكما أن الاختلاف في الكيفية من الشرائط للإنتاج كما مر فكذلك كلية الكبرى فإنها إذا كانت جزئية فالأكبر الذي حمل على بعض الأوسط قد يكون غير محمول على الأصغر كما يقال كل إنسان حيوان، وبعض الجسم ليس بحيوان فإنه لا ينتج قولنا بعض الإنسان ليس بجسم؛ إذ البعض من الجسم قد يكون حيوانا، وقد لا يكون بل يكون جمادا مثلا . ولو كانت الجزئية هي الصغرى كما يقال بعض الجسم ليس بحيوان وكل إنسان حيوان فإنه ينتج قولنا بعض الجسم ليس بإنسان؛ ولا فساد فيه فإنه يمكن سلب الخاص عن بعض العام وإن كان لا يمكن سلب العام عن بعض الخاص أصلا : إذا عرفت هذا فنقول اشتراط الاختلاف في الكيفية يسقط ثمانية من الضروب واشتراط كلية الكبرى يسقط أربعة أخرى فبقيت المنتجة منها أربعة.
And the difference may be in accidents, or it may not be rather in essential consequences. The first is useless, for compatible and contradictory things share in it. And how not, for it is not impossible with respect to one particular thing with respect to different things. If that is so, reasoning by it is not valid at all; then it follows that this figure would be composed of possible particular and general, existential non-necessary and non-permanent, temporal, distributive, and absolute general premises. In short, it is among those that do not convert in negatives, neither simply nor mixed, and it is well-known that if it is from the two absolute premises, it is productive. But the truth is that it does not produce; rather it produces only if the negative premise is convertible to itself, which is the conditioned with the condition of the permanence of the subject being described by what it is described with. As for the second, it is beneficial because it is among what is valid for reasoning, and it has been established on one of the two sides always what is not established on the other at all; that is by contradiction between them inevitably. And just as difference in quality is among the conditions for productivity as mentioned, so also universality of the major premise; for if it is particular, then the major term which is predicated of some of the middle may not be predicated of the minor, as when it is said: every human is an animal, and some body is not an animal, then it does not produce our statement: some human is not a body; for some of the body may be an animal, or may not be, but be inanimate, for example. And if the particular is the minor, as when it is said: some body is not an animal, and every human is an animal, then it produces our statement: some body is not a human; and there is no fault in it, for it is possible to negate the specific from some of the general, although it is not possible to negate the general from some of the specific at all. If you understand this, we say: the condition of difference in quality eliminates eight of the moods, and the condition of universality of the major premise eliminates four others, so the productive ones among them remain four.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage refines the condition of difference in quality and adds another condition. The author distinguishes two types of difference in quality: accidental (al-'awāriḍ) and essential (al-lawāzim). Accidental difference does not work because both compatible and contradictory subjects can share accidental attributes. If only accidental difference were required, the figure would include many invalid moods from non-convertible propositions. The correct condition is essential difference, based on necessary consequences that hold for one subject but not the other. Additionally, the major premise must be universal. If the major is particular, the major term might not apply to all subjects of the middle, leading to invalid inferences. The author gives an example: 'Every human is an animal, some body is not an animal' does not yield a valid conclusion, whereas if the minor is particular, 'Some body is not an animal, every human is an animal' validly yields 'Some body is not a human.' This is because it is possible to negate a specific term (human) from some of the general (body), but not vice versa. Applying these two conditions—difference in quality (eliminating 8 moods) and universality of the major (eliminating 4 moods)—leaves four productive moods in the second figure.

Key Terms
العوارض
اللوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does accidental difference fail to produce a valid conclusion in the second figure?
A) Because accidental attributes are not convertible.
B) Because both contradictory and compatible subjects share accidental attributes, so the middle term does not distinguish them.
C) Because the major premise must be universal.
D) Because the minor premise must be affirmative.

Key Takeaway
For a second-figure syllogism to be productive, the difference in quality must be in essential consequences (lawāzim), not in accidental attributes.

The Condition of Universality of the Major Premise in the Second Figure
Author stipulates universality of major premise and demonstrates with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the major premise must be universal for a second-figure syllogism to be productive, and identify the four valid moods.

Core Idea
A particular major premise cannot guarantee a valid conclusion because the major term may not apply to all instances of the middle.

In addition to the condition of essential difference, the author emphasizes that the major premise must be universal. Using a concrete example, he shows that a particular major leads to invalidity, while a particular minor can be valid. This understanding allows us to count exactly which moods of the second figure are productive.

Guided Path
1
Locate the condition
Find where the author states 'وكلية الكبرى' (universality of the major premise) and read the example that follows.
2
Analyze the invalid example
The syllogism 'Every human is an animal, some body is not an animal' fails because the major premise (some body is not an animal) is particular. The major term (body) is not predicated of all of the middle (animal).
The attempted conclusion 'some human is not a body' does not follow.
3
Understand the valid example
When the particular premise is the minor (some body is not an animal, every human is an animal), the conclusion 'some body is not a human' is valid. This is because it is possible to negate a specific term from some of the general.
The author notes that it is not possible to negate the general from the specific.
4
Combine with the earlier condition
The condition of universality of the major eliminates 4 moods. Together with the elimination of 8 moods from difference in quality, only 4 productive moods of the second figure remain.
These four are the standard valid moods of the second figure (Cesare, Camestres, Festino, Baroco).

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Conditions for Productivity in the Second Figure
Author states conditions for a productive second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two conditions (difference in quality and universality of major premise) to determine whether a second-figure categorical syllogism is productive.

Core Idea
A productive second-figure syllogism requires premises differing in quality and a universal major premise.

In this passage, the author refines the conditions under which second-figure syllogisms yield necessary conclusions. He emphasizes two key conditions: the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and the major premise must be universal. Without both, no conclusion follows. These conditions are essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic theology, such as proofs for the existence of the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Figure
Confirm the syllogism is in the second figure. In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises, and the terms are arranged as (P-M) and (S-M). The passage discusses how such a figure can be productive only under certain conditions.
The second figure is also called 'figure of restriction' because it typically yields negative conclusions.
2
Check Difference in Quality
Examine the two premises. They must differ in quality: one must be affirmative and the other negative. The passage explicitly states اختلاف في الكيفية (difference in quality) as a condition. Note that this is necessary, not optional.
In the second figure, if both premises are affirmative or both negative, no conclusion follows.
3
Check Universality of the Major Premise
The major premise (the one containing the larger term, i.e., the predicate of the conclusion) must be universal (كلية الكبرى). If it is particular, the syllogism is not productive, as exemplified by the passage: 'Every human is an animal, and some body is not an animal' does not produce a conclusion because the major is particular negative.
The minor premise can be particular or universal; only the major must be universal.
4
Determine Productivity
If both conditions (difference in quality and universal major) are met, the syllogism is productive. If either condition fails, no conclusion can be drawn. The passage notes that these conditions eliminate many possible moods, leaving four productive ones.
Even with these conditions, the specific mood must also be valid (e.g., Camestres, Cesare, Festino, Baroco).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المختلفة. ولو كان كذلك فلا يصح الاستدلال به البتة؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن ينعقد هذا الشكل من الممكنة الخاصة والعامة والوجودية اللا ضرورية واللا دائمة والوقتية والمنتشرة والمطلقة العامة. وبالجملة من جملة ما لا ينعكس في السوالب لا بسيطا ولا مختلطا والمشهور أنه إذا كان من المطلقتين ينعقد منتجا. والحق أنه لا ينتج بل إنما ينتج إذا كانت السالبة منعكسة على نفسها وهي المشروطة بشرط دوام الموضوع موصوفًا بما وصف به. وأما الثاني فإنه يفيد لما أنه من جملة ما يصح الاستدلال به، وقد صح على أحد الطرفين دائما ما لا يصح على الآخر البتة؛ وذلك بالتعاند بينهما لا محالة. وكما أن الاختلاف في الكيفية من الشرائط للإنتاج كما مر فكذلك كلية الكبرى فإنها إذا كانت جزئية فالأكبر الذي حمل على بعض الأوسط قد يكون غير محمول على الأصغر كما يقال كل إنسان حيوان، وبعض الجسم ليس بحيوان فإنه لا ينتج قولنا بعض الإنسان ليس بجسم؛ إذ البعض من الجسم قد يكون حيوانا، وقد لا يكون بل يكون جمادًا مثلا. ولو كانت الجزئية هي الصغرى كما يقال بعض الجسم ليس بحيوان وكل إنسان حيوان فإنه ينتج قولنا بعض الجسم ليس بإنسان؛ ولا فساد فيه فإنه يمكن سلب الخاص عن بعض العام وإن كان لا يمكن سلب العام عن بعض الخاص أصلاً: إذا عرفت هذا فنقول اشتراط الاختلاف في الكيفية يُسقط ثمانية من الضروب واشتراط كلية الكبرى يُسقط أربعة أخرى فبقيت المنتجة منها أربعة.
[The second figure can be formed] from various [propositions]. And if it were so, it would not be valid for inference at all; then it would be necessary that this figure be formed from particular possible, general possible, existential non-necessary, non-perpetual, temporal, spread, and general absolute premises. In summary, from all that does not convert in negatives, neither simple nor mixed. The well-known view is that if it is from two absolute premises, it forms a productive [syllogism]. The truth is that it does not produce, rather it only produces if the negative is self-converting, i.e., the conditional with the condition of the continuity of the subject described as such. As for the second [premise], it is useful because it is among what is valid for inference, and what holds for one of the two extremes always does not hold for the other at all; that is necessarily due to the opposition between them. And just as difference in quality is among the conditions for production, as passed, so is the universality of the major premise; for if it is particular, then the major term predicated of some of the middle may not be predicated of the minor, as when it is said: 'Every human is an animal, and some body is not an animal' – then it does not produce the statement 'Some human is not a body'; because some of the body may be an animal, and may not be, rather be inanimate for example. And if the particular is the minor, as when it is said: 'Some body is not an animal, and every human is an animal' – then it produces: 'Some body is not a human'; there is no defect in it, because it is possible to negate the specific from some of the general, although it is not possible to negate the general from some of the specific at all. If you know this, we say: The condition of difference in quality removes eight of the moods, and the condition of universality of the major removes four others, so the productive ones remaining are four.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions under which the second figure of the categorical syllogism produces a necessary conclusion. The author begins by noting that the second figure is commonly formed from various types of propositions (possible, existential, etc.), but these often do not yield valid inferences because their negatives are not convertible. The well-known view holds that two absolute premises produce a conclusion, but the author corrects this: production only occurs if the negative premise is 'self-converting', i.e., the subject is permanently described by the predicate. The key conditions for productivity are: (1) difference in quality between the two premises (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal. The author illustrates the latter with examples: if the major is particular, no conclusion follows, but if the minor is particular, a valid conclusion may follow (negating the specific from the general is possible). The passage concludes by stating that the quality condition eliminates eight possible moods, and the universality condition eliminates four more, leaving four productive moods. The explanation sets the stage for the detailed enumeration of these moods that follows.

Key Terms
انعكاس (inkisas): Conversion of a proposition; specifically, the property of a negative premise being convertible to an equivalent proposition (e.g., 'no A is B' converts to 'no B is A'). [Note: In earlier logic, 'aks refers to simple conversion; later logicians distinguish between 'aks and 'aks al-naqid (contraposition).]
كلية الكبرى (kulliyyat al-kubra): Universality of the major premise; a necessary condition for a productive second figure syllogism.
اختلاف في الكيفية (ikhtilaf fi al-kayfiyya): Difference in quality between the two premises (one affirmative, one negative), a necessary condition for productivity in the second figure.

Key Takeaway
For a second-figure syllogism to be productive, its premises must differ in quality and its major premise must be universal.

First Productive Mood of the Second Figure (Cesare)
Author presents the first mood: both universal, major negative, producing universal negative, with three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Cesare mood of the second figure and reconstruct its three proofs: conversion, reductio, and necessary concomitant.

Core Idea
The second figure's first mood proves universal negatives via conversion, contradiction, or necessary concomitance.

We now study the first productive mood of the second figure, known as Cesare. This mood uses two universal premises with a negative major to yield a universal negative conclusion. The author provides three distinct methods to prove its validity: by conversion to the first figure, by reductio ad absurdum, and by an argument based on necessary concomitance. Understanding these proofs deepens your grasp of syllogistic validity across figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood components
Recognize that the mood has both premises universal, the major negative, and the conclusion universal negative. Write the form: Every (j) is (b), No (a) is (b), therefore No (j) is (a).
In mnemonics, this is Cesare.
2
Work through the conversion proof
Convert the major premise 'No a is b' to its converse 'No b is a'. Then the syllogism becomes: Every j is b, No b is a, therefore No j is a. This is a valid first-figure mood (Celarent).
Conversion is valid for universal negatives.
3
Work through the reductio proof
Assume the conclusion 'No j is a' is false, so its contradictory 'Some j is a' is true. From that and the major 'No a is b', deduce 'Some j is not b'. But the minor says 'Every j is b' – contradiction. Hence the conclusion must be true.
This shows assuming the opposite leads to impossibility.
4
Grasp the necessary concomitant proof
Since every j is b, j is a necessary concomitant of b. If a is contrary to b (no a is b), then a must be contrary to its concomitant j, so no a is j. This is equivalent to no j is a.
This proof uses the principle that contrariety transfers to necessary concomitants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالضرب الأول من كليتين والكبرى سالبة ينتج سالبة كلية هكذا كل (ج) ب)، ولا شيء من (أب) فلا شيء من (ج) .أ». والبيان فيه إما بعكس الكبرى حتى يرتد إلى الضرب الثاني من الشكل الأول؛ ويتبين لما أن الأول أقرب من الطبع لاشتماله على الترتيب الطبيعي. وإما بالخلف كما يقال لو كذب قولنا لا شيء من ج أ» لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض «ج أ» فنقول بعض «ج» «أ»، ولا شيء من «أ» (ب) فبعض «ج» ليس «ب»؛ وقد كان كل ج « «ب»، هذا خلف. وإما بغيرهما كما يقال إذا صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض «ج أ» فلا يصدق من المقدمتين إحديهما ؛ وذلك لأن الجيم الذي هو «أ»، إما أن لا يكون (ب) وحينئذ لا يمكن صدق الصغرى، وإما أن يكون وحينئذ لا يمكن صدق الكبرى؛ إذ الجيم الذي هو «أ» لا يكون «ب» إلا وأن يكون بعض «أ» «ب». أو يقال إن الشيء لا يكون منافيًا للشيء إلا وأن يكون منافيًا لملزومه لاستحالة وجود الملزوم بدون اللازم؛ فالأكبر فيما نحن فيه وهو «أ» إذا كان منافيا للأوسط وهو «ب» كان منافيا لملزومه وهو «ج» وإلا لا يكون منافيًا للأوسط. وإذا كان منافيا لـ «ج» كان من اللوازم أن يصح قولنا لا شيء من «أ» «ج»؛ وبالعكس.
The first mood [consists of] two universal [premises] with the major negative, producing a universal negative, like: 'Every (j) is (b), and no (a) is (b), therefore no (j) is (a).' The proof for it is either by converting the major so that it reduces to the second mood of the first figure; and it becomes clear because the first figure is more natural due to its containing the natural order. Or by reductio, as when it is said: If our statement 'No (j) is (a)' were false, its contradictory 'Some (j) is (a)' would be true; then we say: 'Some (j) is (a), and no (a) is (b), so some (j) is not (b)'; but it was [given that] 'Every (j) is (b)' – this is a contradiction. Or by another [method], as when it is said: If its contradictory 'Some (j) is (a)' is true, then one of the two premises is not true. This is because the (j) that is (a) either is not (b) – then the minor [premise] cannot be true, or it is (b) – then the major cannot be true; since the (j) that is (a) is not (b) unless some (a) is (b). Or it is said: A thing is not contrary to another thing unless it is contrary to its necessary concomitant, because it is impossible for the concommitant to exist without its necessary concomitant; so the major term in our case – (a) – if it is contrary to the middle term (b), it is contrary to its necessary concomitant (j), otherwise it would not be contrary to the middle. And if it is contrary to (j), then necessarily our statement 'No (a) is (j)' is true, and likewise the converse.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage describes the first productive mood of the second figure: both premises universal, the major negative, yielding a universal negative conclusion (Celarent in the first figure, but here Cesare in the second). The author presents three methods of proof. The first proof is by conversion ('aks) of the major premise, turning the syllogism into the second mood of the first figure, which is known to be valid and more natural. For example, if we have 'Every j is b, no a is b', converting the major gives 'No b is a', then the syllogism becomes: 'Every j is b, no b is a, therefore no j is a'. This is valid. The second proof is by reductio (khalf): assume the conclusion false, so 'Some j is a' is true. From that and the major 'No a is b', we deduce 'Some j is not b', which contradicts the minor 'Every j is b'. The third proof is a direct argument: if the contradictory 'Some j is a' holds, then either that j is b (then the major 'No a is b' fails because some a is b) or it is not b (then the minor 'Every j is b' fails). Alternatively, using the concept of necessary concomitant: if a is contrary to b (i.e., no a is b), then since j necessarily implies b (every j is b), a must be contrary to j as well, yielding the conclusion 'No a is j', which is the converse of the desired 'No j is a'. Thus the mood is valid.

عكس
خلف
ملزوم

Second Mood of the Second Figure: Camestres (AEE)
Author presents the second mood: both universal, minor negative, producing universal negative, with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second mood of the second figure (Camestres) and prove its validity using conversion, reductio, or the concomitant argument.

Core Idea
When the minor premise contradicts the middle term, the minor must also contradict the major.

In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises. The first mood had a universal affirmative minor; now we examine the case where the minor is universal negative. The author provides three distinct proofs that this mood yields a universal negative conclusion. Understanding these proof strategies is essential for constructing and evaluating syllogisms in Islamic sciences, particularly in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood's premises and conclusion
Locate the minor premise (الصغرى, universal negative), major premise (الكبرى, universal affirmative), and conclusion. In the text: 'لا شيء من (ج) ب' and 'كل (أ) ب' yield 'لا شيء من (ج) أ'. Note the terms: j is the minor term, a is the major term, b is the middle term.
The minor is negative; the major is affirmative.
2
Walk through the conversion proof
Convert the minor premise: 'No j is b' becomes 'No b is j'. Then, using the major 'Every a is b', derive a first-figure syllogism: 'No b is j, Every a is b, therefore No a is j'. Finally, convert the result to get the desired conclusion 'No j is a'.
This mirrors the conversion used in the first mood of the second figure.
3
Walk through the reductio proof
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion: 'Some j is a'. From the major 'Every a is b', deduce 'Some j is b'. This contradicts the minor 'No j is b'. Therefore the assumption is false, so the original conclusion holds.
This is a standard proof by contradiction used in logic and theology.
4
Understand the concomitant argument
The author argues: if the minor term (j) contradicts the middle (b), then it must contradict whatever the middle necessarily entails (the major term a). Since 'Every a is b', a is a necessary concomitant of b. Therefore j contradicts a, yielding 'No j is a'.
This reasoning relies on the idea of necessary concomitance (mulazama).
5
Test the mood with an Islamic example
Apply the mood to a kalam example: 'No temporal thing is eternal' (minor, universal negative), 'Every creator is eternal' (major, universal affirmative). The conclusion 'No temporal thing is a creator' follows necessarily.
The example appears in the checkpoint and shows the mood's utility in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضرب الثاني من كليتين والصغرى سالبة ينتج سالبة كلية كقولنا لا شيء من (ج) ب»، وكل (أ ب) فلا شيء من (ج) أ». والبيان فيه إما بعكس الصغرى وجعلها كبرى ثم عكس النتيجة، وإما بالخلف كما مر، وإما بالغير، فإنه إذا لم يصدق قولنا لا شيء من (ج) أو لم يصدق بالصغرى أو الكبرى فإن الجيم الذي هو «أ» إما أن يكون «ب» وحينئذ لا يمكن صدق الصغرى، وإما أن لا يكون وحينئذ لا يمكن صدق الكبرى. ولأن الأصغر إذا نافى الأوسط نافى ملزومه وهو الأكبر فيلزم المنافاة بين (ج) و «أ»؛ وحينئذ يلزم صدق قولنا لا شيء من «ج» «أ».
The second mood [consists of] two universal [premises] with the minor negative, producing a universal negative, like our saying: 'No (j) is (b), and every (a) is (b), therefore no (j) is (a).' The proof for it is either by converting the minor and making it the major, then converting the result; or by reductio as before; or by another [method]: if our statement 'No (j) is (a)' is not true, then either the minor or the major is not true. This is because the (j) that is (a) either is (b) – then the minor cannot be true; or it is not (b) – then the major cannot be true. And because if the minor [term] contradicts the middle, it contradicts its necessary concomitant which is the major, so contradiction is necessary between (j) and (a); therefore our statement 'No (j) is (a)' is necessary.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the second productive mood of the second figure: both premises universal, the minor negative, producing a universal negative conclusion (compare to Camestres in the first figure). The author gives three proofs. The first proof involves converting the minor premise (transposing subject and predicate) and then converting the result (perhaps after rearrangement). For example, from 'No j is b' (minor) and 'Every a is b' (major), convert the minor to 'No b is j'. Then combine with the major to get 'No a is j' via the first figure? Actually, the proof likely reorders to get a valid first figure syllogism. The second proof is reductio: assume 'Some j is a' true; then from the major 'Every a is b', we get 'Some j is b', which contradicts the minor 'No j is b'. The third direct argument: if 'No j is a' is false, then there is a j that is a. That j either is b (contradicts minor) or is not b (contradicts major). Alternatively, using concomitant: the minor term (j) contradicts the middle (b), so it must contradict the middle's necessary concomitant (a, because every a is b), leading to the conclusion 'No j is a'. Thus the mood is valid.

Key Terms
صغرى
كبرى

Key Takeaway
The second figure's second mood (Camestres) has a universal affirmative major and universal negative minor, yielding a universal negative conclusion, proven by conversion, reductio, and concomitant reasoning.

Third Mood of the Second Figure
Author presents the third mood: minor affirmative particular, major universal negative, producing particular negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and validate the third mood of the second figure using conversion or reductio.

Core Idea
The third mood of the second figure yields a particular negative from a particular affirmative and a universal negative.

The author introduces the third productive mood of the second figure. The minor premise is a particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some j is b'), the major premise is a universal negative (e.g., 'No a is b'), and the conclusion is a particular negative ('Some j is not a'). This mood can be proven by converting the major premise and reducing it to a first-figure mood (Ferio).

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood components
Locate the minor premise (particular affirmative) and the major premise (universal negative) in the Arabic. Note the middle term: it is the predicate in both premises, confirming the second figure.
The conclusion is always particular negative when the minor is particular and the major is universal negative.
2
Convert the major premise
Convert the universal negative 'No a is b' to 'No b is a' (simple conversion). This yields a first-figure mood with the same minor: 'Some j is b' and 'No b is a'.
Simple conversion of a universal negative is valid and preserves truth.
3
Reduce to first figure
The resulting syllogism is Ferio: 'Some j is b, no b is a, therefore some j is not a'. This is valid in the first figure, proving the original mood is valid.
The mood name Festino in medieval logic corresponds to this reduction.
4
Verify by reductio ad absurdum
Assume the conclusion is false (all j is a). Combine with the minor 'Some j is b' to get a contradiction with the major 'No a is b'. Thus the conclusion must be true.
This method directly proves validity without conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث من موجبة جزئية كبراها سالبة كلية ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا بعض «ج» «ب» ولا شيء من «أ» (ب) فليس كل «ج أ» لما مر من طريقي العكس والخلف وغيرهما.
The third [mood is] from an affirmative particular [minor] with its major universal negative, producing a particular negative, like our saying: 'Some (j) is (b), and no (a) is (b), therefore not every (j) is (a)' – according to what has passed of the two methods of conversion and reductio and others.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the third productive mood of the second figure: the minor is a particular affirmative (some j is b), the major is a universal negative (no a is b), and the conclusion is a particular negative (not every j is a, i.e., some j is not a). This corresponds to the mood Festino in the first figure. The author does not detail the proofs here but refers to the same two methods used earlier: conversion (of the major, then reduction to a first figure mood like Ferio) and reductio ad absurdum. For example, by converting the major 'No a is b' to 'No b is a', the syllogism becomes: 'Some j is b, no b is a, therefore some j is not a', which is valid in the first figure. This mood is valid, and the author assumes the student can apply the same techniques. The conclusion is particular negative because the minor is particular; a universal conclusion cannot follow from a particular premise.

The Syllogism

First Mood of the Second Figure: Three Proofs
Author proves first mood of second figure with multiple methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a reductio proof for a second-figure syllogism and identify the logical relationship between the major term and the middle term's necessary concomitant.

Core Idea
The second figure proves negativity by showing that the middle term separates the extremes.

The first mood of the second figure has a universal negative conclusion. The author presents three distinct methods to prove its validity: reductio ad absurdum, analysis of contradictory assumption, and the relation of necessary concomitant. Each method illuminates a different logical property of the syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood components
Note the three terms: J (minor), B (middle), A (major). The premises are: Every J is B (universal affirmative minor) and No A is B (universal negative major). The conclusion is: No J is A (universal negative).
This is Camestres in traditional terminology.
2
Follow the reductio proof
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion: Some J is A. Combine with the premises: from Some J is A and No A is B, infer Some J is not B. But the minor premise says Every J is B, so we get a contradiction. Therefore the assumption is false, and the conclusion holds.
This is proof by contradiction (khalf).
3
Analyze the non-conversion method
Assume Some J is A is true. Then consider that J which is A. If it is not B, then the minor premise 'Every J is B' is false. If it is B, then some A is B, contradicting the major premise 'No A is B'. Thus the assumption leads to a false premise.
This proof does not rely on conversion or reductio; it directly shows the impossibility of the contradictory.
4
Understand the necessary concomitant method
Recognize that J is the necessary concomitant (malzūm) of B: if B exists, J necessarily exists. Since A is contrary to B (No A is B), A must also be contrary to J. Therefore No A is J, which is equivalent to No J is A (conversion of universal negative).
This method uses the relation of necessary condition (lāzim) and necessary concomitant (malzūm).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا شيء من ج أ» لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض «ج أ» فنقول بعض «ج» «أ»، ولا شيء من «أ» (ب) فبعض «ج» ليس «ب»؛ وقد كان كل ج « «ب»، هذا خلف. وإما بغيرهما كما يقال إذا صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض «ج أ» فلا يصدق من المقدمتين إحديهما ؛ وذلك لأن الجيم الذي هو «أ»، إما أن لا يكون (ب) وحينئذ لا يمكن صدق الصغرى، وإما أن يكون وحينئذ لا يمكن صدق الكبرى؛ إذ الجيم الذي هو «أ» لا يكون «ب» إلا وأن يكون بعض «أ» «ب». أو يقال إن الشيء لا يكون منافيًا للشيء إلا وأن يكون منافيًا لملزومه لاستحالة وجود الملزوم بدون اللازم؛ فالأكبر فيما نحن فيه وهو «أ» إذا كان منافيا للأوسط وهو «ب» كان منافيا لملزومه وهو «ج» وإلا لا يكون منافيًا للأوسط. وإذا كان منافيا لـ «ج» كان من اللوازم أن يصح قولنا لا شيء من «أ» «ج»؛ وبالعكس.
No J is A (is true) because its contradictory, our statement 'Some J is A', is false. So we say 'Some J is A' and 'No A is B' yields 'Some J is not B'; but it was the case that 'Every J is B'. This is a contradiction. And alternatively, by other than these two [conversion and reductio], as is said: if its contradictory, our statement 'Some J is A', is true, then one of the two premises is false. That is because the J that is A: either it is not B, and then the minor premise cannot be true; or it is B, and then the major premise cannot be true, because the J that is A is not B except if some A is B. Or it is said: a thing is not contrary to a thing except if it is contrary to its necessary concomitant, because it is impossible for the necessary concomitant to exist without the necessary condition. So the major term, which is A, if it is contrary to the middle term, which is B, then it is contrary to its necessary concomitant, which is J; otherwise, it is not contrary to the middle term. And if it is contrary to J, then it is necessary that our statement 'No A is J' is true; and conversely.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage proves the validity of the first mood of the second figure. The author presents three proofs: (1) reductio ad absurdum (خلف): assume the contradictory of the conclusion ('Some J is A'), combine with the premises 'Every J is B' and 'No A is B', derive 'Some J is not B', which contradicts 'Every J is B'. (2) Non-conversion method (غير): if the contradictory 'Some J is A' were true, then either that particular J (which is A) is not B (making the minor premise false) or it is B (making the major premise false, because then some A would be B, contradicting 'No A is B'). (3) The method of necessary concomitant (ملزوم): the major term A, if contrary to the middle B, must be contrary to B's necessary concomitant J (since J is B's necessary concomitant), yielding 'No A is J'.

Key Terms
الضرب الأول
نقيض
خلف
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Every intoxicant is halal

C) Some intoxicant is halal
D) No halal thing is intoxicant

Key Takeaway
The first mood of the second figure (Camestres) is valid and can be proven by reductio, non-conversion, or the necessary concomitant method.

Identifying the Fourth Mood and Its Conversion Problem
Author explains why conversion fails for this mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth mood of the second figure and explain why conversion cannot prove it.

Core Idea
The fourth mood refuses conversion because its minor premise is particular negative.

The second figure has four moods, and the fourth is the trickiest: its minor is a particular negative and its major a universal affirmative. Though the conclusion is valid, you cannot prove it by simply converting premises. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
From the passage, the minor is 'Not every J is B' (particular negative), major is 'Every A is B' (universal affirmative), conclusion is 'Not every J is A' (particular negative).
These are the defining features of this mood.
2
Check conversion of the minor
The minor 'Some J is not B' does not convert validly. Converting it would be invalid in Aristotelian logic.
Thus we cannot use the minor in a new syllogism after conversion.
3
Check conversion of the major
The major 'Every A is B' converts to 'Some B is A' (particular affirmative). Together with the unconverted minor we have two particular premises.
Two particular premises produce no valid syllogistic conclusion.
4
Understand why reduction fails
The usual method of reducing to the first figure by converting one premise fails here because the conversion of the major yields a particular, leading to two particulars.
This forces us to use other proof methods: reductio or iqtiran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع من سالبة جزئية كبراها موجبة كلية ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا ليس كل ج ب ، وكل «أب» فليس كل (ج) أ»، وهذا من جملة ما لا يمكن بیانه بالعكس؛ إذ صغراه لا تنعكس وعكس كبراه جزئية؛ ولا قياس عن جزئيتين. فلا فائدة في الرد إلى الأول فإذن بيانه إما بالخلف كما مر ، وإما بالاقتران فيعرض ذلك البعض كلا؛ ونزل منزلة الضرب الثاني من هذا الشكل كما إذا فرض الجيم الذي ليس بد: ثمَّ يقال لا شيء من دب»، وكل «أب» فلا شيء من «د أ». وبعد هذا يقال بعض اج دا ، ولا شيء من «د أ» فليس كل ج .أ» . وإما بالغير من وجوه البيان وهو الذي مرَّ ذكره في الضرب الأول.
The fourth [mood consists of] a particular negative [minor] and its major a universal affirmative; it yields a particular negative, as in our statement: 'Not every J is B' (i.e., some J is not B), and 'Every A is B', so 'Not every J is A' (i.e., some J is not A). This is among those that cannot be demonstrated by conversion, because its minor cannot be converted, and the conversion of its major yields a particular [proposition]; and there is no syllogism from two particulars. So there is no benefit in reducing it to the first [figure]. Thus its demonstration is either by reductio as previously, or by coupling (iqtirān) in that we consider that part as universal; and we treat it like the second mood of this figure, as when we assume the J that is not B: then we say 'No D is B', and 'Every A is B', so 'No D is A'. After this we say 'Some J is D', and 'No D is A', so 'Not every J is A'. Or by the other method which was mentioned in the first mood.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage describes the fourth mood of the second figure: minor 'Some J is not B' (particular negative), major 'Every A is B' (universal affirmative), conclusion 'Some J is not A' (particular negative). The author notes that conversion does not work for this mood because the minor (particular negative) does not convert validly, and converting the major gives a particular affirmative, and two particular premises produce no conclusion. So we cannot reduce to the first figure by simple conversion. Instead, proof is by reductio: assume 'Every J is A', then with major 'Every A is B' we get 'Every J is B', contradicting 'Some J is not B'. Alternatively, by a method called 'اقتران' (coupling): we take the subset of J that is not B, call it D, then treat it as a universal negative minor: 'No D is B' and 'Every A is B' gives 'No D is A'; then we combine 'Some J is D' (since some J is not B) with 'No D is A' to get 'Some J is not A' (since some J is D and D is not A). This effectively reduces to the second mood of the second figure. Finally, the 'other' method from the first mood can also be applied.

Key Terms
سالبة جزئية
اقتران

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The major premise is particular negative

Key Takeaway
The fourth mood's minor cannot convert, and the converted major becomes particular, so conversion fails; alternative proofs are needed.

Proving the Fourth Mood by Reductio ad Absurdum
Author mentions reductio as one proof method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a reductio ad absurdum proof for the fourth mood of the second figure.

Core Idea
Reductio shows that assuming the opposite of the conclusion leads to contradiction with the premises.

Since conversion fails, one way to prove the fourth mood is reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf). The author mentions this method compactly: 'as previously'. Let's unpack how it works for this mood.

Guided Path
1
State the original premises and conclusion
Minor: Some J is not B. Major: Every A is B. Desired conclusion: Some J is not A.
We need to show this conclusion is necessary.
2
Assume the contrary of the conclusion
Assume that the conclusion is false: i.e., assume 'Every J is A' (the contradictory of 'Some J is not A').
This is the standard starting point of a reductio.
3
Combine the assumption with the major premise
From 'Every J is A' (assumption) and 'Every A is B' (major), we derive by Barbara (first figure) that 'Every J is B'.
This is a valid categorical syllogism.
4
Reach a contradiction
The derived 'Every J is B' directly contradicts the minor premise 'Some J is not B'. Hence the assumption is false, so the original conclusion must be true.
The contradiction is immediate, proving the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع من سالبة جزئية كبراها موجبة كلية ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا ليس كل ج ب ، وكل «أب» فليس كل (ج) أ»، وهذا من جملة ما لا يمكن بیانه بالعكس؛ إذ صغراه لا تنعكس وعكس كبراه جزئية؛ ولا قياس عن جزئيتين. فلا فائدة في الرد إلى الأول فإذن بيانه إما بالخلف كما مر ، وإما بالاقتران فيعرض ذلك البعض كلا؛ ونزل منزلة الضرب الثاني من هذا الشكل كما إذا فرض الجيم الذي ليس بد: ثمَّ يقال لا شيء من دب»، وكل «أب» فلا شيء من «د أ». وبعد هذا يقال بعض اج دا ، ولا شيء من «د أ» فليس كل ج .أ» . وإما بالغير من وجوه البيان وهو الذي مرَّ ذكره في الضرب الأول.
The fourth [mood consists of] a particular negative [minor] and its major a universal affirmative; it yields a particular negative, as in our statement: 'Not every J is B' (i.e., some J is not B), and 'Every A is B', so 'Not every J is A' (i.e., some J is not A). This is among those that cannot be demonstrated by conversion, because its minor cannot be converted, and the conversion of its major yields a particular [proposition]; and there is no syllogism from two particulars. So there is no benefit in reducing it to the first [figure]. Thus its demonstration is either by reductio as previously, or by coupling (iqtirān) in that we consider that part as universal; and we treat it like the second mood of this figure, as when we assume the J that is not B: then we say 'No D is B', and 'Every A is B', so 'No D is A'. Commentary
This passage describes the fourth mood of the second figure: minor 'Some J is not B' (particular negative), major 'Every A is B' (universal affirmative), conclusion 'Some J is not A' (particular negative). The author notes that conversion does not work for this mood because the minor (particular negative) does not convert validly, and converting the major gives a particular affirmative, and two particular premises produce no conclusion. Proving the Fourth Mood by the Iqtiran (Coupling) Method
Author explains the iqtiran method step by step.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the iqtiran method to prove the fourth mood of the second figure.

Core Idea
Iqtiran isolates the 'offending' part of the term to create a universal minor premise.

Another method mentioned by the author is 'iqtiran' or coupling. Here we introduce a new term D representing the subset of J that is not B. This turns the particular negative minor into a universal negative, enabling a valid syllogism in the second figure.

Guided Path
1
Define the new term D
From the minor 'Some J is not B', we consider the subset of J that is not B. Call this subset D. So 'All D are J' and 'No D is B'.
D is a universal concept representing those J that are not B.
2
Form the first syllogism
We have 'No D is B' (universal negative) and 'Every A is B' (universal affirmative). In the second figure, this yields 'No D is A' (universal negative).
This is the second mood of the second figure (universal negative minor, universal affirmative major).
3
Form the second syllogism
We have 'Some J is D' (from the definition of D) and 'No D is A' (from step 2). In the first figure (Ferio), we get 'Some J is not A'.
This is the desired conclusion.
4
Combine the steps
The iqtiran method essentially breaks the proof into two simple syllogisms: first derive 'No D is A', then derive 'Some J is not A'.
This method avoids conversion and uses only valid moods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع من سالبة جزئية كبراها موجبة كلية ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا ليس كل ج ب ، وكل «أب» فليس كل (ج) أ»، وهذا من جملة ما لا يمكن بیانه بالعكس؛ إذ صغراه لا تنعكس وعكس كبراه جزئية؛ ولا قياس عن جزئيتين. فلا فائدة في الرد إلى الأول فإذن بيانه إما بالخلف كما مر ، وإما بالاقتران فيعرض ذلك البعض كلا؛ ونزل منزلة الضرب الثاني من هذا الشكل كما إذا فرض الجيم الذي ليس بد: ثمَّ يقال لا شيء من دب»، وكل «أب» فلا شيء من «د أ». وبعد هذا يقال بعض اج دا ، ولا شيء من «د أ» فليس كل ج .أ» . وإما بالغير من وجوه البيان وهو الذي مرَّ ذكره في الضرب الأول.
The fourth [mood consists of] a particular negative [minor] and its major a universal affirmative; it yields a particular negative, as in our statement: 'Not every J is B' (i.e., some J is not B), and 'Every A is B', so 'Not every J is A' (i.e., some J is not A). Imaginary Moods in the Second Figure: Reduction to the First
Author demonstrates an extra claimed mood and explains why it is imaginary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an imaginary mood in the second figure and reduce it to a genuine mood in the first figure using conversion.

Core Idea
Not every valid second-figure syllogism is a distinct mood; only four are genuine.

Some logicians claimed the second figure has more than four productive moods. In this passage, the author examines one such claim: a mood with universal negative minor and particular affirmative major. He shows that this 'mood' reduces to the first figure after conversion. Thus it is not a distinct mood of the second figure but an imaginary one. Understanding this teaches us how to test the genuineness of syllogistic moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed mood
Find the premises and conclusion in the author's example: 'No J is B' (universal negative), 'Some A is B' (particular affirmative), therefore 'Some J is not A' (particular negative). This arrangement matches the second figure (middle term B is predicate in both premises).
The mood would be EIO in the second figure if genuine.
2
Check if the conclusion follows directly
In the second figure, the only way to get a valid conclusion is with a universal major premise. Here the major (Some A is B) is particular, which does not satisfy the conditions for a genuine second-figure mood. So the syllogism cannot be valid as is.
The second figure requires the major premise to be universal and negative for a negative conclusion.
3
Reduce to the first figure by conversion
Convert the major premise 'Some A is B' to 'Some B is A' (valid for particular affirmative). This gives: 'No J is B' (minor), 'Some B is A' (major). Now the terms are arranged for the first figure (middle term B is subject of the major and predicate of the minor). This is the first-figure mood Ferio (EIO).
Conversion preserves truth for particular affirmative.
4
Identify the genuine mood in the first figure
The reduced syllogism 'No J is B, Some B is A, therefore Some J is not A' is a valid first-figure mood (Ferio). The original mood is not a distinct second-figure mood because it reduces to the first figure via conversion.
Imaginary moods are those that appear productive but are reducible to other figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي زعم في الشكل الأول أن المنتج منه لا يكون منحصرا في الأربعة من الضروب فقد زعم في هذا الشكل أيضًا؛ وذلك لأن السالبة الكلية إذا كانت صغرى الموجبة الجزئية ينتج سالبة جزئية كما لا يقال لا شيء من «ج ب»، وبعض «أب» ينتج ليس بعض (ج) أ» بعكس الكبرى والرد إلى الأول. فإنه إذا ارتد إلى الأول وذلك أن يقال لا شيء من (ج) ب)، وبعض ب أ) ينتج ليس بعض ج أ لما مرَّ في الشكل الأول. غير أنه من الوهميات في هذا الشكل؛ وفي ذلك كذلك. وقد مرَّ الكلام فيه فلا يمكن أن ينتج من الضروب إلا الأربعة المذكورة.
Then what was claimed in the first figure that the productive moods are not limited to four, they also claimed in this figure similarly. That is because if the universal negative is minor and the particular affirmative is major, it produces a particular negative, as in 'No J is B' and 'Some A is B' yields 'Not some J is A' (i.e., some J is not A) by converting the major and reducing to the first figure. For when reduced to the first, it becomes: 'No J is B' and 'Some B is A' yields 'Some J is not A' as was mentioned for the first figure. However, it is among the imaginary moods in this figure; and similarly in that figure. And the discussion about it has passed; so only the four mentioned moods can produce [a conclusion].
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage addresses the claim that the second figure may have more than four productive moods, just as some claimed for the first figure. The author gives an example: universal negative as minor (No J is B) and particular affirmative as major (Some A is B) purportedly yields a particular negative (Some J is not A). The proof would involve converting the major 'Some A is B' to 'Some B is A' (valid by conversion of particular affirmative) and then using the first figure: 'No J is B' and 'Some B is A' yields 'Some J is not A' (the fourth mood of the first figure, Ferio). However, the author dismisses this as an 'imaginary' (وهمي) mood in the second figure, because it reduces to the first figure and is not genuinely a distinct mood of the second figure. Therefore, only the four moods previously listed are considered valid in the second figure.

Key Terms
وهميات (wahmiyyāt) — imaginary moods
سالبة كلية (universal negative)
موجبة جزئية (particular affirmative)
سالبة جزئية (particular negative)
عكس (conversion)
رد إلى الأول (reduction to the first figure)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The major premise is universal

Key Takeaway
A syllogism with a particular affirmative major in the second figure reduces to the first figure, making it an imaginary mood rather than a distinct second-figure mood.

Variations of the First Mood in the Second Figure
Variation of the four moods by term scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two variations of the first mood of the second figure based on the scope of the middle term relative to the minor.

Core Idea
The middle term's scope relative to the minor defines distinct valid forms of the first mood.

In the previous lesson, we learned about the four moods of the second figure. Now, the author deepens our understanding by showing that each mood is not monolithic; it can appear in different forms depending on whether the middle term is more general than, equal to, or more specific than the terms it relates to. For the first mood, he specifies two possible forms based on the relation between the middle and minor terms.

Guided Path
1
Recall the first mood of the second figure (Cesare)
The first mood has a universal negative conclusion. Its premises are: major premise 'No P is M' (universal negative) and minor premise 'All S is M' (universal affirmative).
Here M is the middle term, S the minor, P the major.
2
Focus on the relation between middle and minor
In the minor premise 'All S is M', the middle term M can be either more general than the minor S (S is a subset of M) or equal to S (coextensive).
The relation is determined by the real extension of the terms in the subject matter.
3
Identify the two variations
Variation 1: M is more general than S. Variation 2: M is equal to S (musāwāh). These are the only possibilities for a true universal affirmative minor premise.
The major premise remains unchanged; the major and middle are always mutually exclusive (mubāyana) in this mood.
4
Why no third variation?
If M were more specific than S, the minor premise 'All S is M' would be false (since S would extend beyond M). So only these two variations are valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كل واحد من هذه الضروب الأربعة يقع على وجوه مختلفة بحسب اختلاف الأحوال في حدودها من العموم والخصوص والمساواة وما ليس بواحد منها [أ] أما الضرب الأول فالأوسط فيه إما أن يكون أعم بالنسبة إلى الأصغر، وإما أن لا يكون بل يكون مساويًا له. وهذا بالنسبة إلى الأصغر لا غير؛ إذ بينه وبين الأكبر مباينة فيقع هذا الضرب إذن على وجهين. [ب] وكذلك الضرب الثاني من الضروب الأربعة، فإن نسبة الأوسط إلى الأكبر فيه كنسبة الأوسط إلى الأصغر في الأول على خلاف [ت] الضرب الثالث فإنه
Then each of these four moods occurs in different ways according to the different states of their terms with respect to generality, particularity, equality, and what is none of these. [A] As for the first mood, the middle term in it is either more general than the minor term, or not, but equal to it. This is only with respect to the minor term, since between it and the major there is contrariety (mubāyana). So this mood occurs in two ways. [B] Similarly, the second mood among the four: the relation of the middle to the major in it is like the relation of the middle to the minor in the first, but opposite. [T] The third mood: ...
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage examines the four moods of the second figure in terms of the logical relations between terms. The author notes that each mood can have variations based on whether the middle term is coextensive with, broader than, or narrower than the subject/predicate (excluding the case of contrariety between the extremes). For the first mood (universal negative conclusion), the middle term (B) can be either more general than the minor term (J) or equal to it. The middle and major (A) are always contraries (mubāyana) in this mood, so no variation there. Thus the first mood has two forms. The second mood is analogous but with the middle's relation to the major instead of the minor. The text begins to discuss the third mood but is cut off. These distinctions are important for understanding how the same mood can be instantiated in different logical contexts, affecting its validity or productivity.
