Variations of Second-Figure Moods: Term Relations, Quality, and Complexity
Author enumerates the many variations of each mood based on term relations, negation, and complexity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given second-figure syllogism according to its wujuh dimensions (term relation, negation/affirmation, and term complexity) and compute the total number of valid variations for each mood.

Core Idea
Each mood in the second figure is a family of variations defined by term relations, quality, and complexity.

In this passage, the author unpacks the combinatorial richness behind the four moods of the second figure. Understanding these dimensions will help you recognise the diverse actual forms a mood can take, which is critical when analyzing real syllogisms in Islamic logic. You will learn to parse the author's enumeration and apply it to concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four moods
The passage refers to the four moods of the second figure as 'first mood', 'second mood', etc. In standard terminology, these are Cesare, Camestres, Festino, and Baroco. Note that the author describes them generically without naming them.
The second figure moods are defined by the middle term being the predicate of both premises.
2
Extract term relations for each mood
For each mood, determine how the middle term relates to the minor or major in generality/particularity. For mood 1, the middle is either more general than the minor or equal to it (2 ways). For mood 2, the middle relates to the major similarly (2 ways). For mood 3, the middle relative to the minor can be more general, more specific, equal, or none of these (4 ways). Mood 4 follows the pattern of mood 3 but relative to the major (4 ways).
The author treats these relations as the first dimension of wujuh.
3
Analyze the negation/affirmation dimension
Each premise can be affirmative, privative of subject, privative of predicate, or privative of both (4 types per premise). This yields 16 combinations per mood, but only 8 are valid per the rules of the first figure (the author invokes a prior result). So each mood has 8 valid wujuh from this dimension.
The valid combinations correspond to those that respect the distribution conditions of the second figure.
4
Add the term complexity dimension
Each premise can be two-term (simple subject-predicate) or three-term (complex, e.g., with an embedded middle). This gives 4 possibilities per mood: both simple, both complex, minor simple major complex, minor complex major simple.
The author calls this 'thunaiyya wa thulathiyya'.
5
Compute the total aspects per mood
Multiply the counts from the three dimensions: for mood 1 and 2: 2 (term relations) × 8 (valid negation/affirmation) × 4 (complexity) = 64. The author gives 68, which is slightly higher (perhaps including additional possibilities or an extra count). For mood 3 and 4: 4 × 8 × 4 = 128, which matches the author's 'double' of 64. Note the discrepancy for mood 1/2 as a point of caution.
The author then multiplies across all simple and mixed syllogisms, leading to even larger numbers.

Source Text

ثم كل واحد من هذه الضروب الأربعة يقع على وجوه مختلفة بحسب اختلاف الأحوال في حدودها من العموم والخصوص والمساواة وما ليس بواحد منها [أ] أما الضرب الأول فالأوسط فيه إما أن يكون أعم بالنسبة إلى الأصغر، وإما أن لا يكون بل يكون مساويًا له. وهذا بالنسبة إلى الأصغر لا غير؛ إذ بينه وبين الأكبر مباينة فيقع هذا الضرب إذن على وجهين. [ب] وكذلك الضرب الثاني من الضروب الأربعة، فإن نسبة الأوسط إلى الأكبر فيه كنسبة الأوسط إلى الأصغر في الأول على خلاف [ت] الضرب الثالث فإنه يقع فيه على أربعة أوجه لما أن الأوسط بالنسبة إلى الأصغر قد يكون أعم من الأصغر، وقد يكون أخص منه، وقد يكون مساويًا، وقد يكون لا أعم ولا أخص ولا مساويًا. [د] وكذلك في الرابع منها فإن نسبة الأوسط إلى الأكبر فيه كنسبة الأوسط إلى الأصغر في الثالث، هذا بالنسبة إلى العموم والخصوص. فأما بالنسبة إلى العدول والتحصيل فالوجوه الواقعة في كل ضرب منها ستة عشر ثمانية منها ساقطة، وثمانية باقية كما في الشكل الأول؛ إذ كل واحدة من الصغرى والكبرى قد تكون محصلة، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع، وقد تكون معدولة المحمول، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع والمحمول. وكما أن كل واحد منها متعدد بحسب العموم والخصوص والعدول والتحصيل فكذلك بحسب الغير وذلك أن تكون كل واحدة من الصغرى والكبرى في كل ضرب من الضروب، إما أن تكون ثنائية، أو ثلاثية، أو الصغرى ثنائية دون الكبرى، أو بالعكس. فيكون كل ضرب منها على أربعة أوجه فیضرب ما يكون واقعا من الوجوه في كل ضرب بحسب العموم والخصوص في الثمانية الباقية التي هي بحسب العدول والتحصيل. والمبلغ في هذه الأربعة كذلك حتى يحصل من الوجوه في الضرب الأول مبلغ ثمانية وستين، وكذلك في الضرب الثاني، وفي الثالث ضعف هذا المبلغ، وكذلك في الرابع. ثم إذا ضربت هذه الوجوه في جميع الأقيسة البسيطة والمختلطة بلغت مبلغ أضعاف هذه المبالغ.
Then each of these four moods occurs in various ways according to the different states of their terms regarding generality, particularity, equality, and what is not one of them. [A] As for the first mood, the middle term is either more general than the minor or equal to it. This is only with respect to the minor, since between it and the major there is a distinct difference. So this mood occurs in two ways. [B] Similarly, the second mood of the four, the relation of the middle to the major is like the relation of the middle to the minor in the first, but on the contrary. [C] The third mood occurs in four ways: because the middle term with respect to the minor may be more general than the minor, or more specific than it, or equal, or neither more general, nor more specific, nor equal. [D] Similarly in the fourth mood, the relation of the middle to the major is like the relation of the middle to the minor in the third. This is with respect to generality and particularity. As for negation and affirmation, the aspects occurring in each mood are sixteen, eight of which are invalid and eight valid, as in the first figure: because each of the minor and major may be affirmative, or privative of subject, or privative of predicate, or privative of both subject and predicate. And just as each of them is multiple according to generality, particularity, negation, and affirmation, so too according to the other: that each of the minor and major in each mood is either two-term or three-term, or the minor is two-term but not the major, or vice versa. So each mood occurs in four aspects. Then multiply the aspects that occur in each mood according to generality and particularity by the eight remaining aspects which are according to negation and affirmation. The sum for these four is such that the aspects in the first mood amount to sixty-eight, and likewise in the second, and in the third double this amount, and likewise in the fourth. Then if these aspects are multiplied across all simple and mixed syllogisms, the amount reaches multiples of these sums.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage classifies the different possible forms (wujuh) of each mood in the second figure. The author begins by noting that each mood can vary based on the relation of the middle term to the minor or major (generality, particularity, equality, or none). For the first mood, the middle is either more general than the minor or equal to it, giving 2 wujuh. For the second, similarly but with the major instead, also 2. For the third, the middle relative to the minor can be one of four possibilities: more general, more specific, equal, or unrelated, giving 4 wujuh. The fourth follows the third pattern. Next, the author considers negation and affirmation ('udul wa tahsil): each premise can be affirmative, privative-of-subject, privative-of-predicate, or privative-of-both. This yields 4×4 = 16 combinations, of which half (8) are valid per the rules of the first figure. So each mood has 8 valid wujuh from this perspective. Then a further dimension is whether each premise is two-term (simple subject-predicate) or three-term (complex). Each premise can be two-term or three-term, and the opposition of the two gives 4 possibilities per mood. To get the total number of wujuh per mood, the author multiplies the wujuh from generality (2 or 4) by the 8 valid wujuh from negation/affirmation, obtaining 16 for first and second, 32 for third and fourth. But then he multiplies by the 4 from the two-term/three-term dimension, yielding 64 for first and second, 128 for third and fourth. However, he states that the first mood has 68, which is a discrepancy (perhaps due to additional possibilities or a count from all three dimensions). He says second has same, third double, fourth same as third. Then he further multiplies across all simple and mixed syllogisms, yielding even larger numbers. This detailed enumeration shows the combinatorial complexity of syllogistic forms, though the logic remains focused on the four basic moods.

Key Terms
وجوه (wujuh): Aspects or variations of a syllogistic mood based on the relations of terms, quality of premises, and their complexity. [Note: Used broadly in logic for various classifications.]
عدول و تحصيل ('udul wa tahsil): Negation and affirmation; refers to whether a proposition is affirmative or privative (with negation on subject or predicate). [Note: Standard distinction in categorical propositions.]
ثنائية و ثلاثية (thunaiyya wa thulathiyya): Two-term (simple subject-predicate) vs three-term (complex, e.g., with a middle term embedded) premises. [Note: Less common classification; may refer to the number of distinct terms in the premise.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The second-figure moods are not monolithic; they admit multiple valid variations based on term relations, quality, and complexity, which a logician must master.

The Four Relations of the Middle Term in the Third Figure
Author introduces the four possible relations of the middle term to the minor in the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the relationship between the middle term and the minor term in the third figure as more general, more specific, equal, or neither.

Core Idea
The middle term's relation to the minor determines the figure's potential moods.

In the third figure, the middle term can relate to the minor in four distinct ways. Understanding these relations is key to identifying which syllogistic moods are possible. This lesson will guide you through the taxonomy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle and minor terms
In a third-figure syllogism, the middle term appears as the subject of both premises, and the minor term is the subject of the conclusion. Locate these terms in a given syllogism.
2
Compare the extensions of the middle and minor
Consider the logical scope: does the middle term include the minor entirely, partially, or not at all? Determine if the middle term is more general, more specific, equal, or incomparable.
Equal means the terms are coextensive. Incomparable means neither is included in the other.
3
Classify the relationship
Assign one of the four categories: more general (a'am), more specific (akhass), equal (musawi), or none (la a'am wa la akhass wa la musawi).
This classification is used to enumerate possible moods.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take a real syllogism from kalam or fiqh and repeat the classification.
For instance, in 'every intoxicant is forbidden; some intoxicant is khamr; therefore some khamr is forbidden', the middle 'intoxicant' is more general than the minor 'khamr'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقع فيه على أربعة أوجه لما أن الأوسط بالنسبة إلى الأصغر قد يكون أعم من الأصغر، وقد يكون أخص منه، وقد يكون مساويًا، وقد يكون لا أعم ولا أخص ولا مساويًا. وكذلك في الرابع منها فإن نسبة الأوسط إلى الأكبر فيه كنسبة الأوسط إلى الأصغر في الثالث، هذا بالنسبة إلى العموم والخصوص. فأما بالنسبة إلى العدول والتحصيل فالوجوه الواقعة في كل ضرب منها ستة عشر ثمانية منها ساقطة، وثمانية باقية كما في الشكل الأول؛ إذ كل واحدة من الصغرى والكبرى قد تكون محصلة، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع، وقد تكون معدولة المحمول، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع والمحمول. وكما أن كل واحد منها متعدد بحسب العموم والخصوص والعدول والتحصيل فكذلك بحسب الغير وذلك أن تكون كل واحدة من الصغرى والكبرى في كل ضرب من الضروب، إما أن تكون ثنائية، أو ثلاثية، أو الصغرى ثنائية دون الكبرى، أو بالعكس. فيكون كل ضرب منها على أربعة أوجه فيضرب ما يكون واقعا من الوجوه في كل ضرب بحسب العموم والخصوص في الثمانية الباقية التي هي بحسب العدول والتحصيل. والمبلغ في هذه الأربعة كذلك حتى يحصل من الوجوه في الضرب الأول مبلغ ثمانية وستين، وكذلك في الضرب الثاني، وفي الثالث ضعف هذا المبلغ، وكذلك في الرابع. ثم إذا ضربت هذه الوجوه في جميع الأقيسة البسيطة والمختلطة بلغت مبلغ أضعاف هذه المبالغ.
It occurs in four ways because the middle term relative to the minor term may be more general than the minor, or more specific than it, or equal to it, or neither more general nor more specific nor equal. Likewise in the fourth [figure], the relation of the middle to the major in it is like the relation of the middle to the minor in the third, this with respect to generality and specificity. As for with respect to negation and affirmation, the modes occurring in each mood are sixteen, eight of which are invalid and eight valid, as in the first figure; since each of the minor and major [premises] may be affirmative, or have a negated subject, or have a negated predicate, or have both subject and predicate negated. And just as each one of them is multiple according to generality/specificity and negation/affirmation, so too according to the other [aspect], namely that each of the minor and major in each mood is either bipartite or tripartite, or the minor is bipartite without the major, or vice versa. So each mood has four modes. Multiply the modes occurring in each mood according to generality/specificity by the remaining eight which are according to negation/affirmation. The total in these four [figures] is such that from the modes in the first mood the sum is sixty-eight, likewise in the second mood, and in the third double this sum, and likewise in the fourth. Then when you multiply these modes by all the simple and mixed syllogisms, the sum reaches multiples of these sums.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the combinatorial possibilities of syllogistic moods in the third and fourth figures. The author begins by stating that the middle term can relate to the minor term in four ways: more general, more specific, equal, or none of these. This is the basis for classifying the figures. Then he addresses the fourth figure, noting that the relation of the middle to the major in the fourth is like the relation of the middle to the minor in the third. After discussing generality/specificity, he turns to negation/affirmation (adool wa tahseel), explaining that each figure's moods have 16 sub-moods, of which 8 are valid and 8 invalid, similar to the first figure. He then adds another dimension: each premise can be bipartite (two terms) or tripartite (three terms, likely including a third term like 'every' or 'some'), leading to four combinations per mood. Multiplying the generality/specificity modes (4) by the negation/affirmation modes (8 valid ones) and then by the bipartite/tripartite modes (4) yields 4 × 8 × 4 = 128 modes for each mood? But the author says the first mood yields 68, which suggests a different calculation. The author's arithmetic likely considers only certain combinations. The passage ends by noting that multiplying these modes by all simple and mixed syllogisms yields even larger sums, indicating the vast array of potential syllogistic forms.

The Sixteen Sub-Moods: Negation and Affirmation in Syllogisms
Author discusses the sixteen sub-moods based on negation/affirmation and notes that eight are valid, similar to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eight valid combinations of subject/predicate negation and affirmation from the sixteen possible in each syllogistic figure.

Core Idea
Each syllogistic premise can have four forms; only eight of sixteen mood combinations are valid.

Beyond the quantity and quality of propositions, logicians also consider whether the subject and predicate are affirmative or negated. The passage explains that each figure has sixteen sub-moods based on negation/affirmation, of which eight are valid, like in the first figure. This lesson unpacks those combinations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the four premise forms
Each premise can be: affirmative subject and predicate, negated subject alone, negated predicate alone, or both negated. List these four forms for both minor and major premises.
These are called 'muhassala', 'ma'dulat al-mawdu'', 'ma'dulat al-mahmul', and 'ma'dulat al-tarafayn'.
2
Compute the total combinations
Since each premise has 4 forms, the total is 4 × 4 = 16 sub-moods for each mood of each figure.
The passage confirms this and says 8 are valid, 8 invalid.
3
Determine the valid sub-moods using the first figure as reference
In the first figure, the valid moods are AAA, EAE, AII, EIO. For each, map the negation/affirmation pattern. For example, AAA requires both premises affirmative in subject and predicate; EAE requires minor affirmative, major negative predicate; etc.
This yields exactly 8 valid combos: the four moods each correspond to a specific negation/affirmation pattern.
4
Apply to a kalam argument
Take a syllogism about divine attributes: 'Everything created is contingent; everything contingent is not eternal; therefore nothing created is eternal.' Identify the negation/affirmation pattern and check if it is among the eight valid.
This argument uses affirmative minor and major with negated predicate, which is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقع فيه على أربعة أوجه لما أن الأوسط بالنسبة إلى الأصغر قد يكون أعم من الأصغر، وقد يكون أخص منه، وقد يكون مساويًا، وقد يكون لا أعم ولا أخص ولا مساويًا. وكذلك في الرابع منها فإن نسبة الأوسط إلى الأكبر فيه كنسبة الأوسط إلى الأصغر في الثالث، هذا بالنسبة إلى العموم والخصوص. فأما بالنسبة إلى العدول والتحصيل فالوجوه الواقعة في كل ضرب منها ستة عشر ثمانية منها ساقطة، وثمانية باقية كما في الشكل الأول؛ إذ كل واحدة من الصغرى والكبرى قد تكون محصلة، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع، وقد تكون معدولة المحمول، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع والمحمول. وكما أن كل واحد منها متعدد بحسب العموم والخصوص والعدول والتحصيل فكذلك بحسب الغير وذلك أن تكون كل واحدة من الصغرى والكبرى في كل ضرب من الضروب، إما أن تكون ثنائية، أو ثلاثية، أو الصغرى ثنائية دون الكبرى، أو بالعكس. فيكون كل ضرب منها على أربعة أوجه فيضرب ما يكون واقعا من الوجوه في كل ضرب بحسب العموم والخصوص في الثمانية الباقية التي هي بحسب العدول والتحصيل. والمبلغ في هذه الأربعة كذلك حتى يحصل من الوجوه في الضرب الأول مبلغ ثمانية وستين، وكذلك في الضرب الثاني، وفي الثالث ضعف هذا المبلغ، وكذلك في الرابع. ثم إذا ضربت هذه الوجوه في جميع الأقيسة البسيطة والمختلطة بلغت مبلغ أضعاف هذه المبالغ.
It occurs in four ways because the middle term relative to the minor term may be more general than the minor, or more specific than it, or equal to it, or neither more general nor more specific nor equal. Likewise in the fourth [figure], the relation of the middle to the major in it is like the relation of the middle to the minor in the third, this with respect to generality and specificity. As for with respect to negation and affirmation, the modes occurring in each mood are sixteen, eight of which are invalid and eight valid, as in the first figure; since each of the minor and major [premises] may be affirmative, or have a negated subject, or have a negated predicate, or have both subject and predicate negated. And just as each one of them is multiple according to generality/specificity and negation/affirmation, so too according to the other [aspect], namely that each of the minor and major in each mood is either bipartite or tripartite, or the minor is bipartite without the major, or vice versa. So each mood has four modes. Multiply the modes occurring in each mood according to generality/specificity by the remaining eight which are according to negation/affirmation. The total in these four [figures] is such that from the modes in the first mood the sum is sixty-eight, likewise in the second mood, and in the third double this sum, and likewise in the fourth. Then when you multiply these modes by all the simple and mixed syllogisms, the sum reaches multiples of these sums.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the combinatorial possibilities of syllogistic moods in the third and fourth figures. The author begins by stating that the middle term can relate to the minor term in four ways: more general, more specific, equal, or none of these. This is the basis for classifying the figures. Then he addresses the fourth figure, noting that the relation of the middle to the major in the fourth is like the relation of the middle to the minor in the third. After discussing generality/specificity, he turns to negation/affirmation (adool wa tahseel), explaining that each figure's moods have 16 sub-moods, of which 8 are valid and 8 invalid, similar to the first figure. He then adds another dimension: each premise can be bipartite (two terms) or tripartite (three terms, likely including a third term like 'every' or 'some'), leading to four combinations per mood. Multiplying the generality/specificity modes (4) by the negation/affirmation modes (8 valid ones) and then by the bipartite/tripartite modes (4) yields 4 × 8 × 4 = 128 modes for each mood? But the author says the first mood yields 68, which suggests a different calculation. The author's arithmetic likely considers only certain combinations. The passage ends by noting that multiplying these modes by all simple and mixed syllogisms yields even larger sums, indicating the vast array of potential syllogistic forms.

Calculating the Total Modes: Multiplying Dimensions
Author extends the combinatorial enumeration to include bipartite/tripartite forms and gives specific counts for each mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the total number of syllogistic modes in a given figure by combining the three dimensions: generality/specificity, negation/affirmation, and bipartite/tripartite structure.

Core Idea
Multiplying independent dimensions yields vast combinatorial numbers, but the author's specific count of 68 suggests additional restrictions.

The passage introduces a third dimension: each premise can be bipartite (two terms) or tripartite (three terms, possibly including quantifiers). Combining this with the previous two dimensions leads to a mathematical count. The author gives specific numbers 68, 68, 136, 136 for the four moods. This lesson walks through the multiplication and explores why the numbers are not simply 4×8×4 = 128.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three dimensions
The three dimensions are: (1) the four relations of generality/specificity, (2) the eight valid negation/affirmation combos, and (3) the four bipartite/tripartite structures.
The passage mentions 'bipartite or tripartite' and their combinations.
2
Multiply the dimensions naively
4 (generality) × 8 (negation/affirmation) × 4 (bipartite/tripartite) = 128 modes per mood.
This is the expected number if all combinations are possible.
3
Compare with the author's numbers
The author states the first mood has 68 modes, the second also 68, the third double (136), and the fourth also 136. Notice the discrepancy: 68 instead of 128 for the first two.
This indicates that some combinations are excluded, perhaps due to logical constraints not specified in this passage.
4
Hypothesize the missing restrictions
Consider that not all generality relations combine with all bipartite/tripartite forms. For instance, certain relations might force a specific bipartite/tripartite structure.
Advanced logicians might elaborate on these restrictions.

Objection and Response on Equal vs Non-Equal Necessary Consequence
Author presents objection and responds with denials and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze an objection-response pair about necessary consequence and identify the logical moves of denial and counterexample.

Core Idea
A necessary consequent can be equal to its antecedent without contradiction, as daylight follows sunrise.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange on the nature of necessary implication (luzum). The objector argues that if something follows from another, it must be either equal or non-equal to it, and both are impossible, so necessary consequence is impossible. The author responds by denying both premises and providing key counterexamples. Understanding this refinement is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence that rely on necessary relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector argues that if something is a necessary consequent (lāzim), it must be either equal (musāwin) or non-equal (ghayr musāwin) to its antecedent (malzūm). Both options are claimed to be false, so necessary consequence is impossible.
2
Parse the two-part argument
First part: If equal, then the consequent would be a consequent of something else, which is false. Second part: If non-equal, then they must be inseparable, otherwise it wouldn't be necessary, but the objector asserts they are separable.
3
Locate the author's response and denials
The author denies both parts of the objection. He states that equality is possible even if it is a consequent of something else, and that non-equality does not necessarily prevent implication.
4
Examine the counterexample
The author gives the example of sunrise and daytime: they are inseparable and equal in existence. Sunrise is the antecedent, daytime is the consequent, yet they are equal.
This shows that an equal consequent is possible.
5
Understand the refinement about separation
The author distinguishes between separation between the consequent and antecedent (which does not prevent implication) and absolute separation without considering which is which (which does prevent implication).
This nuanced point protects the validity of many necessary relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه إذا لزم فلا يخلو من أن يكون مساويا له، وإنه باطل ضرورة كونه لازما للغير؛ واللازم للغير يمكن أن يوجد عند وجود ذلك الغير دون وجود غيره. أو لا يكون مساويًا، وإنه باطل أيضًا ضرورة عدم الافتراق بينهما أصلا وإلا لا يكون لازما أبدًا. لكنا نقول في الجواب [١] عن الأول إنه لا يمكن أن يكون مساويا به وإن كان لازما للغير. فإن من الأشياء ما يكون لازما للشيء وغيره لازما مساويًا الملزومه. وأما قوله «اللازم للغير يمكن أن يوجد عند وجود ذلك الغير دون وجود غيره، فذلك في حيز المنع لأنه لا يمكن إلا وأن يمكن الافتراق بينهما في التحقق. ومن الجائز أن لا يمكن كما في طلوع الشمس ووجود النهار فإنه لا يمكن أن ينفك أحدهما عن الآخر؛ وقد كان ضوء العالم لهذا. ولذلك كذلك لازما مساويًا للملزوم. وأما قوله في القسم الآخر ضرورة عدم الافتراق بينهما وإلا لا يكون لازما فذلك في حيز المنع أيضًا. فإن الافتراق بينهما من حيث هو الافتراق بينهما لا يكون مانعا عن اللزوم، والافتراق بين اللازم والملزوم لا يكون مانعا عن اللزوم بل المانع هو الافتراق بينهما على الإطلاق من غير أن ينظر إلى أحدهما بأنه لازم أو ملزوم . ولا يقال كيف يمكن أن يكون الأعم مانعا عن الشيء، والأخص منه لا يكون مانعا عن ذلك؛ فإن الأعم وهو الإنسان مثلا مانع عما يكون مانعا عن كونه أعم، والأخص منه وهو هذا الإنسان لا يكون مانعا عن ذلك.
For if it follows, then it is either equal to it, and that is false due to necessity of it being a consequent of something else; and what is consequent upon something else can exist when that something else exists without existing without it. Or it is not equal, and that is also false due to the necessity of non-separation between them at all, otherwise it would not be a consequent at all. But we say in response to the first: it is impossible for it to be equal to it even if it is a consequent of something else. For some things are consequent upon a thing and other things are equal consequents of their antecedent. As for his statement 'what is consequent upon something else can exist when that something else exists without existing without it', that is subject to denial because it is not possible unless separation between them in actuality is possible. And it is possible that it is not possible, as in sunrise and the existence of daytime, for it is impossible for one to separate from the other; and the light of the world is due to this. And therefore it is likewise an equal consequent of the antecedent. And as for his statement in the other division, regarding the necessity of non-separation between them otherwise it would not be a consequent, that is also subject to denial. For separation between them inasmuch as it is separation between them is not a preventive of implication, and separation between the consequent and the antecedent is not a preventive of implication; rather the preventive is separation between them absolutely without considering one of them as consequent or antecedent. And it cannot be said: how can the more general be a preventive of something while the more specific is not a preventive of that? For the more general, for example 'human', is a preventive of what would prevent it from being more general, while the more specific, i.e., 'this human', is not a preventive of that.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents the first full objection-answer pair in a dialectical exchange about necessary consequence (luzum). The objection (number 2 in the text) argues that if something follows from something else, it is either equal to that thing or not. If equal, it must be a consequent of something else (which is possible but the objector claims false). If not equal, then they must be inseparable (otherwise it wouldn't be a necessary consequent). The objector claims both are false. The author responds by denying both premises. He gives examples: sunrise and daytime are inseparable and equal in existence. The objection's second part about non-separation is also denied: separation per se does not prevent implication; rather absolute separation without considering one as antecedent or consequent is the preventive. He uses an analogy: 'human' as a general term prevents something that would prevent it from being general, but the specific 'this human' does not prevent that. The passage demonstrates the dialectical method of naqd (objection) and jawab (response) in logical discourse.

Key Terms
لازم
ملزوم
لزوم
مساوٍ

D) Law is a consequent of the lawgiver.

Key Takeaway
A necessary consequent can be equal to its antecedent, and separation between consequent and antecedent does not necessarily negate implication; only absolute separation does.

Five Objections on Necessary Concomitance and Syllogistic Conditions Refuted
Author refutes five objections regarding conditions for necessary concomitance and syllogistic productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute common objections regarding necessary concomitance (luzum) and the conditions for syllogistic productivity in the first figure.

Core Idea
A concomitant does not require absolute inseparability; only certain forms of separation invalidate it.

In this passage, the author systematically refutes five objections that critics raise against the conditions for necessary concomitance and syllogistic validity. These objections often confuse absolute separation with relational separation, or mistake linguistic variation for logical variation. By learning these refutations, you will sharpen your ability to defend logical principles against fallacious challenges in Islamic dialectical debates.

Guided Path
1
First objection: necessity of non-separation
Find the claim that if two things can separate, they cannot be concomitant. Note the author's counterexample of sunrise and daytime, and his distinction between separation as such and relational separation.
2
Second objection: following from another
Understand the objection that a concomitant cannot follow from something else without absurdity. See the author's response that needing another does not negate the attribute; it only negates it after the fact.
3
Third objection: subject and predicate in conclusion
Analyze the objection that the subject in the conclusion must be the predicate. Note the author's response that difference requires clarification, not impossibility.
4
Fourth objection: middle term identity
Examine the objection that the middle term in both premises must be identical in all respects. Recognize that the middle term must be unified in meaning; differences due to language are irrelevant.
5
Fifth objection: difference in quality
Evaluate the objection that the first figure does not require premises to differ in quality. Understand that the examples given involve modified terms and are not properly syllogistic compositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في التحقق. ومن الجائز أن لا يمكن كما في طلوع الشمس ووجود النهار فإنه لا يمكن أن ينفك أحدهما عن الآخر؛ وقد كان ضوء العالم لهذا. ولذلك كذلك لازما مساويًا للملزوم. وأما قوله في القسم الآخر ضرورة عدم الافتراق بينهما وإلا لا يكون لازما فذلك في حيز المنع أيضًا. فإن الافتراق بينهما من حيث هو الافتراق بينهما لا يكون مانعا عن اللزوم، والافتراق بين اللازم والملزوم لا يكون مانعا عن اللزوم بل المانع هو الافتراق بينهما على الإطلاق من غير أن ينظر إلى أحدهما بأنه لازم أو ملزوم . ولا يقال كيف يمكن أن يكون الأعم مانعا عن الشيء، والأخص منه لا يكون مانعا عن ذلك؛ فإن الأعم وهو الإنسان مثلا مانع عما يكون مانعا عن كونه أعم، والأخص منه وهو هذا الإنسان لا يكون مانعا عن ذلك. [٣] وعن الثاني منها نقول إنه يلزم من الغير كما ذكرتم فلا يلزم منه المحال أصلا فإنه يمكن أن يلزم عن هذا لذاته عن ذلك كذلك. والافتقار إلى الغير من حيث هو الافتقار إلى الغير لا يخرجه عن كونه متصفّا بهذه الصفة بل المخرج هو الافتقار إلى الغير بعد تحقق هذا وهو الضرب الأول من هذا الشكل. ألا ترى أنه إذا لزم عنه لذاته وكان مفتقرًا إليه بعد كونه لازما كذلك كان مفتقرًا إلى ما يكون ذلك مفتقرًا إليه من الركن والشرط وغير ذلك. [٤] وعن الثالث نقول يمكن أن يكون الموضوع في النتيجة غير المحمول لكن كونه غير ذلك لا يكون بينا بنفسه بل يفتقر إلى البيان والبيان من جملة ما يظهر به ذلك لا من جملة ما يتحقق به ذلك، وهذا ظاهر. [٥] وعن الرابع إن الأوسط فيما ضربتم من الأمثال هو الحيوان من حيث إنه حيوان لا حيوان كذا؛ والحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان لا يمكن أن يكون في الصغرى / غير ما في الكبرى أو بالعكس. وأما أن المنكر في المرة الثانية غير ما في الأولى فذلك بحسب اللغة؛ والكلام فيما نحن فيه بحسب الاصطلاح فلا يقدح فيه. [٥] وعن الخامس إنه من جملة ما قد مرّ الكلام فيه، ولأن العادة جرت في قولهم كل «ج» «ب» إنه إنما يستعمل ذلك على قصد أن كل «ج» (ب) حال ما يكون (ب) فيجب أن يعتبر هذا في هذا الشكل وإن قيل سلمنا أن ما ذكرتم منتج لذاته لكن لم قلتم إن إنتاجه يفتقر إلى الاختلاف بين مقدمتين في الكيف؟ وكيف يفتقر وقد لزم في كثير من الصور بدونه كما إذا قلت كل كاتب متحرّك، وكل ساكن لا متحرك فلا شيء من الكاتب بساكن؟ وكذلك إذا قلت كل فقيه عالم بالضرورة، وكل سفيه عالم لا بالضرورة فلا واحد من الفقيه بسفيه . وعلى هذا فإنه يتعدد حسب تعدّد المعدولات وذوات الجهات. فنقول مثل هذه الاعتراضات وإن كان مسطورًا في البعض من الكتب فلا يكون كما ينبغي ؛ إذ التركيب في الأول لا يكون من التركيبات القياسية على الخصوص من التركيبات الواقعة في هذا الشكل. وقد كان من اللوازم أن يكون الأوسط متحدًا في التركيبات القياسية وإنه لا يكون متحدا في ذلك. ولا يلزم منه ما ذكرتم أيضًا وهو السالبة بل يلزم منه ذلك إذا كان قولنا لا متحرك بمعنى قولنا ليس بمتحرك؛ وليس كذلك لما مر من قبل أن المعدول غير المحصل ولا يمكنه أن يقول يعنى به هذا. فإنه إذا كان كذلك كان من جملة ما نحن في تحقيقه وهو الضرب الأول من هذا الشكل. وأما الثاني فذلك يعرف من بعد في المختلطات أنه هل هو من هذا الشكل أم لا؟ والكلام في هذا المقام هو الكلام في الغير.
In verification. And it is possible that it cannot be, as in the rising of the sun and the existence of daytime, for it is impossible for one to separate from the other; and it was that light of the world is for this. And therefore it is likewise a necessary concomitant equal to the subject. And as for his statement in the other division, 'the necessity of non-separation between them, otherwise it would not be a concomitant,' that is subject to denial as well. For separation between them inasmuch as it is separation between them does not prevent concomitance, and separation between the concomitant and the subject does not prevent concomitance; rather, the preventer is separation between them absolutely without regard to one of them being a concomitant or subject. And it is not said: how can the more general be a preventer of something, while the more specific is not a preventer of that? For the more general, like human, prevents what prevents it from being more general, while the more specific, like this human, does not prevent that. [3] And regarding the second of them, we say: it follows from the other as you mentioned, so no impossibility follows from it at all, for it is possible for this to follow from itself and for that to follow from that similarly. And the need for the other inasmuch as it is a need for the other does not remove it from being described by this attribute; rather, what removes it is the need for the other after this is realized, and that is the first mode of this figure. Do you not see that if it follows from itself and it is in need of it after being a concomitant similarly, it is in need of what that is in need of, such as the pillar and condition and other things. [4] And regarding the third, we say: it is possible that the subject in the conclusion is not the predicate, but its being otherwise is not self-evident; rather, it needs clarification, and clarification is among the means by which it is made apparent, not among the means by which it is realized, and this is clear. [5] And regarding the fourth, the middle term in the examples you gave is 'animal' inasmuch as it is animal, not 'animal' as it were; and 'animal' inasmuch as it is animal cannot be in the minor premise different from what is in the major, or vice versa. And as for the fact that the negated term in the second instance is different from the first, that is according to language; and the discussion here is according to convention, so it does not detract. [5] And regarding the fifth, it is among that which has already been discussed, and because the custom in their saying 'every Ja B' is that it is only used with the intention that every Ja (is) B in the state that it is B, so this must be considered in this figure. And if it is said: we grant that what you mentioned is productive in itself, but why do you say its production requires difference between the two premises in quality? And how does it require it when it has occurred in many forms without it, as when you say: every writer is moving, and every stationary thing is not moving, so no writer is stationary. And similarly if you say: every jurist is necessarily knowledgeable, and every fool is not necessarily knowledgeable, so no jurist is a fool. And upon this, it multiplies according to the multiplicity of modified propositions and those with modes. So we say: such objections, even if recorded in some books, are not as they should be, for the composition in the first is not among the syllogistic compositions, especially those occurring in this figure. And it was among the necessities that the middle term be unified in syllogistic compositions, and it is not unified in that. And what you mentioned, i.e., the negative, does not follow from it; rather, it follows from it if our saying 'not moving' means 'is not moving'; and it is not so, because it was stated before that the modified term is not affirmative and he cannot intend by it this. For if it were so, it would be among what we are investigating, which is the first mode of this figure. And as for the second, it will be known later in the mixed moods whether it belongs to this figure or not. And the discussion in this place is a discussion of something else.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of five objections regarding the conditions for necessary concomitance (luzum) and syllogistic productivity. The author begins by discussing a case where two things are necessarily concomitant but cannot be separated, such as sunrise and daytime. He argues that the necessity of non-separation is not a requirement for concomitance; rather, separation in a specific sense does not negate the relation. The preventer is absolute separation, not relational. He uses an example: the more general ('human') prevents what prevents it from being more general, but the more specific ('this human') does not. This is a logical principle about relative preventers. 

Objection 2: The author responds that a concomitant may follow from something else, and this does not entail impossibility. Need for another does not negate the attribute; what negates it is need after the fact. This is the first mode of the figure. 

Objection 3: The subject in the conclusion may differ from the predicate, but this is not self-evident; it requires clarification. Clarification makes it apparent, not real. 

Objection 4: The middle term in the examples is 'animal' considered specifically; it cannot differ between premises. Differences due to language changes are irrelevant; the discussion is about technical terms. 

Objection 5: The author says it was already covered. The custom in using 'every J is B' implies a state, so this must be considered. A further objection asks why production requires difference in quality, citing examples where it does not. The author responds that such compositions are not properly syllogistic; the middle term must be unified. Negative conclusions only follow if 'not moving' means 'is not moving', but modified terms are not affirmative. This relates to the first mode of the figure. The second mode will be discussed later in mixed moods. 

This passage illustrates careful logical reasoning about the conditions for validity, emphasizing that apparent counterexamples often involve subtle shifts in meaning or composition.

The Syllogism
beginner

The Third Figure: Middle Term as Subject in Both Premises
Author defines the third figure of the syllogism, where the middle term is subject in both premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third figure of the syllogism by checking the position of the middle term in both premises.

Core Idea
The third figure links two predicates through a common subject.

This passage introduces the third figure of the syllogism, where the middle term serves as the subject in both the minor and major premises. This structure allows us to reason about two properties that share a common subject. For example, if we know that every human is rational and every human is an animal, we can infer something about the relation between rationality and animality. Understanding this figure is essential for analyzing arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
Find the term (hadd al-awsat) that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
2
Check the minor premise
Determine whether the middle term is the subject (mubtada') of the minor premise (sughra).
3
Check the major premise
Determine whether the middle term is also the subject of the major premise (kubra).
4
Conclude the figure
If the middle term is subject in both premises, the syllogism belongs to the third figure.
This distinguishes it from the first figure (middle term subject in minor, predicate in major) and the second figure (middle term predicate in both).

[4.104] Chapter: On the Third Figure. As for the third figure, which is the one in which the middle term is a subject in both the minor and major premises, that reverts to inference by the conjunction of two things in one subject; for the minor term and the major term, when they meet in one subject which is the middle term in them, they meet in...
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage introduces the third figure of the syllogism, which is defined by the position of the middle term: it serves as the subject in both the minor and major premises. The author explains that this figure is based on reasoning about two things (the minor and major terms) coming together in a single subject (the middle term). For example, if we know that 'every human is rational' and 'every human is an animal', we can infer something about the relation between rationality and animality. This is the foundation of the third figure. The text cuts off before completing the explanation, likely continuing on the next page.

Refuting Counterexamples to the Second Figure's Quality Condition
Author refutes an objection claiming the second figure does not require premises differing in quality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify invalid counterexamples to the second figure's quality condition by detecting modified terms and modal shifts.

Core Idea
Modified terms break middle-term identity; modal examples belong to mixed syllogisms.

An opponent claims that the second figure can produce valid conclusions even when premises do not differ in quality, offering two examples. The author carefully dismantles these examples, revealing that one is not a proper categorical syllogism and the other belongs to a different logical category. This lesson trains you to spot such spurious counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition under attack
The rule is: In the second figure, the two premises must differ in quality—one affirmative and one negative. The objector tries to show valid syllogisms where this is not the case.
2
Examine the first example
The objector gives: Every writer is moving; every stationary thing is not moving; therefore no writer is stationary. Check the second premise: 'not moving' is a modified term (muʿaddala), not a simple negative. The middle term becomes 'moving' vs 'not moving', which are not identical.
3
Assess the middle term identity
In a valid categorical syllogism, the middle term must be exactly the same in both premises. Here, 'moving' and 'not moving' are different concepts, so this is not a valid second-figure syllogism.
4
Analyze the second example
The objector gives: Every jurist is necessarily knowledgeable; every foolish person is not necessarily knowledgeable; therefore no jurist is foolish. The premises involve modalities ('necessarily', 'not necessarily'), which fall under mixed syllogisms (mukhtalitat), not the non-modal second figure discussed here.
5
Conclude the objection fails
The author dismisses both examples: the first is not properly syllogistic due to modified terms, and the second belongs to another category. The quality condition for the pure second figure remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الخامس إنه من جملة ما قد مرّ الكلام فيه، ولأن العادة جرت في قولهم كل «ج» «ب» إنه إنما يستعمل ذلك على قصد أن كل «ج» (ب) حال ما يكون (ب) فيجب أن يعتبر هذا في هذا الشكل وإن قيل سلمنا أن ما ذكرتم منتج لذاته لكن لم قلتم إن إنتاجه يفتقر إلى الاختلاف بين مقدمتين في الكيف؟ وكيف يفتقر وقد لزم في كثير من الصور بدونه كما إذا قلت كل كاتب متحرّك، وكل ساكن لا متحرك فلا شيء من الكاتب بساكن؟ وكذلك إذا قلت كل فقيه عالم بالضرورة، وكل سفيه عالم لا بالضرورة فلا واحد من الفقيه بسفيه . وعلى هذا فإنه يتعدد حسب تعدّد المعدولات وذوات الجهات. فنقول مثل هذه الاعتراضات وإن كان مسطورًا في البعض من الكتب فلا يكون كما ينبغي ؛ إذ التركيب في الأول لا يكون من التركيبات القياسية على الخصوص من التركيبات الواقعة في هذا الشكل. وقد كان من اللوازم أن يكون الأوسط متحدًا في التركيبات القياسية وإنه لا يكون متحدا في ذلك. ولا يلزم منه ما ذكرتم أيضًا وهو السالبة بل يلزم منه ذلك إذا كان قولنا لا متحرك بمعنى قولنا ليس بمتحرك؛ وليس كذلك لما مر من قبل أن المعدول غير المحصل ولا يمكنه أن يقول يعنى به هذا. فإنه إذا كان كذلك كان من جملة ما نحن في تحقيقه وهو الضرب الأول من هذا الشكل. وأما الثاني فذلك يعرف من بعد في المختلطات أنه هل هو من هذا الشكل أم لا؟ والكلام في هذا المقام هو الكلام في الغير.
And concerning the fifth [objection] that it is among what has already been discussed, and because the custom in their saying 'Every C is B' is that they use that with the intention that every C is B at the time it is B, so this must be considered in this figure. If it is said: we concede that what you mentioned is productive by itself, but why do you say that its production depends on the difference between the two premises in quality? How does it depend when it has occurred in many forms without it, as when you say: every writer is moving, and every stationary thing is not moving, so no writer is stationary? And similarly when you say: every jurist is necessarily knowledgeable, and every foolish person is not necessarily knowledgeable, so not a single jurist is foolish. And accordingly it multiplies according to the multiplicity of modified terms and those with modalities. So we say: such objections, even if recorded in some books, are not as they should be; because the composition in the first is not among the syllogistic compositions, especially those occurring in this figure. It was necessary that the middle term be unified in syllogistic compositions, and it is not unified in that. Nor does what you mentioned follow, namely the negative; rather it follows from that if our saying 'not moving' means our saying 'is not moving'; but it is not so because it has passed before that the modified term is not affirmative, and he cannot say that he means it by this. For if it were so, it would be among what we are investigating, which is the first mood of this figure. As for the second, that will be known later in the mixed syllogisms whether it belongs to this figure or not. The discussion in this place is about the other.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a critical response to an objection against the claim that the second figure requires the two premises to differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). The objector presents two examples: (1) 'Every writer is moving, and every stationary thing is not moving, therefore no writer is stationary' – here both premises are affirmative? Actually the second is negative: 'not moving' is a negative predication? But the objector argues that the quality is not different? Wait: the objector claims that in this example the premises are not different in quality? Actually both premises have 'is' and 'is not' – one is affirmative, the other negative. But the objection is that production occurs without difference? Let's re-read: The objector says 'though it has occurred in many forms without it' meaning without the difference? But his examples do have difference? Perhaps the objector means that examples like 'every writer is moving' (affirmative) and 'every stationary thing is not moving' (also affirmative? because 'not moving' is a modified term? Actually in classical logic, 'every S is not-P' is considered negative? There's nuance. The author's answer clarifies that the first example is not a proper categorical syllogism because the second premise uses a modified term ('not moving') which is not the same as a negative proposition. The middle term must be identical; here the middle is 'moving' vs 'not moving' – different. The second example involves modalities (necessary/not necessary) and belongs to mixed syllogisms, not pure second figure. Thus the objection fails.

The Third Figure: Conditions and Productive Moods
Author defines the third figure, explains its epistemic purpose, and enumerates productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third figure by the position of the middle term, apply its two conditions (affirmative minor and universal premise) to determine whether a given pair of premises yields a valid conclusion, and calculate the number of productive moods from the 16 possible premise combinations.

Core Idea
The third figure yields only particular conclusions because the middle term is a shared subject.

In this passage, the author defines the third figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is the subject of both premises. He explains why this figure can only produce particular conclusions and lays out two key conditions for a syllogism to be productive: the minor premise must be affirmative, and at least one premise must be universal. By applying these conditions, six valid moods remain from the initial sixteen possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject in both premises. When examining a syllogism, verify that the term that appears in both premises is the subject in each. This is the defining feature of the third figure.
For example, in the valid mood later, the middle term 'human' is subject in both 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is a horse'.
2
Understand the epistemic limitation
Because the middle term is a common subject, the minor and major terms meet only in that subject. Their relationship beyond that subject is unknown, so the conclusion can only be particular (some S is P or some S is not P). Confirm that the author states this explicitly.
This is why the third figure cannot produce universal conclusions.
3
Apply the minor premise condition
The minor premise must be affirmative. If it is negative, the major term could be a genus of both the minor and middle terms, leading to an invalid conclusion. Test this with the author's example: 'No human is a horse; every human is an animal' does not yield 'Some horse is not an animal'.
4
Apply the universality condition
At least one premise must be universal. If both are particular, the minor and major terms may refer to different parts of the middle term, so no necessary conclusion follows. Test with the example: 'Some animal is human; some animal is horse' yields no conclusion about human and horse.
5
Calculate the productive moods
Start with 16 possible quantified premise pairs (4x4). The condition of affirmative minor eliminates 8 (those with a negative minor). The condition of at least one universal eliminates 2 more (those with both particular). The remaining 6 are productive.
The author gives the final count as six.

فصل : [ في الشكل الثالث ] أما الشكل الثالث وهو الذي يكون الأوسط فيه موضوعا في الصغرى والكبرى فذلك راجع إلى الاستدلال باجتماع الأمرين في موضوع واحد؛ إذ الأصغر والأكبر إذا اجتمعا في موضوع واحد وهو الأوسط فيهما يلتقيان في ذلك المحل لا محالة، ولا يعلم حالهما فيما وراء ذلك فيحكم على حسب ذلك. ولهذا لا ينتج إلا جزئية موجبة كانت أو سالبة. والضروب الحاصلة بالنسبة إلى المحصورات وغيرها هي ما يحصل من ضرب الثمانية في الثمانية. وبالنسبة إلى المحصورات لا غير هي ما يحصل من ضرب الأربعة في الأربعة كما في الشكل الأول والثاني لكن البعض منها ساقط لما أن الشرط في إنتاج هذا الشكل هو أن يكون إحدى مقدمتيه كلية كما في الكل. فإنها إذا لم تكن كلية البتة يحتمل أن يكون البعض الذي فيه الأصغر يغاير الذي فيه الأكبر ؛ وحينئذ لا يحصل الالتقاء بين الأصغر والأكبر كما في الجنس إذا حمل على بعضه نوع وعلى بعضه الآخر آخر بالإيجاب فيقال بعض الحيوان إنسان، وبعض الحيوان فرس فلا ينتج بعض الإنسان فرس : والحق هنا ر السلب. وكذلك إذا حمل على بعضه نوع وعلى بعضه الآخر فصل ذلك النوع بالسلب كما يقال بعض الحيوان إنسان، وبعض الحيوان ليس بناطق فإنه لا ينتج قولنا بعض الإنسان ليس بناطق: والحق هنا هو الإيجاب. وكما أن كلية إحدى مقدمتيه من الشرائط فكذلك موجبية الصغرى كما في الشكل الأول فإنها إذا لم تكن موجبة يحتمل أن يكون الأكبر جنسًا للأصغر والأوسط كما يقال لا واحد من الإنسان بفرس، وكل إنسان حيوان فلا ينتج قولنا بعض الفرس ليس بحيوان. فأما إذا كانت السالبة هي الكبرى فإنه ينتج كما يقال كل إنسان حيوان، ولا واحد من الإنسان بفرس فبعض الإنسان ليس بفرس؛ وقد مرَّ من قبل أن سلب الخاص عن بعض العام ممكن بخلاف سلب العام عن بعض الخاص : إذا عرفت هذا فنقول اشتراط موجبية الصغرى يُسقط من الضروب الستة عشر ثمانية، واشتراط كلية إحدى مقدمتيه ضربين آخرين؛ فبقيت المنتجة منها ستة.
Chapter: On the Third Figure. As for the third figure, which is the one where the middle term is the subject in both the minor and major premises, that goes back to inference by two things coming together in one subject; for the minor and major terms, when they come together in one subject which is the middle term in both, they meet in that place necessarily, and their state beyond that is not known, so judgment is made accordingly. For this reason, it only produces a particular conclusion, whether affirmative or negative. The moods that arise with respect to quantified [premises] and others are what results from multiplying eight by eight. And with respect to quantified alone, they are what results from multiplying four by four as in the first and second figures, but some of them are invalid because the condition for production in this figure is that one of its two premises be universal, as in all. For if they are not universal at all, it is possible that the part which contains the minor term differs from that which contains the major; and then no meeting occurs between the minor and major, as when a genus is predicated of some of its species and of some others another [species] by affirmation; it is said: 'Some animal is human, and some animal is horse' – then 'Some human is horse' is not produced; the truth here is the negative. Similarly, if a species is predicated of some of it and the differentia of that species is predicated of others by negation, as is said: 'Some animal is human, and some animal is not rational' – then our statement 'Some human is not rational' is not produced; the truth here is the affirmative. And just as the universality of one of its premises is a condition, so too is the affirmativity of the minor premise as in the first figure; for if it is not affirmative, it is possible that the major term is a genus of the minor and middle, as is said: 'No human is a horse, and every human is an animal' – then our statement 'Some horse is not an animal' is not produced. As for when the negative is the major, it does produce, as is said: 'Every human is an animal, and no human is a horse' – then 'Some human is not a horse' [is produced]; and it has passed before that negating the specific from some of the general is possible, unlike negating the general from some of the specific. Once you understand this, we say: the condition of the minor premise being affirmative eliminates eight of the sixteen moods, and the condition of one of its premises being universal eliminates two more; so six productive moods remain.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure and explains its conditions and productive moods. The author begins by stating the definition: the third figure has the middle term as subject in both premises. It is used to infer from two terms (minor and major) both sharing the same subject (the middle). Because the middle is common to both, the minor and major necessarily meet in that subject, but their relationship beyond that subject is unknown, so only particular conclusions (some S is P or some S is not P) can be drawn. The author then notes that if we consider all possible premise combinations (including non-quantified), there are 64 moods (8×8), but for quantified categorical propositions, the possible premise pairs are 16 (4×4, as in the first two figures). However, not all are productive. Two conditions are needed: (1) at least one premise must be universal; (2) the minor premise must be affirmative. The author explains why: if both premises are particular, the minor and major might refer to different parts of the middle, so no necessary conclusion follows. He gives examples: 'Some animal is human, some animal is horse' yields no conclusion about human and horse; the correct conclusion is negative. Similarly, 'Some animal is human, some animal is not rational' does not yield 'Some human is not rational'; the correct conclusion is affirmative because the non-rational animals might be other species. The second condition (affirmative minor) is illustrated: if the minor is negative (e.g., 'No human is a horse'), the major premise 'Every human is an animal' does not allow a valid conclusion about horses not being animals. However, if the minor is affirmative and the major is negative, it works (e.g., 'Every human is an animal, no human is a horse' yields 'Some human is not a horse'). This is because negating a specific from a general is possible. Finally, applying these conditions: the requirement that the minor be affirmative eliminates 8 of the 16 possible premise pairs (those with a negative minor), and the requirement that at least one premise be universal eliminates another 2, leaving 6 productive moods for the third figure.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
Conditions for Productivity in the Third Figure
Author explains the two necessary conditions for third-figure syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two conditions (universal premise and affirmative minor) to determine whether a given pair of third-figure premises yields a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
Third figure needs a universal premise and an affirmative minor to yield a particular conclusion.

The third figure of categorical syllogism only produces particular conclusions, but not every pair of premises works. The author identifies two essential conditions: at least one premise must be universal, and the minor premise must be affirmative. Without these, the terms may not align, as illustrated with examples from animal species. Understanding these conditions helps you test any third-figure syllogism for validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Confirm the syllogism is in the third figure: the middle term is the subject in both premises.
The middle term is the term common to both premises, e.g., 'animal' in the examples.
2
Check universality condition
Verify that at least one premise is universal. If both are particular, no conclusion follows because the terms might not intersect.
The author's example: 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is horse' do not yield 'Some human is horse'.
3
Check affirmative minor condition
Verify the minor premise (the one with the minor term, subject of conclusion) is affirmative. If it is negative, the syllogism fails.
Example: 'No human is a horse' (minor negative) and 'Every human is an animal' (major universal) do not yield 'Some horse is not an animal'.
4
Test with a valid case
Apply the conditions to a valid mood: e.g., both universals, minor affirmative, major affirmative. Expect a particular affirmative conclusion.

It is necessarily that locus, and their states beyond that are not known, so judgment is made accordingly. For this reason it only produces a particular, whether affirmative or negative. The moods obtained with respect to quantified propositions and others are what result from multiplying eight by eight. With respect to quantified propositions alone, they are what result from multiplying four by four, as in the first and second figures, but some of them are dropped because the condition for the productivity of this figure is that one of its two premises is universal, as in the whole. For if they are not universal at all, it is possible that the part containing the minor differs from that containing the major; then there is no meeting between the minor and the major, as when a genus is predicated of some of its parts as a species and of other parts as another species by affirmation, so one says 'Some animal is human', and 'Some animal is horse', then 'Some human is horse' does not follow; and the truth here is negation. Similarly, if it is predicated of some parts as a species and of other parts as the differentia of that species by negation, as one says 'Some animal is human', and 'Some animal is not rational', then our statement 'Some human is not rational' does not follow; and the truth here is affirmation. And just as the universality of one of its two premises is a condition, so also the affirmativity of the minor is a condition, as in the first figure; for if it is not affirmative, it is possible that the major is genus of the minor and the middle, as one says 'No human is a horse', and 'Every human is an animal', then our statement 'Some horse is not an animal' does not follow. But if the negative is the major, then it does follow, as one says 'Every human is an animal', and 'No human is a horse', then 'Some human is not a horse'; and it has passed before that the negation of the specific from some of the general is possible, unlike the negation of the general from some of the specific. If you know this, we say: the condition of affirmativity of the minor drops eight from the sixteen moods, and the condition of universality of one of its two premises drops two more; so the productive among them remain six. [1] The first mood: from two universal affirmatives produces a particular affirmative, as our statement 'Every J is B', and 'Every J is A', so 'Some B is A'. Its demonstration is either by conversion of the minor so that it turns into the third mood of the first figure, so one says 'Some B is J', and 'Every J is A', so 'Some B is A'; or by reductio ad absurdum, as one says: if our statement 'Some B is A' were false, its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'No B is A'. Then we say: 'Every J is B', and 'No B is A'; and it had been 'Every J is A' — this is a contradiction. Or by another method: as one says: if its contradictory, which is 'No B is A', is true, then necessarily one of the two premises is not true. That is because the B from which A is negated either has J negated from it universally, in which case the minor cannot be true at all, or it does not have J negated but rather some B must be J, in which case the major cannot be true. For some B cannot be J unless some J is B, and it had been that 'No J is A' in this form. Or one says: the meeting between the minor and the major is among the necessary consequences in this figure; and that is among the things by which the attribution of one to the other is realized, as known previously in categorical propositions, so this is attributed to that, and that is what is sought. [2] The second mood: from two universal premises with the major negative produces a particular negative, as our statement 'Every J is B', and 'No J is A', so 'Not every B is A'. Its demonstration is either by conversion of the minor to turn into the fourth mood of the first figure, or by reductio ad absurdum and other methods as passed. [3] The third mood: from two affirmatives with the minor particular produces a particular affirmative, as our statement 'Some J is B', and 'Every J is A', so 'Some B is A', by the methods passed. [4] The fourth mood: from two affirmatives with the major particular produces a particular affirmative, as our statement 'Every...'
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage discusses the productive moods of the third figure of the categorical syllogism, known in Arabic as الشكل الثالث. The author begins by reiterating a key characteristic: the third figure only yields a particular conclusion, whether affirmative or negative. He then explains the total number of possible mood combinations when considering quantified propositions (محصورات) and non-quantified ones: multiplying the eight types of premises yields 64 combinations, but for quantified alone, it is 4×4=16 (as in the first and second figures). However, not all are productive. The first condition for productivity in the third figure is that at least one premise must be universal. If both are particular, the terms might not meet, as illustrated with the example of 'animal' as a genus: if we say 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is horse', we cannot conclude 'Some human is horse' because the intersection might be different. Similarly, if one premise is negative, the conclusion might not follow. The author then introduces a second condition: the minor premise must be affirmative. This is demonstrated with the example: 'No human is a horse' and 'Every human is an animal' does not yield 'Some horse is not an animal', because the major 'animal' is genus of both. However, if the negative is the major, it works: 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is a horse' does give 'Some human is not a horse'. After establishing these conditions, the author states that the requirement of an affirmative minor eliminates 8 of the 16 possible quantified moods, and the requirement of a universal premise eliminates another 2, leaving 6 productive moods. He then enumerates the first four: (1) two universal affirmatives → particular affirmative; (2) two universals with major negative → particular negative; (3) minor particular, major universal, both affirmative → particular affirmative; (4) minor universal, major particular, both affirmative → particular affirmative. For each, he provides demonstration methods: conversion (عكس), reductio ad absurdum (خلف), or other reasoning. The text cuts off during the fourth mood.

The Six Productive Moods and Their Demonstrations
Author enumerates and demonstrates the six productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six productive moods of the third figure and demonstrate the validity of the first mood using conversion, reductio, or other methods.

Core Idea
Six mood combinations satisfy the conditions; each can be proved by conversion, reductio, or direct reasoning.

After establishing the conditions, the author lists the six moods that survive, though only four are fully given in the text due to a cut-off. For each mood, he provides a concrete example using variables and demonstrates validity through conversion (caks), reductio ad absurdum (khalaf), or another logical method. The first mood, two universal affirmatives, is demonstrated in detail, showing how the conclusion necessarily follows.

Guided Path
1
Recall the conditions
Remember that in the third figure, one premise must be universal and the minor affirmative. This reduces 16 possible moods to 6.
2
Identify the first mood
The first mood has two universal affirmatives (AAA in third figure). It produces a particular affirmative conclusion.
Example: Every J is B, Every J is A, therefore Some B is A.
3
Understand demonstration via conversion
Convert the minor premise 'Every J is B' to 'Some B is J' (valid by conversion per accidens). Then use the third mood of the first figure (Darii) to conclude 'Some B is A'.
This shows the mood reduces to a known valid mood.
4
Understand demonstration via reductio (khalaf)
Assume the conclusion false (No B is A). Together with 'Every J is B' apply second-figure mood Cesare to get 'No J is A', which contradicts the major premise 'Every J is A'. Hence the conclusion must be true.
5
Apply to the other moods
The second mood (universal affirmatives with major negative) produces a particular negative, demonstrable by conversion to the fourth mood of the first figure (Fresison). The third and fourth moods involve particular premises and are demonstrated similarly.
The text cuts off after the fourth mood, but the pattern continues.

The moods obtained with respect to quantified propositions and others are what result from multiplying eight by eight. With respect to quantified propositions alone, they are what result from multiplying four by four, as in the first and second figures, but some of them are dropped because the condition for the productivity of this figure is that one of its two premises is universal, as in the whole. For if they are not universal at all, it is possible that the part containing the minor differs from that containing the major; then there is no meeting between the minor and the major, as when a genus is predicated of some of its parts as a species and of other parts as another species by affirmation, so one says 'Some animal is human', and 'Some animal is horse', then 'Some human is horse' does not follow; and the truth here is negation. Similarly, if it is predicated of some parts as a species and of other parts as the differentia of that species by negation, as one says 'Some animal is human', and 'Some animal is not rational', then our statement 'Some human is not rational' does not follow; and the truth here is affirmation. And just as the universality of one of its two premises is a condition, so also the affirmativity of the minor is a condition, as in the first figure; for if it is not affirmative, it is possible that the major is genus of the minor and the middle, as one says 'No human is a horse', and 'Every human is an animal', then our statement 'Some horse is not an animal' does not follow. But if the negative is the major, then it does follow, as one says 'Every human is an animal', and 'No human is a horse', then 'Some human is not a horse'; and it has passed before that the negation of the specific from some of the general is possible, unlike the negation of the general from some of the specific. If you know this, we say: the condition of affirmativity of the minor drops eight from the sixteen moods, and the condition of universality of one of its two premises drops two more; so the productive among them remain six. [1] The first mood: from two universal affirmatives produces a particular affirmative, as our statement 'Every J is B', and 'Every J is A', so 'Some B is A'. Its demonstration is either by conversion of the minor so that it turns into the third mood of the first figure, so one says 'Some B is J', and 'Every J is A', so 'Some B is A'; or by reductio ad absurdum, as one says: if our statement 'Some B is A' were false, its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'No B is A'. Then we say: 'Every J is B', and 'No B is A'; and it had been 'Every J is A' — this is a contradiction. Or by another method: as one says: if its contradictory, which is 'No B is A', is true, then necessarily one of the two premises is not true. That is because the B from which A is negated either has J negated from it universally, in which case the minor cannot be true at all, or it does not have J negated but rather some B must be J, in which case the major cannot be true. For some B cannot be J unless some J is B, and it had been that 'No J is A' in this form. Or one says: the meeting between the minor and the major is among the necessary consequences in this figure; and that is among the things by which the attribution of one to the other is realized, as known previously in categorical propositions, so this is attributed to that, and that is what is sought. [2] The second mood: from two universal premises with the major negative produces a particular negative, as our statement 'Every J is B', and 'No J is A', so 'Not every B is A'. Its demonstration is either by conversion of the minor to turn into the fourth mood of the first figure, or by reductio ad absurdum and other methods as passed. [3] The third mood: from two affirmatives with the minor particular produces a particular affirmative, as our statement 'Some J is B', and 'Every J is A', so 'Some B is A', by the methods passed. [4] The fourth mood: from two affirmatives with the major particular produces a particular affirmative, as our statement 'Every...'

Commentary
This passage discusses the productive moods of the third figure of the categorical syllogism, known in Arabic as الشكل الثالث. The author begins by reiterating a key characteristic: the third figure only yields a particular conclusion, whether affirmative or negative. He then explains the total number of possible mood combinations when considering quantified propositions (محصورات) and non-quantified ones: multiplying the eight types of premises yields 64 combinations, but for quantified alone, it is 4×4=16 (as in the first and second figures). However, not all are productive. The first condition for productivity in the third figure is that at least one premise must be universal. If both are particular, the terms might not meet, as illustrated with the example of 'animal' as a genus: if we say 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is horse', we cannot conclude 'Some human is horse' because the intersection might be different. Similarly, if one premise is negative, the conclusion might not follow. The author then introduces a second condition: the minor premise must be affirmative. This is demonstrated with the example: 'No human is a horse' and 'Every human is an animal' does not yield 'Some horse is not an animal', because the major 'animal' is genus of both. However, if the negative is the major, it works: 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is a horse' does give 'Some human is not a horse'. After establishing these conditions, the author states that the requirement of an affirmative minor eliminates 8 of the 16 possible quantified moods, and the requirement of a universal premise eliminates another 2, leaving 6 productive moods. He then enumerates the first four: (1) two universal affirmatives → particular affirmative; (2) two universals with major negative → particular negative; (3) minor particular, major universal, both affirmative → particular affirmative; (4) minor universal, major particular, both affirmative → particular affirmative. For each, he provides demonstration methods: conversion (عكس), reductio ad absurdum (خلف), or other reasoning. The text cuts off during the fourth mood.
