First Mood of Second Figure: Proving a Particular Affirmative Conclusion
Author presents first mood, its conclusion, and three proof methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the validity of the first mood of the second figure using conversion, reductio, or logical analysis, and apply it to Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Two universal affirmatives yield a particular affirmative – the second figure never gives a universal conclusion.

The second figure of categorical syllogisms has its own moods. The first mood combines two universal affirmative premises but only yields a particular affirmative conclusion. Why? The author demonstrates through three different proof methods, each revealing an aspect of the logical structure. Let's work through them step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood's premises and conclusion
Find the two premises and the conclusion in the Arabic. The premises are 'Every J is B' and 'Every J is A'. The conclusion is 'Some B is A'. Note that both premises are universal affirmative (A), and the conclusion is particular affirmative (I).
The mood is A A I in the second figure.
2
Understand the conversion method
Convert the minor premise 'Every J is B' to 'Some B is J'. Then combine with the major premise 'Every J is A' to form a first figure syllogism (Disamis): Some B is J, Every J is A, so Some B is A. This shows the conclusion follows via first figure validity.
Conversion preserves truth only for particular affirmative.
3
Follow the reductio proof
Assume the conclusion is false: 'No B is A'. Combine this with the major premise 'Every J is B' to derive that no J is A (by second figure). But this contradicts the given 'Every J is A'. Hence the assumption is false, so 'Some B is A' must be true.
Reductio uses the principle of non-contradiction.
4
Analyze the third proof method
Assume the contradictory 'No B is A' is true. Then consider J relative to B: either J is universally negated from B (making 'Every J is B' false) or some B is J (making 'Every J is A' false because 'No J is A' would then contradict). Thus the contradictory assumption leads to falsity of a premise.
This method directly shows the conflict without conversion.
5
Recognize the necessity of a particular conclusion
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises, so no term is distributed in the conclusion beyond what is distributed in the premises. Since both premises are affirmative, the conclusion cannot be universal negative or universal affirmative; only particular affirmative is possible.
This follows from the rules of distribution.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The first mood from two universal affirmatives yields a particular affirmative, as when we say: Every J is B, and every J is A, so some B is A. Its proof is either by converting the minor premise so that it returns to the third mood of the first figure: some B is J, and every J is A, so some B is A. Or by reductio: if our statement 'some B is A' were false, its contradictory would be true, namely 'no B is A'. We then say: every J is B, and no B is A, but it was [given that] every J is A; this is a contradiction. Or by a different method: if its contradictory, i.e., 'no B is A', were true, it would entail that one of the two premises is not true. That is because the B from which A is negated: either J is universally negated from it, and then the minor premise cannot be true at all; or it is not negated, but then some B must be J, and then the major premise cannot be true. For some B cannot be J unless some J is B, and it was [assumed] that no J is A in this case. Or it is said: the conjunction between the minor and major from the necessary implications in this figure; and that is part of what is established of the attribution of one to the other, as was previously known in categorical propositions, so this is attributed to that, thus it is what is sought.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage explains the first mood of the second figure of categorical syllogisms, where both premises are universal affirmatives and the conclusion is a particular affirmative. The author gives three methods of proof. First, by converting the minor premise (from 'every J is B' to 'some B is J') and reconfiguring it as a syllogism in the first figure's third mood (which is valid). Second, by reductio ad absurdum: assume the negation of the conclusion ('no B is A') and combine it with the major premise to derive a contradiction with the given minor premise. Third, a more intricate logical analysis: if the contradictory 'no B is A' is true, then consider the middle term J relative to B. Either J is universally negated from B, which falsifies the minor premise (every J is B), or some B is J, which combined with 'no J is A' would make the major premise (every J is A) false. Thus the contradictory assumption leads to the falsehood of a premise. The author also mentions a more conceptual argument about the necessary connection between terms. This mood is valid for a particular affirmative conclusion.

Second Mood of the Second Figure: Universal Major Negative
Author presents second mood with both universal and major negative, concluding particular negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the second mood of the second figure and verify that its conclusion follows.

Core Idea
Universal premises with a negative major yield a particular negative conclusion.

In the first mood of the second figure, both premises were universal affirmative, giving a universal negative conclusion. Now we consider the second mood, where the major premise is negative. The same figure and universal quantity apply, but the conclusion becomes particular negative. Understanding this mood strengthens your ability to evaluate syllogisms in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the two universal statements. The minor premise ('Every J is B') is universal affirmative. The major premise ('No J is A') is universal negative. Notice the major is the one containing the predicate of the conclusion.
2
Determine the figure
The middle term (J) is the subject of both premises, which is the defining feature of the second figure. So this is a second-figure syllogism.
3
Find the conclusion
The conclusion is 'Not every B is A' (i.e., some B is not A). It is a particular negative (O-proposition). Verify that it follows from the premises: if every J is B and no J is A, then there is at least one B (since there is at least one J) that is not A.
4
Understand the proof via conversion
Convert the minor premise 'Every J is B' by accident (al-aks al-mustawi) to 'Some B is J'. This gives a first-figure syllogism: 'Some B is J' and 'No J is A', which is the fourth mood of the first figure (Celantes? Actually in Aristotelian terms, it's Ferio? Wait: In the first figure fourth mood, the major is universal negative and the minor is particular affirmative, yielding a particular negative. So this matches.)
This shows how second-figure moods can be reduced to first-figure for verification.
5
Alternative proof: reductio ad absurdum
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion: 'Every B is A'. Then with 'Every J is B' we get 'Every J is A' (Barbara in first figure). But that contradicts the major 'No J is A'. Hence the original conclusion must hold.
Reductio is a universal method for all valid moods.

Third Mood of Second Figure: Particular Affirmative Conclusion
Author presents third mood with both affirmative and minor particular, concluding particular affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third mood of the second figure (both premises affirmative, minor particular) and verify its conclusion using conversion or reductio.

Core Idea
Two affirmatives in the second figure with a particular minor yield a particular conclusion.

This lesson introduces the third mood of the second figure, where both premises are affirmative but the minor premise is particular. The author presents this mood as valid and demonstrates that the conclusion is a particular affirmative. We will analyse the structure of the syllogism and learn how the conclusion is derived using methods such as conversion or reductio, as established in earlier lessons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Premises
Locate the two premises: the major premise is 'Every J is A' (universal affirmative) and the minor premise is 'Some J is B' (particular affirmative). Note that both are affirmative.
The major premise contains the major term (A) and the middle term (J); the minor premise contains the minor term (B) and the middle term (J).
2
Determine the Figure
Examine the position of the middle term J. It appears as the subject in both premises, which is characteristic of the second figure as classified in this text.
In standard second figure, the middle term is predicate in both premises; however, this text treats the middle term as subject in both for this mood.
3
Observe the Mood Characteristics
Note that both premises are affirmative (universal and particular), and the minor premise is particular. This combination is unique to the third mood of the second figure.
The mood is identified by the quantity and quality of the premises.
4
Derive the Conclusion
From these premises, the conclusion is 'Some B is A' (particular affirmative). The author asserts that this follows by the methods previously explained, such as conversion of the minor premise or reductio ad impossibile.
The conclusion is particular affirmative, matching the quantity of the minor premise.
5
Validate Using Methods
To confirm validity, apply the methods mentioned: convert the minor premise 'Some J is B' to 'Some B is J', then combine with the major premise 'Every J is A' in the first figure to obtain 'Some B is A'. This demonstrates the reasoning.
The conversion and first-figure application are taught in earlier lessons.

C) No B is A
D) Some B is not A

Key Takeaway
The third mood of the second figure takes two affirmative premises with a particular minor and yields a particular affirmative conclusion, validated by conversion or reductio.

Fifth Mood of Second Figure: Particular Negative Conclusion
Author presents fifth mood with minor affirmative particular and major universal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth mood of the second figure and verify that a particular affirmative minor with a universal negative major yields a particular negative conclusion.

Core Idea
Particular affirmative + universal negative in second figure forces a particular negative conclusion.

In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. The fifth mood combines a particular affirmative minor (Some J is B) with a universal negative major (No J is A). This arrangement yields a particular negative conclusion (Not every B is A), as proven by standard reduction methods like conversion or reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the minor premise (particular affirmative: 'Some J is B') and the major premise (universal negative: 'No J is A'). Note the middle term 'J' appears as predicate in both.
The minor is the premise containing the subject of the conclusion; the major contains the predicate.
2
Determine figure and mood
Since the middle term (J) is the predicate in both premises, this is the second figure. The minor is particular affirmative (I), the major is universal negative (E), so the mood is IEO (but note the conclusion is O).
In the second figure, the conclusion is always negative.
3
Check the conclusion
The conclusion is particular negative (O): 'Not every B is A' (i.e., Some B is not A). Verify that this follows from the premises using the second figure's rule: the conclusion must be negative, and the minor being particular forces the conclusion to be particular.
The conclusion 'not every B is A' is equivalent to 'Some B is not A'.
4
Apply reduction (if needed)
To prove validity, reduce to first figure: Convert the minor (Some J is B) to (Some B is J) by accidental conversion. Then the major (No J is A) can be converted to (No A is J). Then use first figure (Ferio) with premises (No A is J) and (Some B is J) to get (Some B is not A).
The 'methods already mentioned' include conversion and reductio.

Source Text

The fifth mood, from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yields a particular negative: Some J is B, and no J is A, so not every B is A, by the methods already mentioned.

The Sixth Mood and Proof by Assumption
Author presents sixth mood requiring assumption proof due to inability to convert.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sixth mood of the second figure and explain why it requires proof by assumption or reductio.

Core Idea
Not every syllogism is reducible by conversion – some need assumption or reductio.

You have seen that most second-figure moods can be proved by conversion to the first figure. However, the sixth mood (universal affirmative minor, particular negative major) resists that strategy. In this lesson, we examine why conversion fails and how the assumption method (iftirad) provides an alternative proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood's premises
The minor premise is 'Every J is B' (universal affirmative). The major premise is 'Not every J is A' (particular negative). The conclusion is 'Not every B is A' (particular negative). Write them down.
J is the middle term.
2
Test conversion feasibility
Convert the minor: 'Every J is B' becomes 'Some B is J' (particular affirmative). The major 'Not every J is A' does not convert at all (particular negative does not convert). After conversion, we have two particular premises, which yield no conclusion. Thus conversion fails.
Two particular premises are invalid in any figure.
3
Introduce the assumption method
The author introduces a new term D and assumes: 'A is not D' (or possibly 'No A is D'), 'Every J is D', and 'Every J is B'. From these, we deduce 'Every D is B' (though the text is unclear; likely from 'Every J is B' and 'Every J is D' we get 'Some D is B' then universal? The key is that the assumption allows a new syllogism).
The exact logic is textually problematic, but the method is meant to circumvent the conversion obstacle.
4
Derive the conclusion
From the assumed premises, we conclude 'Not every B is A', matching the original conclusion. This shows the mood is valid even though conversion failed.
The assumption method effectively creates a different syllogism that supports the same conclusion.
5
Summarize the necessity
The sixth mood cannot be proved by mere conversion; it requires the more powerful techniques of reductio or assumption. Understanding this deepens your grasp of syllogistic validity.
Reductio works similarly by assuming the opposite conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The sixth mood, from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, yields a particular negative: Every J is B, and not every J is A, so not every B is A. This is among those that cannot be proved by conversion, because the major does not convert, and the converse of the minor is particular, and there is no syllogism from two particulars. So it is proved either by reductio as before or by assumption. That is: we assume the judgment that A is not D, and every J is D, and every J is B; then we say every D is B, and no J is A, so not every B is A. Or by another method as before.

Commentary
This passage explains the sixth mood of the second figure, which is the most complex. The minor is universal affirmative, the major is particular negative. The conclusion is particular negative. The author notes that this mood cannot be proved by conversion because: the major (not every J is A) cannot be converted (particular negative does not convert), the conversion of the minor (every J is B) gives a particular affirmative (some B is J), and two particular premises do not yield a syllogism. Therefore, only reductio or assumption works. The assumption method involves introducing a new term D such that 'A is not D' (meaning no A is D?), and every J is D, and every J is B. Then from 'every J is B' and 'every J is D' we get that every D is B? Actually the text says 'then we say every D is B', which is questionable; perhaps it means from the premises we deduce some D is B, but then combine with 'no J is A'? The argument is not fully clear in the OCR. Nonetheless, the main point is that this mood requires special proof.

Counting Valid Variations in Second Figure Moods
Author enumerates variation counts for each mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of valid aspects for each mood of the second figure under the criteria of generality/specificity and direct/privative terms.

Core Idea
Each second-figure mood has a fixed set of valid term-relation and term-quality variants.

After mastering the six moods of the second figure, we now see that each mood can appear in different valid forms depending on how the terms relate in generality/specificity, and whether terms are direct or privative. The author gives exact counts for each mood. Understanding these numbers helps you instantly assess whether a given second-figure syllogism is potentially valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two criteria
The passage distinguishes two ways a mood can vary: (1) by generality/specificity (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ) among the minor, middle, and major terms; (2) by direct/privative (ʿudūl wa taḥṣīl) terms in the premises.
Generality/specificity refers to inclusion relations (e.g., all vs. some). Direct/privative refers to whether terms are positive (direct) or negated (privative).
2
Learn the numbers for generality/specificity
The author states: 1st mood has 4 aspects, 2nd has 2, 3rd has 8, 4th has 8, 5th has 4, 6th has 2. Memorize these counts.
These numbers come from the possible combinations of universal/particular and affirmative/negative among the premises for each mood.
3
Understand the direct/privative variation
Each premise can be direct (positive), privative in subject, privative in predicate, or both. That gives 4 possibilities per premise, so 4 × 4 = 16 combinations per mood. But only 8 are valid because in the second figure both premises must be affirmative for a valid syllogism (otherwise the middle term is not properly distributed).
The author says 'as previously stated' (مرَّ من قبل) – this rule is from earlier in the text.
4
Test on a specific mood
Take the first mood (All B are A, no C is B). Under generality/specificity, it has 4 valid forms. List them: (1) All B are A, no C is B; (2) All B are A, some C is not B; (3) Some B is A, no C is B; (4) Some B is A, some C is not B. Under direct/privative, out of 16 possible only 8 are valid – those where both premises are affirmative (direct or privative subject/predicate in a way that keeps the middle term distributed).
You can verify with your own examples using Islamic legal terms.

This is with respect to generality and specificity and other things. As for with respect to privative and direct terms, the aspects occurring in each mood are sixteen: eight of them are invalid, and eight remain valid as previously stated, because each of the minor and major premises may be direct, or privative in subject or predicate or both.

The number of valid combinations for each mood is given: first mood has 4, second has 2, third has 8, fourth has 8, fifth has 4, sixth has 2. The total combinations are 2 premises * 4 possibilities each = 16, but half are invalid as previously explained in the text (مرَّ من قبل).

Proving the Sixth Mood of the Third Figure: Reductio and Supposition
Author explains why conversion fails and demonstrates two alternative proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proof by reductio ad absurdum or by supposition for a categorical syllogism that cannot be proven by conversion.

Core Idea
When conversion fails, reductio or supposition saves the syllogism.

The sixth mood of the third figure cannot be proven by simple conversion because its premises do not convert properly. The logicians therefore developed two alternative methods: reductio ad absurdum (khalf) and supposition (iftirad). In this lesson, we see how these methods work using the example from the text, and learn to apply them to similar cases in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion of the sixth mood
From the text: the minor premise is 'Every C is B' (universal affirmative), the major is 'Not every C is A' (particular negative). The conclusion is 'Not every B is A' (particular negative). Note the terms: ج (C), ب (B), أ (A).
This mood is valid in the third figure where the middle term is the subject of both premises.
2
Check why conversion fails
The major premise 'Not every C is A' is a particular negative (O proposition). Simple conversion of O is invalid (it does not preserve truth). The minor premise 'Every C is B' converts to 'Some B is C' (particular affirmative, I), but then we would have two particular premises (I and O), and no valid syllogism can have two particular premises. Thus conversion alone cannot prove the mood.
This is why the author says 'the major does not convert, and the conversion of the minor is particular; and no syllogism from two particulars'.
3
Understand the reductio method
Assume the opposite of the conclusion: 'Every B is A' (universal affirmative). Combine with the minor premise 'Every C is B' to get 'Every C is A' (by Barbara in first figure). But this contradicts the major premise 'Not every C is A'. Hence the assumption is false, so the conclusion 'Not every B is A' is true.
This is the method of khalf: assuming the contradictory leads to a contradiction with given premises.
4
Understand the supposition method (iftirad)
Suppose there exists a term D such that: (1) 'Not every D is A' (i.e., some D is not A), (2) 'Every C is D', and (3) 'Every C is B'. From (3) and (2) we get 'Every D is B' (by Barbara? Actually careful: from 'Every C is B' and 'Every C is D' we cannot directly get 'Every D is B'. The text says 'every D is B' but this requires a different combination: actually the author's example is: 'One supposes a judgment that is not A is D, and every C is D, and every C is B, then we say: Every D is B, and nothing of C is A, so not every B is A.' This seems a bit off. Let's reconstruct: The author likely means: Suppose there is a D such that 'No D is A' (universal negative) and 'Every C is D', then from 'Every C is B' and 'Every C is D' we get 'Some D is B' (by third figure?), but the text says 'every D is B' which is problematic. Alternatively, a standard supposition proof for the third figure sixth mood (Disamis) uses the valid syllogism: Some C is not A, Every C is B, therefore Some B is not A. But the author's version might be different. For teaching, we can simplify: The supposition method introduces a new term to form a valid syllogism that yields the conclusion. The exact steps are intricate; the main point is that the method exists.
The supposition method is less intuitive but demonstrates the creativity of logicians in proving validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامس من موجبة جزئية كبراها سالبة كلية ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا بعض «ج» «ب»، ولا شيء من (ج) أ) فليس كل ب أ لما مرَّ من الطرق. [] والسادس من موجبة كلية كبراها سالبة جزئية ينتج سالبة جزئية كقولنا كل «ج» «ب»، وليس كل (ج) » « أ » فليس كل «ب» «أ»؛ وهذا من جملة ما لا يمكن بيانه بالعكس؛ إذ الكبرى لا تنعكس، وعكس صغراها جزئية؛ ولا قياس عن جزئيتين، فتبين إذن إما بالخلف كما مر وإما بالافتراض. وذلك أن يفرض الحكم الذي ليس «أ» «د» وكل «ج» «د»، وكل «ج» «ب» ثم نقول كل «د» ب، ولا شيء من (ج) أ) فليس كل «ب» «أ». وإما بالغير كما مر.
The fifth [mood] from a particular affirmative [premise] whose major is a universal negative yields a particular negative conclusion, as in our saying: Some C is B, and nothing of C is A, so not every B is A, according to the methods mentioned. The sixth [mood] from a universal affirmative [premise] whose major is a particular negative yields a particular negative conclusion, as in our saying: Every C is B, and not every C is A, so not every B is A; this is among that which cannot be explained by conversion, for the major does not convert, and the conversion of the minor is particular; and no syllogism from two particulars, so it is shown either by reductio as mentioned or by supposition. That is, one supposes a judgment that is not A is D, and every C is D, and every C is B, then we say: Every D is B, and nothing of C is A, so not every B is A. Or by another [method] as mentioned.

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the fifth and sixth moods of the third figure. The author first presents the fifth mood: a particular affirmative minor premise and a universal negative major premise yield a particular negative conclusion. An example is given with terms C, B, A. He then notes that this conclusion can be derived by previously mentioned methods. Next, the sixth mood: a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major also yield a particular negative conclusion. The author explains that this mood cannot be demonstrated by simple conversion because the major is particular negative and does not convert, and converting the minor yields a particular, and no syllogism can have two particular premises. Therefore, it is proven either by reductio ad absurdum (khalf) or by supposition (iftirad). For reductio, one assumes the contradictory of the conclusion and shows it leads to a falsehood. For supposition, one posits a middle term D and constructs a valid syllogism. This careful demonstration shows the rigor of the logicians in establishing each valid mood.

Key Takeaway
The sixth mood of the third figure (Bocardo) cannot be proven by conversion; it requires reductio ad absurdum or supposition, demonstrating the need for advanced proof techniques in categorical logic.

Enumerating Valid Faces of Syllogistic Moods
Author enumerates valid faces for each mood under three criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of valid faces (wujuh) for any syllogistic mood based on the three criteria of generality/specificity, negation/affirmation, and binary/ternary term structure.

Core Idea
Valid syllogistic forms multiply across independent criteria; enumeration is multiplicative, not additive.

In this lesson, we explore how classical logicians exhaustively classify every possible valid variation of syllogistic moods. The author details the number of 'faces' (wujuh) for each mood based on three distinctions: generality/specificity relations between terms, whether propositions are affirmative or privative, and whether the premises have two or three distinct terms. By grasping this, you will be able to systematically compute the total valid forms for any mood—a skill essential for rigorous theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three criteria
The text discusses three ways moods vary: by generality/specificity (عموم وخصوص), by negation/affirmation (عدول وتحصل), and by binary/ternary terms (ثنائية وثلاثية). Understand each and how they are independent.
2
Extract faces for the first criterion
For each mood, note the number of faces given for generality/specificity: first mood 4, second 2, third 8, fourth 8, fifth 4, sixth 2.
These numbers are specific to the third figure moods.
3
Understand the negation/affirmation factor
Each mood has 16 possible combinations (minor and major can each be affirmative or privative in subject, predicate, or both), but only 8 are valid due to logical rules. This factor is constant across moods.
The valid 8 were established in earlier lessons.
4
Apply the binary/ternary factor
Each mood has 4 possibilities: both premises binary, both ternary, minor binary and major ternary, or vice versa. Multiply the number from the first criterion by 8 and then by 4 to get total valid faces.
This multiplication gives the total for each mood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كل واحد من هذه الضروب الستة يقع على وجوه مختلفة أيضًا بحسب اختلاف الأحوال في حدودها من العموم والخصوص وغير ذلك. أما الضرب الأول فذلك على أربعة أوجه باعتبار العمومية والخصوصية بين الأصغر والأوسط، والأوسط والأكبر، وأما الثاني فعلى وجهين، وأما الثالث فعلى ثمانية أوجه، وكذلك الرابع، وأما الخامس فعلى أربعة أوجه بخلاف السادس فإنه على وجهين، هذا بالنسبة إلى العموم والخصوص وغيرهما. فأما بالنسبة إلى العدول والتحصيل فالوجوه الواقعة في كل ضرب منها ستة عشر : ثمانية منها ساقطة، وثمانية باقية كما مرَّ من قبل؛ إذ كل واحدة من الصغرى والكبرى قد تكون محصلة، وقد تكون معدولة الموضوع أو المحمول أو منهما. وكما أن كل واحد منها متعدد بحسب العموم والخصوص والعدول والتحصيل فكذلك بحسب الغير؛ وذلك أن تكون كل واحدة من الصغرى والكبرى في كل ضرب من الضروب إما أن تكون ثنائية، أو ثلاثية، أو الصغرى ثنائية دون الكبرى، أو بالعكس؛ فيكون كل ضرب من الضروب الأربعة على أربعة أوجه. فيضرب ما يكون واقعا من الوجوه في كل ضرب بحسب العموم والخصوص في الثمانية الواقعة بحسب العدول والتحصيل، ثم المبلغ في هذه الأربعة كما مر.
Then each one of these six moods occurs in different faces also according to the variation of conditions in their terms regarding generality and specificity and other considerations. As for the first mood, that is on four faces considering the generality and specificity between the minor and middle, and the middle and major; as for the second, on two faces; as for the third, on eight faces, and likewise the fourth; as for the fifth, on four faces, unlike the sixth which is on two faces. This is with respect to generality and specificity and others. As for with respect to negation and affirmation, the faces occurring in each of them are sixteen: eight of them are invalid, and eight remain valid as mentioned before; for each of the minor and major may be affirmative, or privative in subject or predicate or both. And just as each one of them is multiple according to generality/specificity and negation/affirmation, similarly according to the other [consideration]: that each of the minor and major in every mood may be binary or ternary, or the minor binary without the major, or vice versa; so each of the four moods has four faces. Multiply the faces occurring in each mood according to generality/specificity by the eight occurring according to negation/affirmation, then the product in these four is as mentioned.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage classifies the variations of the six moods of the third figure. The author explains that each mood can have multiple 'faces' (wujuh) based on three criteria: (1) the generality or specificity between the terms, (2) whether the propositions are affirmative or privative (negated), and (3) whether the premises are binary (two terms) or ternary (three distinct terms). First, he details the number of faces for each mood based on generality/specificity: the first mood has four, the second has two, the third and fourth each have eight, the fifth has four, and the sixth has two. Second, regarding negation/affirmation, each mood has sixteen possible combinations, but only eight are valid because the rest violate logical rules. This is because the minor and major can each be either affirmative or privative in subject, predicate, or both. Third, considering whether the premises are binary or ternary, each mood (the four that are productive) actually has four possibilities: both binary, both ternary, minor binary and major ternary, or minor ternary and major binary. The author then notes that to find the total valid faces for each mood, one multiplies the faces from generality/specificity by those from negation/affirmation (eight valid) and then by the four from the binary/ternary consideration. This detailed enumeration demonstrates the thoroughness of the logicians in exhausting all possible valid forms.

Distinguishing Genuine Moods from Illusions in the Third Figure
Author refutes an apparent extra mood in the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an illusionary mood in the third figure and explain why it is not productive in itself.

Core Idea
Not every combinable premise pair yielding a conclusion is a valid mood; some need external reduction.

In the third figure of categorical syllogisms, the traditional count of six productive moods is well-established. However, some have imagined additional moods by combining premises that seem to yield a conclusion but actually require conversion and reduction to the first figure. This passage examines one such illusion: a universal negative minor with a particular affirmative major. We will learn the test for 'productive in itself' (muntijan li-dhātihi) to distinguish genuine moods from illusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purported new mood
The illusion involves a universal negative minor (e.g., No C is B) and a particular affirmative major (Some C is A). At first glance, it appears to yield the conclusion 'Some B is not A'.
The passage gives the form: 'Nothing of C is B, and not every C is A, so not every B is A.' Note that 'not every C is A' is equivalent to 'Some C is not A'.
2
Test the mood for self-productivity
A mood is productive in itself (muntijan li-dhātihi) if it yields the conclusion directly from the premises without any conversion or reduction to another figure. Here, the conclusion 'Not every B is A' does not follow directly; it requires converting the minor premise (No C is B becomes No B is C) and then using a first-figure syllogism.
The author explicitly states that this mood is among the 'illusions' (wahmiyyat) because it depends on reduction.
3
Perform the reduction step by step
Convert the minor premise 'No C is B' to 'No B is C' (simple conversion of a universal negative). Then we have: No B is C, and some C is not A (the major premise). In the first figure, we need a universal premise to combine, but we have a particular negative major. However, the author's reduction likely involves rearranging premises. Actually, the text says 'by converting the minor and reducing to the first figure as mentioned in the second figure.' The exact method is not fully detailed, but the key is that it is not immediate.
The reduction shows the conclusion is derived, but not directly from the original pair.
4
Apply the general principle to other potential illusions
The author also warns of other faces where the terms may have contradictory or more-general relationships that prevent self-productivity. For any suspected new mood, ask: Does the conclusion follow solely from the given premises as they stand, without any transformation? If not, it is an illusion.
This test preserves the integrity of the six standard moods.

ثم الوهم الذي مرَّ ذكره في الأول أن المنتج منه لا يكون منحصرا في الضروب الأربعة كما أنه يوهم في الشكل الثاني أنه لا يكون منحصرا في الأربعة فكذلك يوهم في هذا الشكل ; وذلك لأن السالبة الكلية إذا كانت صغرى الموجبة الجزئية تنتج سالبة جزئية فيقال لا شيء من (ج) «ب»، وليس (١) كل «ج» «أ» فليس كل «ب» «أ» بعكس الصغرى والرد إلى الأول كما مرَّ في الشكل الثاني وقد / مرَّ من قبل أنه من جملة الوهميات. ثم لقائل أن يقول هب أنه كذلك لكن المنتج في هذا الشكل من هذه الضروب الأربعة لا يكون منتجا لذاته فإن من الوجوه ما ينافيه نحو الوجوه المذكورة في الشكل الأول والثاني وغيرهما; وذلك من وجوه أيضًا : [١] منها أن يقال إذا كان الشيء الواحد متصفا بشيئين مختلفين يمكن أن يكون كل واحد منهما منافيًا للآخر، ويمكن أن لا يكون. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يحكم على هذا بذلك، أو بالعكس من غير أن يعلم بأن كل واحد منهما ما هو وكيف هو . [٢] ومنها أنه إذا كان متصفا بشيئين مختلفين يمكن أن يكون كل واحد منهما أعم بالنسبة إليه، ويمكن أن لا يكون. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن
Then the illusion that was mentioned earlier regarding the first [figure] that what is productive from it is not restricted to four moods, just as it is imagined in the second figure that it is not restricted to four, so likewise it is imagined in this figure. That is because if the universal negative is the minor [premise] and the particular affirmative is the major, it yields a particular negative, so it is said: Nothing of C is B, and not every C is A, so not every B is A, by converting the minor and reducing to the first figure, as was mentioned in the second figure; and it was mentioned before that this is among the illusions. Then one might say: Grant that it is so, but what is productive in this figure from these four moods is not productive in itself, for among the faces are those that contradict it, such as the faces mentioned in the first and second figures and others. That is also from some faces: [1] Among them is that it is said: If one thing is characterized by two different things, it is possible that each of them is contradictory to the other, and possible that not. And if it were so, then one cannot judge this by that, or vice versa, without knowing what each of them is and how it is. [2] Among them is that if it is characterized by two different things, it is possible that each of them is more general with respect to it, and possible that not. And if it were so, then it cannot...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage addresses an illusion (wahm) about the number of productive moods in the third figure. The author explains that some might think, as in the first and second figures, that the productive moods are not limited to the four traditionally accepted. Specifically, if one takes a universal negative as the minor premise and a particular affirmative as the major, it seems to produce a particular negative conclusion. An example is given: 'Nothing of C is B, and not every C is A, so not every B is A.' By converting the minor (universal negative becomes a universal negative of the converse) and reducing to the first figure, one can derive a conclusion. However, the author notes this was already classified as an illusion. Then a possible objection is raised: even if that mood yields a conclusion, the moods that are productive in this figure from these four moods are not productive in themselves (li-dhatihi). The objection points out that there are faces where the terms are related in ways that contradict the requirement for a valid syllogism, similar to cases in the first and second figures. Two specific problematic cases are mentioned: (1) If a single thing has two different attributes, they may be contradictory or not; without knowing the nature of each, a conclusion cannot be drawn. (2) If a thing has two different attributes, one may be more general than the other, or not; again, a conclusion cannot be guaranteed. The text breaks off, but the author is in the process of refuting the illusion and affirming that only the four moods are truly productive in the third figure.

A mood is genuinely productive only if the conclusion follows directly from the premises without conversion or reduction; illusions arise when we mistake derived conclusions for self-productive ones.

Refuting Objections to Syllogistic Productivity: A Dialectical Analysis
Author refutes five objections to syllogism productivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical objection-response pair and identify the key conditions for syllogistic productivity.

Core Idea
Productivity of a syllogism is intrinsic if premises are true and the middle term is unified.

In this passage, the author defends a logical principle against five objections. Each objection attacks the claim that a syllogism with opposite predicates can still produce a conclusion. The author's responses reveal the exact conditions under which syllogistic reasoning holds. This lesson will guide you through the dialectical moves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the underlying principle
What is the author defending? Look for the claim that a syllogism can be productive even when its predicates are opposites.
The principle is that productivity depends on the unity of the middle term and truth of premises, not on the predicates being compatible.
2
Extract each objection's logical core
Read objections [3], [4], and [5]. For each, distill the logical worry: dependence on external knowledge, conditionality of meeting, and counterexample.
Objection [5] is a concrete example using 'sleeping' and 'not sleeping'.
3
Analyze the author's response strategy
For each objection, see how the author responds: [3] knowledge of no contradiction is implicit, [4] meeting condition is internal, [5] middle term is not unified in the counterexample.
The author consistently argues that the objections misunderstand the conditions.
4
Evaluate the strength of each response
Assess whether the author's rebuttal is logically sound. For [5], check if the middle term 'man' is truly unified in both premises.
If the middle term is the same, one premise must be false; if different, the syllogism is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يحكم على البعض من هذا وهو «ب» بأنه ذلك وهو أبل إذا أمكن فذلك في البعض الذي هو «ج» لا مطلقا ; إذ المطلق أعم من البعض الذي هو «ج» ومن البعض الذي هو غير «ج». [۳] ومنها أن يقال هب أنه يمكن أن يحكم عليه وإنه منتج أيضًا لكنه لا يكون منتجًا إلا وأن لا يكون بين ذينك الشيئين منافاة؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون إنتاجه مفتقرا إلى العلم بعدم المنافاة وذلك من جملة ما ينافي كونه منتجا لذاته . [٤] ومنها أن يقال إنه إذا كان منتجا فلا يخلو من أن يكون الالتقاء بين ذينك الشيئين في محل واحد في زمان واحد شرطاً لكونه منتجا؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن لا يكون منتجا لذاته، أو لا يكون شرطا؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون منتجا بدون ذلك: وذلك محال. [٥] ومنها أن يقال إنه إذا كان منتجا كان منتجًا في جميع الصور وليس كذلك فإنا إذا قلنا كل إنسان نائم، وكل إنسان لا نائم فلا يلزم منه قولنا بعض النائم لا نائم. وعلى هذا بالنسبة إلى جميع الأعراض المفارقة.
لكنا نقول في الجواب [١] عن الأول إنهما إذا التقيا في موضوع واحد لا يمكن أن يكون أحدهما منافيا للآخر لاستحالة الاجتماع بين المنافيين في موضوع واحد ; والكلام في أنهما يلتقيان في موضوع واحد. ولو كانا ملتقيين في موضوع لكان من الممكن أن يحكم بأحدهما على البعض من الآخر بل من الواجب لكونه ضروريًا. [٢] وعن الثاني إن إمكان أعمية كل واحد منهما بالنسبة إليه لا يكون مانعا عن الحكم بأحدهما على الآخر حكمًا جزئيا بل يكون مانعا عن الحكم بأحدهما على الآخر حكما كليا. وأما أنه لا يمكن أن يحكم على بعضه مطلقا فذلك في حيز المنع. فإنه إذا حكم على البعض من الشيء مقيدا; وذلك المقيد هو بعضه فيحكم على البعض منه وهذا هو المطلوب ليس إلا. [٣] وعن الثالث إن العلم بعدم المنافاة بينهما من جملة ما يتضمنه العلم بالمقدمتين فيما نحن فيه لما مر في الجواب عن الأول. [١٦١ و] ولو كان كذلك فلا يكون إنتاجه مفتقرًا إلى الغير. [٤] وعن الرابع أن الالتقاء بينهما في الجملة شرط; والشرط متحقق كذلك; إذ الكلام فيه. ولا يلزم منه أنه لا يكون منتجا لذاته فإنه إذا لزم كان ذلك الشرط من جملة ما يمكن أن يتصور هذا دون ذلك: وليس كذلك. [٥] وعن الخامس إن الأوسط فيما ذكرتم من المثال لا يخلو من أن يكون متحدا; وحينئذ يلزم كذب إحدى المقدمتين لا محالة، أو لا يكون; وحينئذ يكون ذلك بمعزل عما نحن فيه.
That it is judged of some of this, which is 'B', that it is that, which is 'A', if possible, then that is in some of what is 'C', not absolutely; for the absolute is more general than some of what is 'C' and some of what is not 'C'. [3] Among them is that it is said: suppose that it is possible to judge it and that it is also productive, but it is not productive unless there is no contradiction between those two things; then its production would be dependent on knowledge of the absence of contradiction, and that is among the things that contradict its being productive by itself. [4] Among them is that it is said: if it is productive, then it is unavoidable that the meeting between those two things in one place at one time is a condition for its being productive; then it follows that it is not productive by itself, or it is not a condition; then it follows that it is productive without that, and that is impossible. [5] Among them is that it is said: if it is productive, it is productive in all forms, but it is not so; for if we say 'Every man is sleeping, and every man is not sleeping', it does not follow from it our saying 'Some sleeping is not sleeping'. And likewise for all accidental attributes. But we say in answer: [1] Regarding the first, if they meet in one subject, it is impossible for one of them to be contradictory to the other, because of the impossibility of two contradictories coexisting in one subject; and the discussion is about their meeting in one subject. And if they were meeting in a subject, it would be possible to judge one of them of part of the other, indeed necessary, because it is self-evident. [2] Regarding the second, the possibility of each of them being more general relative to it does not prevent judging one of them of the other as a particular judgment, but it prevents judging one of them of the other as a universal judgment. As for the claim that it is absolutely impossible to judge some of it, that is in the realm of denial. For if one judges something of some part of a thing conditionally, and that conditional is part of it, then one judges it of that part, and that is exactly what is desired. [3] Regarding the third, knowledge of the absence of contradiction between them is part of what is included in the knowledge of the two premises in this context, as mentioned in the answer to the first. And if that is the case, then its production is not dependent on something else. [4] Regarding the fourth, the meeting between them in general is a condition; and the condition is realized accordingly, for the discussion is about it. And it does not follow that it is not productive by itself, for if it did follow, that condition would be among the things that can be conceived of this without that, but it is not so. [5] Regarding the fifth, the middle term in the example you mentioned is either unified, in which case one of the two premises is necessarily false, or it is not unified, in which case that is apart from what we are discussing.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where the author refutes five objections against a previously stated logical principle about the productivity of a syllogism. The objections are numbered [3] to [5], while the answers are numbered [1] to [5], covering all five objections (the first two objections likely appeared on a previous page). The author's responses defend the claim that a syllogism can be productive even when its predicates are opposites, provided the middle term is unified.

Objection [3] argues that if a syllogism requires no contradiction between its terms, then its productivity depends on knowing that there is no contradiction, which would make it not productive by itself. The author answers that knowledge of no contradiction is already implicit in knowing the premises, so no external knowledge is needed.

Objection [4] argues that if meeting in a subject is a condition, then either the syllogism is not productive by itself or it can be productive without that condition. The author responds that the condition is indeed met in the relevant case, and it does not undermine self-productivity because the condition is internal to the syllogism.

Objection [5] gives a counterexample: from 'Every man is sleeping' and 'Every man is not sleeping', nothing follows. The author points out that the middle term here is not truly unified—either it is the same term and then one premise is false, or it is not the same and the example is irrelevant.

Throughout, the author emphasizes that the syllogism's productivity is intrinsic as long as the premises are true and the middle term is properly unified.

منتج
منافاة
التقاء
أوسط

B) Because the syllogism is productive by definition regardless of contradiction.
C) Because the objection only applies to disjunctive syllogisms.
D) Because the author assumes the conclusion is already known.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism's productivity is self-contained if its premises are true and the middle term is unified.
